Jerusalem Talmud Beitzah



Chapter 1



Halakhah 1

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> An egg was laid on a holiday. The House of Shammay say that it may be eaten<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">The preparation of food is permitted on a holiday (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 12:16" href="/Exodus.12.16"><i>Ex.</i> 12:16</a>), but this is restricted to foodstuffs which were available before the start of the holiday. The House of Shammai hold that an entire chicken is food since slaughter for food is permitted on the holiday, and since everybody agrees that an egg found inside a slaughtered chicken on a holiday is permitted food, there is no reason to consider a laid egg as something different.</i>, but the House of Hillel say that it may not be eaten<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">The House of Hillel consider the laid egg as a new entity which did not exist before the holiday and which therefore is <i>muqṣeh</i> and may not be moved.</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “An egg was laid on a holiday,” etc. What is the reason of the House of Shammai? It is prepared in its mother’s body<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">Since a chicken may be slaughtered as food on a holiday, it was available all the time and therefore its eggs should not be treated differently.<br>For most of Chapter 1 there exist one or two parallel Genizah fragments edited by L. Ginzberg in his <i>Yerushalmi Fragments</i>, pp. 154–172, noted G and <b>G</b>, 302–303 (G).</i>. What is the reason of the House of Hillel? It is like <i>muqṣeh</i> which dried without him realizing it<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">While <i>muqṣeh</i> is the technical term for anything which is not available to be moved on the holiday or Sabbath, the original meaning is “cut-up”, <i>viz</i>., a heap of cut-up figs lying on the flat roof to ferment and to be made into fig cakes. During the fermentation process the mass is inedible; therefore it has become the paradigm of anything not currently available for use. Here the original meaning is intended. If the owner had inspected his roof on the eve of the holiday he would have realized that the figs were ready food for the holiday and therefore falling under the category of “prepared food” permitted on Sabbath and holiday. But since at sundown of the holiday he had no knowledge of the situation, the figs were not available to him at the start of the holiday and therefore remain forbidden for the rest of the holiday. Similarly, since the owner of the chicken does not know that the egg (which is completely formed with its hard shell) will be laid on the holiday, since it was not available at the start it cannot become available later.</i>, and is <i>muqṣeh</i> which dried without him realizing it not forbidden? But was it not stated<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Beitzah (Lieberman) 1:2" href="/Tosefta_Beitzah_(Lieberman).1.2">Tosephta 1:2</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Beitzah 2b" href="/Beitzah.2b">Babli 2b</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Beitzah 6b" href="/Beitzah.6b">6b</a>.</i>, “if one slaughters a chicken and found eggs inside it, even if they are complete<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">With shell.</i> they are permitted”? Rebbi Ḥananiah and Rebbi Mana. One said, its taste when eaten from its inside is not comparable to its taste when eaten from the outside. But the other said, it is not completed for a chick<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote">It cannot be used for hatching a new chick. The Yerushalmi presumes that every egg potentially is either food or material for hatching, disputed by the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Beitzah 2a" href="/Beitzah.2a">Babli, 2a</a>.</i> until it comes outside. What are they with milk<sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote">Meat is forbidden to be used with milk. A laid egg may be used with either milk or meat. What is the status of a complete egg found inside a slaughtered chicken?</i>? Let us say, if they are connected to blood vessels they are forbidden, otherwise permitted<sup class="footnote-marker">10</sup><i class="footnote">Cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Beitzah 6b" href="/Beitzah.6b">Babli 6b</a>.</i>.
“Everybody agrees that an egg of which a major part was laid before the start of the holiday may be eaten on the holiday<sup class="footnote-marker">11</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Beitzah (Lieberman) 1:3" href="/Tosefta_Beitzah_(Lieberman).1.3">Tosephta 1:3</a>; in one version in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Beitzah 7a" href="/Beitzah.7a">Babli 7a</a> (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Bekhorot 8a" href="/Bekhorot.8a"><i>Bekhorot</i> 8a</a>) this holds true even if the egg in the meantime had again disappeared into the body of the chicken.</i>”. Where do they disagree? If a minor part came out, where the House of Shammai say that it may be eaten, but the House of Hillel say that it may not be eaten<sup class="footnote-marker">12</sup><i class="footnote">This is a logical consequence of the preceding statement. Since we consider an egg of which more than half is visible as completely laid, an egg of which less that half is visible is not laid at all and therefore is an egg totally laid on the holiday and not food available earlier.</i>. “<sup class="footnote-marker">13</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Beitzah (Lieberman) 1:3" href="/Tosefta_Beitzah_(Lieberman).1.3">Tosephta 1:3</a>; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Beitzah 3b" href="/Beitzah.3b">Babli 3b</a>,<a class="refLink" data-ref="Beitzah 4a" href="/Beitzah.4a">4a</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Shabbat 43a" href="/Shabbat.43a"><i>Šabbat</i> 43a</a>. On the holiday (and Sabbath) one may move only things which may be used on the day (except if one needs the place occupied by an unusable implement, when one may clear the place.) Since the egg is neither food nor a usable implement, it cannot be moved.</i> Just as it is forbidden as food so it is forbidden to be moved. If one was mixed with a hundred or a thousand, they are all forbidden<sup class="footnote-marker">14</sup><i class="footnote">In general, if prohibited material is a small minority in permitted material, it may be disregarded. For example, passive non-kosher ingredients in food may be disregarded if they constitute less than 1/60 of the permitted food. But if the prohibition either will disappear by itself (as in the case of an egg laid on the holiday which is permitted food on the next day) or may be removed by some action, no such disregard is permitted; the entire mixture is prohibited until the prohibition disappears.</i>”. How? Following him who said, if the preparation is in doubt it is forbidden; but him who said, if the preparation is in doubt it is permitted, here he agrees that it is forbidden. For wind-fall there is a doubt where it fell today or fell yesterday, but here one is forbidden and it is proof for all of them<sup class="footnote-marker">15</sup><i class="footnote">If fruit is found under a fruit tree on the holiday, there is a doubt whether it is forbidden as fallen on the holiday or whether it is permitted as fallen before the holiday. Therefore if this fruit is put into a basket with many other fruits, there is a doubt whether any of this is forbidden. If we hold that the requirement to use only food available before the holiday is biblical (cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 16:23" href="/Exodus.16.23"><i>Ex.</i> 16:23</a>) the wind-fall is forbidden, but if the requirement is rabbinic and traditional, the wind-fall is permitted cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Beitzah 24a" href="/Beitzah.24a">Babli 24a</a>. There is nothing clearly forbidden and the rule spelled out at the end of the preceding note cannot be applied. But if an egg was laid on the holiday, for the House of Hillel it certainly is forbidden and therefore the rule which forbids the mixture for the day can be applied.</i>.
Others say in the name of Rebbi Eliezer: It may be eaten together with its mother<sup class="footnote-marker">16</sup><i class="footnote">Even a follower of the House of Hillel may eat the egg laid on the holiday if he also eats its mother since then the egg was food at the start of the holiday (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Beitzah 1:1:2" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Beitzah.1.1.2">Note 6</a>).</i>. [What means, “it may be eaten together with its mother”?]<sup class="footnote-marker">17</sup><i class="footnote">Corrector’s addition, justified by G.</i> If its mother was prepared for slaughter<sup class="footnote-marker">18</sup><i class="footnote">This is an objection to R. Eliezer’s statement. If the chicken is raised to produce eggs, it is not food and therefore not prepared for the holiday, nor can be its egg before it is laid.</i>. But are not all chickens prepared for slaughter<sup class="footnote-marker">19</sup><i class="footnote">Since a chicken which does not lay eggs anymore also is eaten, every chicken is potential food.</i>? Rebbi Abba said, it may be eaten based on its mother’s preparation<sup class="footnote-marker">20</sup><i class="footnote">Even a follower of the House of Hillel may eat the egg laid by a chicken raised for its meat. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Beitzah 2a" href="/Beitzah.2a">Babli 2a</a>.</i>.
A calf born on a holiday is permitted since it permits itself by slaughter<sup class="footnote-marker">21</sup><i class="footnote">An unborn calf inside its mother does not need separate slaughter; its mother’s slaughter also permits the calf which is considered part of its mother’s body. But once it is born, it must be slaughtered to become food. Since the birth on the holiday introduces the duty of slaughtering, an action to satisfy this duty removes also the rabbinic prohibition of moving the animal.</i>. Rebbi Ze`ira asked, in that case an animal kept outside should be permitted since it permits itself by slaughter<sup class="footnote-marker">22</sup><i class="footnote">A domestic animal kept all year outside, far from permanent human dwellings, may not be cared for on the holiday (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Beitzah 5:7:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Beitzah.5.7.1">Mishnah 5:7</a>), therefore certainly it is not prepared to be food of the holiday. This seems to contradict the earlier statement.</i>, deer should be permitted since it permits itself by slaughter<sup class="footnote-marker">23</sup><i class="footnote">Since one is prohibited from catching deer, it cannot possibly be holiday food unless caught before the holiday.</i>. Rebbi Ze`ira came back and said, a calf is fully formed the day before, but here<sup class="footnote-marker">24</sup><i class="footnote">In the case of the chicken egg.</i> it was born on the day it was fully formed. Rebbi Abba said, this follows him who said that an animal gives birth after split months; but what is for him who said, an animal gives birth after a fixed number of months<sup class="footnote-marker">25</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Bekhorot 21a" href="/Bekhorot.21a">Babli <i>Bekhorot</i> 21a</a>, a disagreement whether cattle gives birth after a full 9 months or not. If the number of days of gestation is not fixed, the owner of the pregnant animal cannot have the intention to use the unborn calf as food on the holiday since he could not know the prospective date of birth.</i>? Think of it, that he knew when a male had mounted it<sup class="footnote-marker">26</sup><i class="footnote">In this case it might be possible to compute the day of birth in advance and the calf would be food if that day is a holiday.</i>. A pigeon chick born on a holiday is permitted since it permits itself by slaughter<sup class="footnote-marker">28</sup><i class="footnote">An egg is permitted only if no chick developed. A fully formed chick in an egg still is not considered a chick but a forbidden egg; even if a human takes away the shell and slaughters the chick it is the slaughter of an egg which cannot possibly permit any food which was forbidden.</i>; did we not state, an egg which had started to develop, and chicks which do not yet have wings are forbidden as abomination, but one does not whip because of carcass meat? And Rebbi Ḥaggai said, even if he slaughtered them<sup class="footnote-marker">28</sup><i class="footnote">An egg is permitted only if no chick developed. A fully formed chick in an egg still is not considered a chick but a forbidden egg; even if a human takes away the shell and slaughters the chick it is the slaughter of an egg which cannot possibly permit any food which was forbidden.</i>. Rebbi Abun said, explain it about those born with wings<sup class="footnote-marker">29</sup><i class="footnote">For these the argument of <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Beitzah 1:1:5" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Beitzah.1.1.5">Note 21</a> applies.</i>.
Rebbi Abbahu in the name of Rebbi Yose ben Rebbi Ḥanina (asked)<sup class="footnote-marker">30</sup><i class="footnote">Delete with G,<b>G</b>.</i>: <sup class="footnote-marker">31</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Beitzah 7a" href="/Beitzah.7a">Babli 7a</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bekhorot 8a" href="/Bekhorot.8a"><i>Bekhorot</i> 8a</a>.</i> Any which has sexual relations during daytime gives birth during daytime, during nighttime gives birth during nighttime. They objected, does a chicken not have sexual relations only during daytime and it gives birth both during daytime and nighttime<sup class="footnote-marker">32</sup><i class="footnote">Denied by the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Beitzah 7a" href="/Beitzah.7a">Babli 7a</a> which holds that chicken eggs are laid only during daytime and therefore an egg found in the nest at early dawn of a holiday is permitted as laid the day before.</i>? Rebbi Abun said, there is a difference, since it gives birth without a male<sup class="footnote-marker">33</sup><i class="footnote">It may lay several eggs from one fertilization by a male.</i>. Rebbi Ze`ira<sup class="footnote-marker">34</sup><i class="footnote">In both Genizah texts, Ze`ur (distinct from Ze`urah = Ze`ira).</i> in the name of Giddul: A calf born from a <i>ṭerefa</i><sup class="footnote-marker">35</sup><i class="footnote">An animal which cannot be expected to live for another 12 month; practically an animal injured or missing a limb or organ enumerated in <i>Ḥulin</i> Chapter 3. The meat from such an animal is biblically prohibited.</i> on a holiday is permitted; it has the status of something prepared<sup class="footnote-marker">36</sup><i class="footnote">As food for the holiday. The calf is permitted food, the mother is not. Before the birth the calf was forbidden as <i>ṭerefa</i>, now it is permitted and permits itself by slaughter.</i> hidden in something not prepared.
It has been said elsewhere: the egg from a carrion is of permitted use when similar ones are sold in the market; otherwise it is forbidden, according to the opinion of Shammai; Hillel forbids it in any case. What is the reason of the House of Shammai? Shammai allows it because he assumes that the egg was matured before the defect occurred in the animal.<br>If this is so, why should the permission depend on the suitability of the egg for sale in the market? If this is not the case, one would be allowed to eat even the egg that is still attached to the ovary (an integral part of the carrion and forbidden).<br>Is it true that Shammai conforms to the latter Mishna and Hillel to the former?
<sup class="footnote-marker">38</sup><i class="footnote">A slightly corrupt parallel to this text is in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Avodah Zarah 2:7:3" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Avodah_Zarah.2.7.3"><i>Avodah zarah</i> 2:7, Notes 293–302</a>.</i> Rebbi Ḥiyya in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan: At first they were saying, one curdles<sup class="footnote-marker">39</sup><i class="footnote">To make cheese. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Chullin 116a" href="/Chullin.116a">Babli <i>Ḥulin</i> 116a</a>.</i> neither with the stomach content of the carcass<sup class="footnote-marker">40</sup><i class="footnote">An animal which was not slaughtered following the rules of ritual slaughter. whose flesh therefore is forbidden as meat.</i> nor with the stomach contents of the Gentile<sup class="footnote-marker">41</sup><i class="footnote">“Gentile’s stomach content” naturally means “stomach content of animal slaughtered by a Gentile.”</i>. They changed to say, one curdles with the stomach content of the carcass but not with the stomach contents of the Gentile. Rebbi Yose ben Abun said, Samuel bar Abba asked: Does this follow Rebbi Eliezer, since Rebbi Eliezer said, the thoughts of the Non-Jew are about pagan worship<sup class="footnote-marker">42</sup><i class="footnote">Does this follow R. Eliezer (whom practice does not follow in general) since it treats Gentile rennet as more problematic than the one derived from a carcass? Since here practice is said to follow R. Eliezer, the tradition seems questionable; one has to search for parallel traditions either to confirm or to reject.</i>? Rebbi Yasa in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan: Originally, they said, one does curdle neither with carcass stomach contents nor with Gentile’s stomach contents. They changed to say, one does curdle with carcass stomach contents and with Gentile’s stomach contents. Rebbi Yose said, the language of a <i>baraita</i> supports Rebbi Ḥiyya bar Abba<sup class="footnote-marker">43</sup><i class="footnote">The first version which keeps the prohibition of rennet from Gentile sources.</i>: Stomach contents of a Gentile or a carcass is forbidden following the original teaching. The stomach contents of a qualified animal which suckled from a <i>ṭerefa</i><sup class="footnote-marker">35</sup><i class="footnote">An animal which cannot be expected to live for another 12 month; practically an animal injured or missing a limb or organ enumerated in <i>Ḥulin</i> Chapter 3. The meat from such an animal is biblically prohibited.</i> are forbidden following the former teaching, and the stomach contents of a <i>ṭerefa</i> which suckled from a qualified one are permitted following the later teaching. And even if the House of Shammai would argue like the House of Hillel in the first teaching, an egg is a growth of its body<sup class="footnote-marker">44</sup><i class="footnote">This now connects the discussion with the Mishnah here. The problem is to understand the position of the House of Hillel since the preparation of food is permitted on a holiday (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 12:16" href="/Exodus.12.16"><i>Ex.</i> 12:16</a>). According to the Babli and one opinion in the Yerushalmi the prohibition applies only to eggs laid by chickens raised to produce eggs. Then the mother was not food at the start of the holiday. While the egg is food according to everybody, for the House of Hillel it cannot change its character from non-food to food on the holiday. Then the question is raised why milk which is prohibited as food before being ingested by a ruminant becomes permitted by the animal’s stomach. In this question, the revised ruling in matters of rennet would agree with the stance of the House of Shammai. But the answer is that the two cases cannot be compared; the stomach contents of an animal never were part of the animal and do not have the latter’s status.</i>; the contents of the stomach come from the outside. It parallels what Rebbi Yose ben Rebbi Abun said in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan: It happened to the sons of Jehudah ben Shamuai that wolves tore more than 300 of their flock. The case came before the Sages who permitted their stomach contents<sup class="footnote-marker">45</sup><i class="footnote">T he latter teaching which permits rennet from animals prohibited as meat is confirmed by a ruling in an actual case.</i>. They said, an egg is a growth of its body; the contents of the stomach come from the outside.
“If it was laid on the holiday it may be eaten on the Sabbath, on the Sabbath it may be eaten on the holiday<sup class="footnote-marker">46</sup><i class="footnote">An egg laid on a holiday which is a Friday may be eaten on the following Sabbath; and an egg laid on a Sabbath may be eaten on a holiday which is on the following Sunday.</i>. Rebbi Jehudah said in the name of Rebbi Eliezer, this is the disagreement. The house of Shammai say that it may be eaten, but the House of Hillel say that it may not be eaten<sup class="footnote-marker">47</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Beitzah (Lieberman) 1:3" href="/Tosefta_Beitzah_(Lieberman).1.3">Tosephta 1:3</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Beitzah 4a" href="/Beitzah.4a">Babli 4a</a>. According to R. Eliezer, the House of Hillel hold that the prohibition of newly available food on Sabbath and holiday is biblical; therefore since the egg was unavailable one moment before the start of the Sabbath it is unavailable on the Sabbath. For the anonymous majority and the House of Shammai the prohibition is rabbinical; since holiday and Sabbath represent two different kinds of holiness, the prohibition on one day has no influence on the other day.</i>.” Rebbi Ḥanina instructed the Sepphoreans about spontaneous growth of mustard<sup class="footnote-marker">48</sup><i class="footnote">In the Sabbatical year, as exception to R. Simeon’s rule that all spontaneous growth of cultivated plants are forbidden in the Sabbatical.</i> and about the egg following Rebbi Jehudah. Rebbi Joḥanan came and preached to them following the rabbis here and the rabbis there<sup class="footnote-marker">49</sup><i class="footnote">That spontaneous growth of mustard is forbidden as object of trade, and that an egg laid on the holiday was permitted on the Sabbath. From here on to the end of the next paragraph the text also is in <i>Ševi`it</i> 9:1 (Notes 22–30,<b>שׁ</b>).</i>. Rebbi Abba bar Zemina in the name of Rebbi Joṣadaq: Because of these two things did Rebbi Joḥanan descend from Sepphoris to Tiberias. He said, why did you bring me this old man, what I am permitting he prohibits, what I am prohibiting he permits<sup class="footnote-marker">50</sup><i class="footnote">The historicity of this remark is doubtful since R. Joḥanan was R. Ḥanina’s student in Sepphoris (at least in Aggadah). It is reasonable to accept the fact that R. Joḥanan went to Tiberias to become there Chief Rabbi.</i>. Rebbi Abba said, there came a case before Rebbi Yasa and he wanted to act following Rebbi Joḥanan but when he heard that Rav and Rebbi Ḥanina disagreed he refrained.
<sup class="footnote-marker">51</sup><i class="footnote">This paragraph also is copied in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Eruvin 3:8:2-3" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Eruvin.3.8.2-3"><i>Eruvin</i> 3, Notes 169–176</a>, where the readings of <i>Beṣah</i> and <i>Ševi`it</i> are compared.</i> Because they disagreed: May the remainders of a wick, a fire, or oil that burned out on the Sabbath be lit on the holiday<sup class="footnote-marker">52</sup><i class="footnote">This refers to <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Eruvin 3:7:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Eruvin.3.7.1">Mishnah <i>Eruvin</i> 3:9</a> where it is stated that if a holiday precedes or follows a Sabbath one may make two <i>eruvin</i> to move one’s Sabbath domain in two different directions for the two days. This clearly presupposes that the restriction of one’s Sabbath domain to 2’000 cubits outside of town is rabbinical. (It is agreed that the limit of 12 <i>mil</i>, 3 <i>parasang</i>, is biblical.) In the case here, it is agreed that the remainders of spent fuel are <i>muqṣeh</i> on the Sabbath, when lighting a fire was prohibited. If this is a biblical prohibition then it is <i>muqṣeh</i> also on the holiday which is on Sunday, but if it is rabbinic then it will be permitted on the holiday, when lighting a fire is permitted.</i>? Rav and Rebbi Ḥanina both say it is forbidden, but Rebbi Joḥanan says, it is permitted. Rebbi Mana said before Rebbi Yudan, is it the reverse regarding an egg<sup class="footnote-marker">53</sup><i class="footnote">Since the dispute between the Houses of Hillel and Shammai was described earlier as whether a chicken automatically is food on a holiday or only if it was raised for its meat, not for producing eggs.</i>? He said to him, since the rabbis compare it, it means that the two cases are identical<sup class="footnote-marker">54</sup><i class="footnote">At least for the House of Hillel, cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Beitzah 1:1:9" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Beitzah.1.1.9">Note 47</a>.</i>. They said in the name of four Elders<sup class="footnote-marker">55</sup><i class="footnote">According to the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Eruvin 38b" href="/Eruvin.38b">Babli <i>Eruvin</i> 38b</a> the Elders are Rabban Simeon ben Gamliel, R. Ismael ben R. Joḥanan ben Beroqa, R. Eleazar ben R. Simeon, and R. Yose ben R. Jehudah. In <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Eruvin (Lieberman) 4:2" href="/Tosefta_Eruvin_(Lieberman).4.2">Tosephta <i>Eruvin</i> 4:2</a> the statement is R. Meïr’s.</i>: If somebody’s <i>eruv</i> was eaten on the first &lt;day&gt; he is like the people of his town on the second &lt;day&gt;<sup class="footnote-marker">56</sup><i class="footnote">Essentially stated in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Eruvin 3:7:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Eruvin.3.7.1">Mishnah <i>Eruvin</i> 3:9</a>.</i>. Rav Ḥuna said in the name of Rav: Practice follows the four Elders<sup class="footnote-marker">57</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Eruvin 38b" href="/Eruvin.38b">Babli <i>Eruvin</i> 38b</a>.</i>. Rav Ḥisda asked: the argument of Rav seems to be inverted. There, he makes it two sanctities, but here he makes it one sanctity. Because they disagreed: May the remainders of a wick, a fire, or oil that burned out on the Sabbath be lit on the holiday? Rav and Rebbi Ḥanina both say it is forbidden, but Rebbi Joḥanan says, it is permitted. Rebbi Mana said before Rebbi Yudan, is it the reverse regarding an egg? He said to him, since the rabbis compare it, it means that the two cases are identical<sup class="footnote-marker">58</sup><i class="footnote">Instead of the last four sentences the source G simply states that one repeats from the start of the paragraph. Rav Ḥisda’s question really is not answered. The Babli gives several tentative explanations of Rav’s position. The one consistent with the Yerushalmi is that only weekdays can prepare for holidays but the Sabbath cannot prepare for a holiday. Since the reason for Rav’s ruling in the case of fuel is not based on the number of sanctities involved, his decisions are consistent.</i>.
Everybody agrees that wind-fall is forbidden<sup class="footnote-marker">59</sup><i class="footnote">Fruit that fell from its tree on a holiday.</i>. Rebbi Abun bar Ḥiyya asked before Rebbi Ze`ira: What is the difference between wind-fall and eggs? Is wind-fall on its mother<sup class="footnote-marker">60</sup><i class="footnote">The tree. Since fruit hanging on a tree must be harvested before it can be moved, it is biblically forbidden.</i> not forbidden, but are eggs in their mother not permitted? Then if it was laid on the Sabbath it should not be eaten on the holiday, if laid on the holiday it should not be eaten on the Sabbath<sup class="footnote-marker">61</sup><i class="footnote">If the egg only is permitted because it may be eaten in its mother, on a Sabbath when slaughter is prohibited the egg should be prohibited according to everybody if the holiday was on a Friday.</i>! He answered him, is that so? Is it not enough that you were restrictive about it that if it was laid on the holiday it may not be eaten on the holiday that you want to be more restrictive that if laid on the holiday it should not be eaten on the Sabbath, if laid on the Sabbath it should not be eaten on the holiday<sup class="footnote-marker">62</sup><i class="footnote">Since we had concluded earlier that also for the House of Hillel it is a matter of preparation, not harvest or a similar biblical injunction, there is no reason to pile rabbinic restrictions on rabbinic restrictions.</i>? Rebbi Jeremiah asked, what is the status of decorations of a <i>sukkah</i><sup class="footnote-marker">63</sup><i class="footnote">Edible decorations. Since the <i>sukkah</i> may not be demolished during the week of Tabernacles, any decorations are part of the <i>sukkah</i> and may not be taken. The question is about the Eighth Day which is a holiday but not part of Tabernacles and not a time when the <i>sukkah</i> is used. Cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Shabbat 3:6:2" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Shabbat.3.6.2"><i>Šabbat</i> 3, Note 170</a>.</i>? Rebbi Jeremiah turned around and said, all seven &lt;days&gt; they are insignificant in the <i>sukkah</i>, afterwards they are prepared<sup class="footnote-marker">64</sup><i class="footnote">This is a position denied by R. Jeremiah in <i>Šabbat</i> 3 but accepted by R. Yose’s brother. After the holiday the decorations are simply fruits hanging on the wall which can be taken at any moment.</i>. Rebbi Yose said, all seven &lt;days&gt; the holiness of (seven) [<i>sukkah</i> falls]<sup class="footnote-marker">65</sup><i class="footnote">The best readings are those of the Genizah texts, “the holiness of <i>sukkah</i> is”.</i> on them, afterwards the holiness of the holiday falls on them. What did he come to question? If the holiday falls on a Friday according to him who says, they are two kinds of holiness<sup class="footnote-marker">66</sup><i class="footnote">Since the holiness of the <i>sukkah</i> is biblical, and so is the holiness of holiday, it is consistent to extend the holiness of the decorations to the holiday, but it is not necessary to extend it to a following Sabbath.</i>.

Halakhah 2

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> The House of Shammay say that sour dough {causes liability} by the size of an olive and leavened matter by the size of a date<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">This refers to the prohibition of leavened matter on Passover. While the smallest amount of leavened matter is forbidden, only the possession of a minimal amount triggers the liability for a purification sacrifice if found to be unintentional. The House of Shammai hold that this amount is smaller for actively leavening material than for passive leavened material, whereas the House of Hillel do not differentiate between the two. It will be stated in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Beitzah 1:2:2-3:5" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Beitzah.1.2.2-3.5">Halakhah 2</a> that the disagreement only refers to possession; in matters of eating there is no disagreement. The reason this disagreement is mentioned here is that in the first case the House of Shammai is more lenient than the House of Hillel; the same is true in the second case.</i>, but the House of Hillel say that both are by the size of an olive.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> [<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Beitzah 1:2:2-3:5" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Beitzah.1.2.2-3.5">Halakhah 2</a>]<sup class="footnote-marker">67</sup><i class="footnote">Corrector’s addition, to be deleted. He designated the discussion of the second part of <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Beitzah 1:1:2-10:3" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Beitzah.1.1.2-10.3">Mishnah 1</a> as separate Halakhah. Therefore his count of Halakhot in this Chapter deviates systematically from the scribe’s.</i>. Rebbi Zeriqan in the name of Rebbi Yose ben Ḥanina: They stated this only for its elimination, but for eating the volume of an olive<sup class="footnote-marker">68</sup><i class="footnote">Even the House of Shammai agree that eating leavened matter in the volume of an olive on Passover creates liability.</i>. Rebbi Abbahu in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan, whether for elimination or eating, the volume of an olive<sup class="footnote-marker">69</sup><i class="footnote">This statement refers only to the opinion of the House of Hillel. While leavened matter is forbidden on Passover in the most minute amount, liability is created only by the volume of an olive, whether active leavening or passive leavened matter.</i>. Rebbi Mana was standing with Rebbi Ḥizqiah; he said to him, from where did the rabbi hear this?. He told him, from Rebbi Abbahu. He answered, we also are saying, Rebbi Abbahu in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan, whether for elimination or eating, the volume of an olive<sup class="footnote-marker">15</sup><i class="footnote">If fruit is found under a fruit tree on the holiday, there is a doubt whether it is forbidden as fallen on the holiday or whether it is permitted as fallen before the holiday. Therefore if this fruit is put into a basket with many other fruits, there is a doubt whether any of this is forbidden. If we hold that the requirement to use only food available before the holiday is biblical (cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 16:23" href="/Exodus.16.23"><i>Ex.</i> 16:23</a>) the wind-fall is forbidden, but if the requirement is rabbinic and traditional, the wind-fall is permitted cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Beitzah 24a" href="/Beitzah.24a">Babli 24a</a>. There is nothing clearly forbidden and the rule spelled out at the end of the preceding note cannot be applied. But if an egg was laid on the holiday, for the House of Hillel it certainly is forbidden and therefore the rule which forbids the mixture for the day can be applied.</i>, for otherwise one would have to state “37 extirpations in the Torah”<sup class="footnote-marker">70</sup><i class="footnote">If there were different standards for leavening and leavened matter, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Mishnah Keritot 1:1" href="/Mishnah_Keritot.1.1">Mishnah <i>Keritut</i> 1:1</a> which enumerates the 36 transgressions causing extirpation should have enumerated them separately and arrived at a total of 37.</i>.

Halakhah 3

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> If somebody slaughtered a wild animal or a bird<sup class="footnote-marker">71</sup><i class="footnote">Whose blood has to be covered by dust, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 17:13" href="/Leviticus.17.13"><i>Lev.</i> 17:13</a>.</i> on the holiday, the House of Shammai say that he shall dig with a picket<sup class="footnote-marker">72</sup><i class="footnote">Even though digging is an activity forbidden on the holiday, since slaughter for food is permitted without restriction there is biblical authorization to provide the `necessary dust. The House of Hillel agree that by biblical standards this is true; they say that rabbinically one has to refrain from digging if possible.</i> and cover, but the House of Hillel say that he should not slaughter unless he had dust prepared. But they agree that if he slaughtered that he may dig with a picket and cover, &lt;and&gt; that the ashes of the cooking stove are prepared<sup class="footnote-marker">73</sup><i class="footnote">The ashes produced from the firewood in the stove are qualified as dust to cover the blood of birds. Therefore they are useful for activities permitted on the holiday and cannot be <i>muqṣeh</i>. In this context, “prepared” means “available for the preparation of food on the holiday.”</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> <sup class="footnote-marker">74</sup><i class="footnote">This paragraph is from <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Shabbat 7:2:20" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Shabbat.7.2.20"><i>Šabbat</i> 7, Notes 239–254</a>.</i> Rebbi Ḥiyya in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan: He who cooks carcass meat on a holiday is not flogged, because the category of cooking is permitted on a holiday<sup class="footnote-marker">75</sup><i class="footnote">It is presumed that carcass meat, which is forbidden as human food, is not prepared as animal feed. For R. Joḥanan (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Beitzah 12b" href="/Beitzah.12b">Babli 12b</a>) since making fire and cooking is permitted for preparing food on the holiday (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 12:16" href="/Exodus.12.16"><i>Ex.</i> 12:16</a>) it is permitted for any purpose.</i>. Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish said, he is flogged, for the category of cooking is permitted only for food<sup class="footnote-marker">76</sup><i class="footnote">He disputes that cooking be permitted for anything that is not food.</i>. Rebbi Abba bar Mamal objected to this {statement} by Rebbi Joḥanan. Then one who ploughs on a holiday should not be flogged since actions of the category of ploughing are permitted on a holiday<sup class="footnote-marker">77</sup><i class="footnote">This refers to <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Beitzah 1:3:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Beitzah.1.3.1">Mishnah 1:2</a>. Now digging is a derivative of ploughing, therefore some kind of ploughing is biblically permitted on a holiday.</i>. Rebbi Yose in the name of Rebbi Ila: ordinary ploughing was not permitted<sup class="footnote-marker">78</sup><i class="footnote">Since no plough is authorized, the work is not professional and, since the intent is not to prepare the soil for agriculture, the prohibition is rabbinical; the Houses of Shammai and Hillel do not disagree about the interpretation of a biblical commandment.</i>. Rebbi Shammai said before Rebbi Yose: Rebbi Aḥa in the name of Rebbi Ila, this<sup class="footnote-marker">79</sup><i class="footnote">Both the Houses of Shammai and of Hillel do permit to use a professional tool; they must hold that the intent determines liability.</i> is Rebbi Simeon’s, for Rebbi Simeon said, only if he needs the essence of the matter<sup class="footnote-marker">80</sup><i class="footnote">There is liability only if the prohibited action is the object of his intent, not a by-product. Cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Shabbat 2:5:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Shabbat.2.5.1"><i>Šabbat</i> 2, Note 19</a>.</i>. Rebbi Yose met Rebbi Aḥa. He said to him, did you say this? But did not Rebbi Joḥanan say, the words of Rebbi Meïr are that in 24 matters the House of Shammai are lenient and the House of Hillel restrictive, and this is one of them. Should we say 23<sup class="footnote-marker">81</sup><i class="footnote">Since in this interpretation both Houses agree that the digging does not create liability and the biblical commandment to cover the blood overrides the rabbinic “fence around the law”.</i>? But Rebbi Meïr and Rebbi Simeon both said the same<sup class="footnote-marker">82</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Beitzah 1:3:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Beitzah.1.3.1">Mishnah 1:2</a> is anonymous and therefore presumed to be R. Meïr’s. If it implies the position of R. Simeon then both must agree in this matter. The opponent of R. Simeon in this matter is Rebbi Jehudah, student of his father R. Ilai, who was a student of the Shammaite R. Eliezer. It is intrinsically unlikely that the House of Shammai should accept what later was formulated by R. Simeon.</i>. But were we not of the opinion that Rebbi Yose and Rebbi Simeon both said the same<sup class="footnote-marker">83</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Shabbat 2:5:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Shabbat.2.5.1"><i>Šabbat</i> 2, Note 19</a>. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Shabbat 31b" href="/Shabbat.31b">Babli <i>Šabbat</i> 31b</a>.</i>? Should we say, Rebbi Meïr, Rebbi Yose, and Rebbi Simeon all three said the same<sup class="footnote-marker">84</sup><i class="footnote">Then we should hold that this is their (direct or indirect) teacher R. Aqiba’s position and it is difficult to fathom who would disagree; but we see that this opinion is not generally accepted in tannaitic sources.</i>? But matters which are problematic for the rabbis are obvious for you; are those which are obvious for the rabbis problematic for you? If one harvested for grasses<sup class="footnote-marker">85</sup><i class="footnote">He was weeding and using the uprooted weeds as fodder. This is forbidden on a holiday as it is forbidden on the Sabbath.</i> he is liable for harvesting but is not liable for improving the soil. There is only the problem if he harvested in order to improve the soil. Is he liable for harvesting and for improving the soil? Even if you say it follows Rebbi Simeon, but for the rabbis in any case he ploughed, in any case he harvested<sup class="footnote-marker">86</sup><i class="footnote">In the Babli, these rabbis are identified with R. Jehudah.</i>. Rebbi Mana said, the words of the rabbis support my teacher Rebbi Yose, for Rebbi Ḥiyya said in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan, if one compressed a fish<sup class="footnote-marker">87</sup><i class="footnote">A pickled herring. Since the preparation of food is permitted on a holiday, this sentence and the next are copied in error from <i>Šabbat</i>, since the activities are only forbidden on <i>Šabbat</i>. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Shabbat 145a" href="/Shabbat.145a">Babli <i>Šabbat</i> 145a</a>.</i>, if for its body he is not liable, but if to produce fish sauce he is liable. Even if you say that he said this following Rebbi Simeon, but for the rabbis in any case he compressed, in any case he produced fish sauce<sup class="footnote-marker">88</sup><i class="footnote">This is all one <i>Šabbat</i> liability.</i>.
Rav Abun in the name of the rabbis there: this<sup class="footnote-marker">89</sup><i class="footnote">If the Mishnah was to be understood that ashes from the cooking stove may be used without restriction on the holiday, it should have indicated that it may be used for some mundane action. Since the remark about the ashes is appended to the discussion of a religious act, one infers that it may be used for religious acts only.</i> implies that the ashes of the stove are prepared only for the commandment<sup class="footnote-marker">90</sup><i class="footnote">The ל prefixed by the corrector to the word מצוה is standard Babli syntax; as the following text shows clearly it has to be deleted.</i>. Rebbi Mana said, if he did not prepare it<sup class="footnote-marker">91</sup><i class="footnote">Ashes which existed before the holiday may be designated before the holiday for any use intended on the holiday.</i>. But if he prepared it, one covers with it (excrement)<sup class="footnote-marker">92</sup><i class="footnote">Scribe’s text deleted by the corrector who did not understand the text. As noted already by <i>Qorban ha`Edah</i>, this has to be undeleted.</i> and commandment. This parallels what Rebbi Shammai said, Rebbi Aḥa preached in the name of Rav Jehudah: If he prepared it for the commandment one covers with it for the commandment, for excrement one covers with it excrement and commandment. Rebbi Yose ben Rebbi Abun said, Rav Zera and Rebbi Abba bar Joseph disagreed. One said, there is preparation for excrement, but the other said, there is no preparation for excrement. The one who said that there is no preparation objected to the one who said that there is preparation, could he not use it to cover {the blood of} the Koy<sup class="footnote-marker">93</sup><i class="footnote">The blood of kosher wild animals has to be covered and all their fat may be consumed. The blood of domesticated kosher animals is not covered and their visceral fat is forbidden as food. Mishnah <i>Bikkurim</i>2:8–9 states that the Koy is possibly a wild animal or possibly a domesticated animal; therefore his blood has to be covered but its fat may not be eaten. It may not be slaughtered on the holiday; if slaughtered anyhow its blood may not be covered on the holiday.<br>In <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Bikkurim 2:6:6" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Bikkurim.2.6.6"><i>Bikkurim</i>2:7 (Note 154</a>) R. Eleazar says the Koy is the result of the mating of a he-goat with a hind or a stag mating with a she-goat; but the rabbis say, it is a separate kind and the Sages could not determine its classification.<br>In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Beitzah 8b" href="/Beitzah.8b">Babli 8b</a> this is a declarative sentence, not an interrogation.</i>? He answered him, people are apt to err about the Koy<sup class="footnote-marker">94</sup><i class="footnote">And infer that its fat is permitted since its blood is covered.</i>, people are not apt to err about excrement. He said to him, if you would say so, he would take the picket and cover<sup class="footnote-marker">95</sup><i class="footnote">A possible (not prosecutable) holiday violation. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Beitzah 8a" href="/Beitzah.8a">Babli 8a</a>.</i>. And it was stated so<sup class="footnote-marker">96</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Beitzah (Lieberman) 1:5" href="/Tosefta_Beitzah_(Lieberman).1.5">Tosephta 1:5</a>.</i>: “If one brought dust to plaster his roof, lime to whitewash his house, one uses it to cover.” There are Tannaim who state, one does not use it to cover. Rebbi Yose ben Rebbi Abun in the name of Rav Ḥisda, he who said, one uses it to cover, after the deed; he who said, one does not use it to cover, one who comes to ask before he starts<sup class="footnote-marker">97</sup><i class="footnote">The restrictions on using building materials to cover blood or excrement are rabbinic in nature.</i>.
It was stated<sup class="footnote-marker">98</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Beitzah (Lieberman) 1:5" href="/Tosefta_Beitzah_(Lieberman).1.5">Tosephta 1:5</a>, <i>Ḥulin</i>6:1; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Beitzah 8a" href="/Beitzah.8a">Babli 8a</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Chullin 84b" href="/Chullin.84b"><i>Ḥulin</i> 84b</a>.</i>: “Rebbi Yose says, one does not cover the Koy’s blood because it is a doubtful case.” Since circumcision whose certainty pushes aside the Sabbath, in doubt does not push aside the holiday<sup class="footnote-marker">99</sup><i class="footnote">If a baby is born on a Sabbath it is circumcised the next Sabbath. If he is born during twilight, when it was not clear on which calendar date the birth actually was, then it cannot be circumcised before the eighth day counting from the second possible date. If that should be a holiday, the circumcision has to be delayed for another day since violating the holiday for what may be a ninth, not an eighth, day would be desecrating the holiday. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Shabbat 19:5:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Shabbat.19.5.1">Mishnah <i>Šabbat</i> 19:5</a>.</i>, then for covering the blood which in case of certainty does not push aside the Sabbath it only is logical that in a case of doubt it not push aside the holiday<sup class="footnote-marker">100</sup><i class="footnote">Since according to <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Megillah 1:6:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Megillah.1.6.1">Mishnah <i>Megillah</i> 1:8</a> the only activity forbidden on the Sabbath and permitted on a holiday is the preparation of food. Covering the blood is an activity occurring after what is a preparation of food, and only preparations are permitted.</i>. But is it permitted to slaughter on a Sabbath<sup class="footnote-marker">101</sup><i class="footnote">Since slaughter is forbidden on the Sabbath, covering the blood cannot occur and the premise of the argument is vacuous.</i>? Rebbi Yose said it without attribution, Rebbi Yose ben Rebbi Abun in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan: If he slaughtered for a sick person<sup class="footnote-marker">102</sup><i class="footnote">If freshly cooked meat is prescribed by a competent medical authority to a dangerously sick person, it has to be provided on the Sabbath. The obligation to violate the Sabbath extends to slaughter but not to covering the blood.</i>. They told him, does not the <i>shofar</i> in the countryside disprove it? For in case of certainty it does not push aside the Sabbath, but in a case of doubt it pushes aside the holiday<sup class="footnote-marker">103</sup><i class="footnote">Since outside the Temple the <i>shofar</i> is not blown on a Sabbath (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Rosh Hashanah 4:1:1-2" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Rosh_Hashanah.4.1.1-2">Mishnah <i>Roš Haššanah</i> 4:1</a>), but on the holiday falling on a weekday it is blown everywhere even when it is not known whether the 1<sup class="footnote-marker">st</sup> of Tishre is the 30<sup class="footnote-marker">th</sup> or the 31<sup class="footnote-marker">st</sup> of Elul.</i>. What kind of doubt is there? If it is a weekday, he may blow, if it is a holiday, he may blow<sup>104</sup><i class="footnote">Since on New Year’s Day the blowing is a biblical obligation, it certainly is permitted on that day. On a workday one may blow anyhow.</i>. Rebbi Ḥanina<sup class="footnote-marker">105</sup><i class="footnote">With the Genizah sources, read “Ḥananiah” the late Amora, for Ḥanina, the early one.</i> in the name of Rebbi Aḥa: In case of a hermaphrodite<sup class="footnote-marker">106</sup><i class="footnote">Greek ̓ανδρόγυνος “man-woman”, a person having both male and female sex organs. He is considered a probable male and therefore probably required to hear the <i>shofar</i> sound; therefore he may perform the blowing for similar probable males.</i> who blew. And it was stated so<sup class="footnote-marker">107</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Rosh Hashanah (Lieberman) 2:5" href="/Tosefta_Rosh_Hashanah_(Lieberman).2.5"><i>Roš Haššanah</i> Tosephta 2:5</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Beitzah 29a" href="/Beitzah.29a">Babli 29a</a>.</i>: “The hermaphrodite frees his own kind [but does not free persons not of his own kind.]<sup class="footnote-marker">108</sup><i class="footnote">Corrector’s addition, is found in the Tosephta text but not in the Genizah sources; to be deleted.</i> The sexless frees neither his kind nor not his kind<sup class="footnote-marker">109</sup><i class="footnote">The person with neither male nor female sex characteristics may be a male or a female. As a female he would not be obliged by a religious obligation to be performed at a set time; therefore his blowing cannot free even another sexless person since the blower might be a genetic female and the hearer a genetic male.</i>.” Rebbi Yose<sup class="footnote-marker">110</sup><i class="footnote">The late Amora.</i> asked, if in the case of a hermaphrodite who blew, is that where we stated, it has no counter argument? Rebbi (Abbaye) [Abbin]<sup class="footnote-marker">111</sup><i class="footnote">The corrector’s “Abbin” is a distortion of the text. The scribe’s “Abbaye” is confirmed by a Genizah source. While in old and modern lists of Amoraim no Galilean “R. Abbaye” is listed, he appears as an early Amora in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Rosh Hashanah 1:8:4" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Rosh_Hashanah.1.8.4"><i>Roš Haššanah</i> 1:10, Note 321</a>, not to be confused with the Babylonian Abbaye who never has a title.</i> said, that is one of four cases when the Great Rebbi Ḥiyya said, there is no counter-argument. But Rebbi Eleazar the son of Rebbi Eleazar the caper grower<sup class="footnote-marker">112</sup><i class="footnote">He is the person usually known as Ben Qappara.</i> found a counter-argument. Since circumcision which in a case of doubt does not push aside the holiday, in a case of certainty pushes aside holiday night<sup class="footnote-marker">113</sup><i class="footnote">This argument does not make any sense. Since the text is confirmed by the Genizah sources, the loss of a “not” must have happened at a very early stage of transmission. One must read: “in a case of certainty it does not push aside holiday night”, since circumcision is prescribed for the day (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 12:3" href="/Leviticus.12.3"><i>Lev.</i>12:3</a>).</i>, you can say about covering the blood where in case of certainly it pushes aside holiday night, since in case of certainly it pushes aside holiday night, in a case of doubt it should push aside the holiday<sup class="footnote-marker">114</sup><i class="footnote">This is not a proof but a possibility, and does not exclude an opposite opinion.</i>. This establishes that what was problematic to the earlier rabbis and was difficult for them, so it is problematic to the later rabbis and is a difficulty for them. In the opinion of Rebbi Aḥa, the hermaphrodite frees his own kind <i>a priori</i>, in the opinion of Rebbi Yose, <i>a posteriori</i><sup class="footnote-marker">115</sup><i class="footnote">Since R. Aḥa asserts that the hermaphrodite has the right to blow, he must hold that if such a person comes to ask whether he may blow for others, the answer must be positive. But since R. Yose the Amora questions whether the argument about the hermaphrodite can be used against R. Yose the Tanna, he must hold that the Tosephta does not forbid him to blow but does not authorized it <i>a priori</i>.</i>.
“But they agree that if he slaughtered that he may dig with a picket and cover, and that the ashes of the cooking stove are prepared<sup class="footnote-marker">73</sup><i class="footnote">The ashes produced from the firewood in the stove are qualified as dust to cover the blood of birds. Therefore they are useful for activities permitted on the holiday and cannot be <i>muqṣeh</i>. In this context, “prepared” means “available for the preparation of food on the holiday.”</i>.” That which you are saying is about ashes which were burned before the holiday, but it does not apply to ashes which were burned on the holiday<sup class="footnote-marker">116</sup><i class="footnote">Since the wood has been turned into ashes on the holiday, this is non-food material in a state different from what it was at the start of the holiday and therefore is <i>muqṣeh</i>.</i>, if he did not slaughter, but if he slaughtered it is better that he cover with ashes which were burned on the holiday rather than dig with a picket and cover<sup class="footnote-marker">117</sup><i class="footnote">Since <i>muqṣeh</i> is a rabbinic prohibition but digging a biblical violation, it is better to disregard the rabbinic prohibition even though there is biblical permission to disregard the biblical prohibition. (For <i>muqṣeh</i> as rabbinic institution cf. <i>Introduction to Tractates Šabbat and `Eruvin</i>, p. 3, Note 4.)</i>. The colleagues are saying that a positive commandment pushes aside a prohibition<sup class="footnote-marker">118</sup><i class="footnote">And therefore one may dig to obtain dust to cover the blood.</i>. This is understandable following the opinion of Rebbi Jonah who said, a positive commandment pushes aside a prohibition even if they are not written side by side<sup class="footnote-marker">119</sup><i class="footnote">The dispute between RR. Jonah and Yose is also in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Challah 2:1:5" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Challah.2.1.5"><i>Ḥallah</i>2:1, Note 10</a>. It is not mentioned in the Babli (which nevertheless holds that the principle does not apply to holidays since the rules of holidays are both positive commandments and prohibitions.)</i>. Following the opinion of Rebbi Yose who said, a positive commandment pushes aside a prohibition only if they were written side-by-side, since he started the meritorious deed one tells him to clean it up<sup class="footnote-marker">120</sup><i class="footnote">He must agree that if slaughter is biblically permitted on a holiday, with the consumption of meat a positive commandment, there can be no biblical prohibition to deal with all consequences of the slaughter.</i>. Rebbi Ze`ira asked, if a monkey formed a bowl, what<sup class="footnote-marker">121</sup><i class="footnote">The monkey formed a bowl out of clay on the holiday. If a human made the bowl it clearly would have been <i>muqṣeh</i>, and also forbidden as the result of biblically forbidden work. But the monkey is not a human.</i>? Rebbi Yose ben Rebbi Abun said, Rebbi Ze`ira<sup class="footnote-marker">122</sup><i class="footnote">In one of the Genizah sources this is a purely Babylonian dispute involving a Rav Ze`ura.</i> and Rav Hamnuna disagreed. One said, it is forbidden, but the other said, it is permitted. For him who said that it is forbidden it is like cut-up fig cake which dried out without his knowledge<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">While <i>muqṣeh</i> is the technical term for anything which is not available to be moved on the holiday or Sabbath, the original meaning is “cut-up”, <i>viz</i>., a heap of cut-up figs lying on the flat roof to ferment and to be made into fig cakes. During the fermentation process the mass is inedible; therefore it has become the paradigm of anything not currently available for use. Here the original meaning is intended. If the owner had inspected his roof on the eve of the holiday he would have realized that the figs were ready food for the holiday and therefore falling under the category of “prepared food” permitted on Sabbath and holiday. But since at sundown of the holiday he had no knowledge of the situation, the figs were not available to him at the start of the holiday and therefore remain forbidden for the rest of the holiday. Similarly, since the owner of the chicken does not know that the egg (which is completely formed with its hard shell) will be laid on the holiday, since it was not available at the start it cannot become available later.</i>. For him who said that it is permitted it is as if he put it in order erroneously<sup class="footnote-marker">123</sup><i class="footnote">“Putting in order” means separating heave and tithes, to make the harvest totally profane and generally usable. This is a mental act not dependent of material action, rabbinically prohibited on a holiday but valid.</i>. For him who said that it is permitted, may one use its place<sup class="footnote-marker">124</sup><i class="footnote">The hole created by the monkey could be considered as not-existent before the holiday. But the prohibition of newly created things cannot apply to soil.</i>? Would we say that soil may not be used?

Halakhah 4

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> [4]: The House of Shammai say, one does not move the ladder<sup class="footnote-marker">125</sup><i class="footnote">A ladder used only to service dovecotes.</i> from dovecote to dovecote, but one may bend it<sup class="footnote-marker">126</sup><i class="footnote">One may move it from one opening in the dovecote to another by keeping the top of the ladder in contact with the dovecote.</i> from window to window; and the House of Hillel permit it<sup class="footnote-marker">127</sup><i class="footnote">While the ladder is necessary to use the dovecote it is not part of the building and taking the ladder away from one is not demolishing part of the building.</i>. The House of Shammai say, one may not take<sup class="footnote-marker">128</sup><i class="footnote">On the holiday one may not take a pigeon to slaughter as food unless he took the bird in his hand and reserved it for that purpose before the start of the holiday. But for the House of Hillel a timely declaration of intent is enough.</i> unless one had moved when it was still daylight, but the House of Hillel say, he may stand there and say, this and that I shall take.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> Jehudah the son of Rebbi Ḥiyya<sup class="footnote-marker">129</sup><i class="footnote">A son of the Elder Rebbi Ḥiyya. His twin sons, Jehudah and Ḥizqiah, never held a rabbinic title and never served as rabbis of one place, but made it their business to teach Torah at places not served by the rabbinical establishment.</i> went out. They asked him, what are the rules for a ladder to the upper floor? He told them, it is permitted<sup class="footnote-marker">130</sup><i class="footnote">To move it on the holiday.</i>. When he returned to his father, he asked him, what case came to your hands? He said, I permitted them the ladder to the upper floor. He appointed his Tanna before him, who stated<sup class="footnote-marker">131</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Beitzah (Lieberman) 1:8" href="/Tosefta_Beitzah_(Lieberman).1.8">Tosephta 1:8</a>.</i>, “when was this said, about a ladder for a dovecote. But a ladder for the upper floor is forbidden.” What is the difference between a ladder for a dovecote and a ladder for the upper floor? Rebbi Jacob bar Aḥa said, a ladder for a dovecote if he had used it the day before for work it would be weak; a ladder for the upper floor may be used for work the day before<sup class="footnote-marker">132</sup><i class="footnote">The ladder of the dovecote, because it is intended to be moved from one opening to another, is built as light as possible; moving it is no exertion. But the ladder used to access the upper floor, or the roof from the upper floor, essentially is part of the building and is made as heavy as possible. The ladder for the dovecote cannot be used for heavy work without danger that it might break.</i>. Rebbi Yose ben Rebbi Abun said, on the ladder for a dovecote he may stand and use it for his purpose; on the ladder for the upper floor he may not stand and use it for his purpose<sup class="footnote-marker">133</sup><i class="footnote">Since the ladder for the dovecote is so flimsy, anybody who sees him standing on the ladder knows that he does so for a use permitted on the holiday, to take some pigeons as food. But if he is seen moving the heavy ladder in the building to some other place, people will suspect him of using the ladder for work forbidden on the holiday.</i>. A ladder for a dovecote even if it is used to climb to the upper floor; a ladder for the upper floor even if it used to climb to the dovecote<sup class="footnote-marker">134</sup><i class="footnote">A flimsy ladder may be moved everywhere, a sturdy one nowhere.</i>.
“The House of Shammai say, one may not take<sup class="footnote-marker">128</sup><i class="footnote">On the holiday one may not take a pigeon to slaughter as food unless he took the bird in his hand and reserved it for that purpose before the start of the holiday. But for the House of Hillel a timely declaration of intent is enough.</i> unless one had moved when it was still daylight.” The argument of the House of Shammai seems inverted, as we have stated there<sup class="footnote-marker">135</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Beitzah 4:7:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Beitzah.4.7.1">Mishnah 4:7</a>.</i>: “In addition, Rebbi Eliezer said, a person stands near the drying figs Friday afternoon in a Sabbatical year<sup class="footnote-marker">136</sup><i class="footnote">Since produce is ownerless in the Sabbatical year, no tithes do apply. The same statement would apply in a regular year if tithes had been given from the drying figs. But since the obligation of tithes applies only to produce ready to be used, fig cakes (and raisins) usually are tithed only if they are fully dry. Since no tithes are due, a declaration of intent is all that is needed to make the food usable on Sabbath or holiday; no physical action is required.</i> and says, from here I shall eat tomorrow.” Is Rebbi Eliezer not a Shammaite<sup class="footnote-marker">137</sup><i class="footnote">And therefore his statement should be logically consistent with the Mishnah of the House of Shammai. A parallel to this paragraph is in Halakhah 4:7.</i>? One is restrictive if living things are involved.
“The House of Hillel say, he may stand there and say, this and that I shall take.” The argument of the House of Hillel seems inverted, as we have stated there<sup class="footnote-marker">135</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Beitzah 4:7:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Beitzah.4.7.1">Mishnah 4:7</a>.</i>: “But the Sages say, only if he delineates and says, from here to there;” and here they are saying so? Still they hold that one is restrictive if living things are involved<sup class="footnote-marker">138</sup><i class="footnote">While for fig cake the delineation of the heap from which one may take on the holiday is enough, for living birds one requires a declaration which individual birds are selected for the holiday.</i>. Rebbi Yose ben Rebbi Abun said, Levi was knocking on his dovecote and saying, my dovecote shall provide me tomorrow<sup class="footnote-marker">139</sup><i class="footnote">Levi (ben Sisi) holds that for the House of Hillel the entire dovecote is selected, one does not need separate identification of the birds and <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Beitzah 1:4:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Beitzah.1.4.1">Mishnaiot 1:3</a> and 4:7 are totally parallel. Cf. Halakhah 4:7.</i>.

Halakhah 5

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> If he prepared<sup class="footnote-marker">140</sup><i class="footnote">This Mishnah is a continuation of the preceding one. If he designated certain pigeons but the ones available clearly are different, the new birds are <i>muqṣeh</i>.</i> black ones and found white ones, white ones and found black ones, two and found three, they are forbidden<sup class="footnote-marker">141</sup><i class="footnote">If he designated two and found three, if the third is not clearly distinct from the other two it is impossible to know which is available and which is not.</i>. Three and found two, they are permitted<sup class="footnote-marker">142</sup><i class="footnote">We argue that one left, not that all three left and two new ones took up residence there.</i>. Inside the nest and he found outside the nest they are forbidden, but if there are no others around they are permitted<sup class="footnote-marker">143</sup><i class="footnote">If the designated birds were chicks unable to fly and only these chicks were around, it does not matter where they are found in the neighborhood of the dovecote, even if other dovecotes are not too far away.</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> [5]: The Mishnah is Rebbi’s<sup class="footnote-marker">144</sup><i class="footnote">The statement that if three chicks are selected but only two found, that we presume that one flew away, but not that all three flew away and these are new ones.</i>, as it was stated<sup class="footnote-marker">145</sup><i class="footnote">Tosephta <i>Ma`aser Šeni</i> 5:7, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Beitzah 10b" href="/Beitzah.10b">Babli 10b</a>. The entire paragraph is in <i>Ma`aser Šeni</i> 4:9 (Notes 160–166) with the quotes changed as appropriate.</i>: “200 and he found a mina, (the mina was taken away) [a mina was deposited and a mina taken away]<sup class="footnote-marker">146</sup><i class="footnote">The corrector’s text is the Babli’s and the Tosephta’s; it should be deleted. The scribe’s text in parentheses is confirmed by <b>G</b>and the parallel in <i>Ma`aser Šeni</i>.</i>, the words of Rebbi. But the Sages are saying, it is profane.<sup class="footnote-marker">147</sup><i class="footnote">Rebbi holds that if a smaller amount is found it is the remainder of the original dedicated amount. The Sages hold that if money was set aside for Second Tithe and the exact amount was not recovered, it is different money and never dedicated. They must hold that if three chicks were selected but only two found, these are forbidden as never selected.</i>” There, we have stated<sup class="footnote-marker">148</sup><i class="footnote">Mishnah <i>Ma`aser Šeni</i> 4:12 (Note 146).</i>: “If somebody said to his son, ‘Second Tithe is in that corner’ but he found it in another corner, that is profane.” Rebbi Jacob bar Aḥa in the name of Rebbi Assi, this is Rebbi’s, as we have stated<sup class="footnote-marker">145</sup><i class="footnote">Tosephta <i>Ma`aser Šeni</i> 5:7, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Beitzah 10b" href="/Beitzah.10b">Babli 10b</a>. The entire paragraph is in <i>Ma`aser Šeni</i> 4:9 (Notes 160–166) with the quotes changed as appropriate.</i>: “Two hundred and he found a talent, a talent was taken, the word of Rebbi, but the Sages say, it is profane.” He turned around and said, this is everybody’s opinion since pigeon chicks usually start to fly<sup class="footnote-marker">149</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Beitzah 10b" href="/Beitzah.10b">Babli 10b</a>, as final opinion. In this opinion, the two cases are not comparable.</i>. But did not Rebbi Ḥalaphta ben Shaul state, the same rule applies to pigeon chicks and to eggs<sup class="footnote-marker">150</sup><i class="footnote">If on the eve of the holiday he found a number of eggs and instead of taking them away he declared them food for the holiday, but then the next morning he found a different number.</i>? Therefore, it is Rebbi’s. There, his father put it there and he found, here, his he put it there and he found it<sup class="footnote-marker">151</sup><i class="footnote">This would be a different reason to find the two cases not comparable.</i>. Rebbi Abba bar Cohen said before Rebbi Yose in the name of Rebbi Aḥa: Rebbi Abba bar Zavda instructed according to Rebbi for Second Tithe<sup class="footnote-marker">152</sup><i class="footnote">While the two cases are not comparable, they follow parallel rules.</i>.
Rebbi Yudan said, this<sup class="footnote-marker">153</sup><i class="footnote">Discussion of the statement in the Mishnah that if chicks are forbidden who could not fly when designated in the dovecote and then found in front if the dovecote.</i> is only if there were there two broods. But if only one brood is there it does not apply<sup class="footnote-marker">154</sup><i class="footnote">If there is no probability that these may be from another place.</i>. But did we not state, “if there are no others around they are permitted”<sup class="footnote-marker">155</sup><i class="footnote">Is not R. Yudan’s statement that of the Mishnah?</i>? [Rebbi Yose bar Abun said,]<sup class="footnote-marker">156</sup><i class="footnote">The corrector’s additions in parentheses are without bases in the sources and have to be deleted.</i> if there only is a single chick available<sup class="footnote-marker">157</sup><i class="footnote">One might interpret the Mishnah as permitting the use only if there is only one chick and the identity is guaranteed. But following R. Yudan it is sufficient if there is only one brood, even if only part of the chicks are designated as food. Differently in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Beitzah 11a" href="/Beitzah.11a">Babli 11a</a>.</i>. [Only one brood of black ones.]<sup class="footnote-marker">156</sup><i class="footnote">The corrector’s additions in parentheses are without bases in the sources and have to be deleted.</i>
<sup class="footnote-marker">162</sup><i class="footnote">While this text belongs here it also is found in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Shabbat 12:1:6" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Shabbat.12.1.6"><i>Šabbat</i> 12, Notes 39–44</a>.</i> Samuel said, anybody who tightly closes the shutters<sup class="footnote-marker">163</sup><i class="footnote">Greek θυρίς “shield, armor”.</i> on the holiday is liable because of building. This is difficult. Something which if it was done on the Sabbath makes him liable for a purification sacrifice<sup class="footnote-marker">164</sup><i class="footnote">Following Samuel.</i> the House of Hillel permit to restore<sup class="footnote-marker">165</sup><i class="footnote">On the holiday.</i>? Rebbi Ḥanania in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan: They permitted the end because of the start. For if you say that he cannot put them back he will not open. Don’t let him open! Then he detracts from the enjoyment of the holiday. Rebbi Aḥa said, he may put them back on condition that he not restore completely<sup class="footnote-marker">166</sup><i class="footnote">Then the work is not professional and comparable to writing with the back of one’s hand.</i>. Rebbi Yose ben Rebbi Abun said, if there is no door there. But if there is a door he uses the door<sup class="footnote-marker">167</sup><i class="footnote">If customers can have access to the store without the owner removing the shutters, the House of Hillel will agree that the emergency permit is not valid.</i>.

Halakhah 6

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> The House of Shammai say that one may not take the pestle<sup class="footnote-marker">158</sup><i class="footnote">Or any other implement whose common use is not for the preparation of food.</i> to cut meat on it, but the House of Hillel permit. The House of Shammai say that one may not give the hide to be trampled on<sup class="footnote-marker">159</sup><i class="footnote">Hides of animals slaughtered as food on the holiday are raw material for the manufacture of leather. The House of Shammai hold that hides stripped of all the meat are industrial material and <i>muqṣeh</i> on the holiday. The House of Hillel hold that (almost) nothing becomes <i>muqṣeh</i> it it was not so at the start of the holiday. In this version of the Mishnah the hides may not be brought to the workplace.</i> nor lift it<sup class="footnote-marker">160</sup><i class="footnote">Or otherwise move them while <i>muqṣeh</i>.</i> unless meat was left on it, but the House of Hillel permit. The House of Shammai say that one may not remove the shutters<sup class="footnote-marker">161</sup><i class="footnote">Grocery stores usually were shuttered by iron plated movable on hinges; opening the store meant turning the shutters into horizontal sale platforms. Since the grocer may give on credit spices or other food items to housewives whose supply did run out on the holiday, the House of Hillel encourage this by letting the grocer lock his store on the holiday.<br>In the Babli, the independent Mishnah mss., and as quote in <b>G,</b>the sentence about the shutters is first in the Mishnah. Since this sentence is discussed first in the Halakhah, it seems clear that the order of the items in the Mishnah of the <i>editio princeps</i>, which is not from the ms., is an aberration.</i> but the House of Hillel permit even to return them.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> [“The House of Shammai say that one may not take the pestle<sup class="footnote-marker">158</sup><i class="footnote">Or any other implement whose common use is not for the preparation of food.</i> to cut meat on it, but the House of Hillel permit.”] Therefore, not to cut meat on it is forbidden<sup class="footnote-marker">168</sup><i class="footnote">Work tools may be used on the holiday only for preparation of food; otherwise they remain <i>muqṣeh</i>.</i>. This is what <sup class="footnote-marker">169</sup><i class="footnote">The remainder of the text here is from <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Shabbat 17:2:2-3" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Shabbat.17.2.2-3"><i>Šabbat</i> 17, Notes 48–50</a>. R. Nehemiah states there that while the general rule is that implements may be moved and used on Sabbath and holiday, tools of trade may be used only for a direct need of the day.</i> Rebbi (Abba,)<sup class="footnote-marker">170</sup><i class="footnote">Unjustified deletion by the corrector; the name is confirmed by both parallel sources.</i> Ḥinena bar Shelemiah in the name of Rav said: The Sages agree with Rebbi Neḥemiah about the press, the plank, and the mace; the press with which he presses, the plank on which one beats them, the mace with which he smashes them<sup class="footnote-marker">171</sup><i class="footnote">The items mentioned all belong to the wine press and represent considerable investment. The mace is used to mash grapes on the plank put over the vat in the wine press.</i>.
[“The House of Shammai say that one may not give the hide.”] They agree that one may not scrape it<sup class="footnote-marker">172</sup><i class="footnote">Hide which becomes available on the holiday may not be scraped clean from meat fibers; this would be professional preparation for tanning.</i>. Where do we hold? If it is connected to it, it is part of its body<sup class="footnote-marker">173</sup><i class="footnote">When the House of Shammai ask that a piece of meat be on the hide, they could not mean that the meat be still fully connected to the hide since then their statement would be obvious.</i>. If separated, <sup class="footnote-marker">174</sup><i class="footnote">This statement is from <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Shabbat 16:2:3" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Shabbat.16.2.3"><i>Šabbat</i> 16, Notes 79–80</a>.</i> it parallels what Rebbi Jacob bar Aḥa said, Ḥinena from Cartagena in the name of Rebbi Hoshaia: On a saddle bag<sup class="footnote-marker">175</sup><i class="footnote">Greek δισάκκιον. Literally “double bag”.</i> full of coins one puts a loaf and may move it<sup class="footnote-marker">176</sup><i class="footnote">While the saddle bag is <i>muqṣeh</i>,anything <i>muqṣeh</i> may be moved if it serves as a basis for something which may be moved. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Shabbat 142b" href="/Shabbat.142b">Babli <i>Šabbat</i> 142b</a>.</i>. As the following: an Antiochene cooking vessel fell on Rebbi Jeremiah on the Sabbath; they thought about it<sup class="footnote-marker">177</sup><i class="footnote">Since cooking is forbidden on the Sabbath, a cooking vessel may not be moved unless the intention is for some use which is legitimate on the Sabbath.</i> and lifted it. Not that practice follows Rebbi Hoshaia but that Rebbi Jeremiah should not be endangered. They agree that one may not salt it. It was stated: but he might salt meat on it to roast<sup class="footnote-marker">178</sup><i class="footnote">Salting the hide is the first step in the tanning process; as such it is forbidden on the holiday. But since cooking is permitted, the hide may be used as table on which meat is salted before it is roasted. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Beitzah 11a" href="/Beitzah.11a">Babli 11a</a>.</i>. The colleagues in the name of Rav: A person may salt a large quantity even though he will be able to eat form it only a small quantity. Rebbi Aḥa in the name of Rav: One salts cunningly, and salts cunningly; he salts here and salts there until he salted everything<sup class="footnote-marker">179</sup><i class="footnote">The cunning is that one spreads out the salting process and moves from place to place on the hide until every place has been salted. This is an approved circumvention of the holiday rules to encourage people to eat meat on the holiday, which in absence of refrigeration has to be fresh. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Beitzah 11b" href="/Beitzah.11b">Babli 11b</a>.</i>.

Halakhah 7

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> The House of Shammai say, one may bring neither a small child, nor a <i>lulav</i>, nor a Torah scroll into the public domain, but the House of Hillel permit it<sup class="footnote-marker">180</sup><i class="footnote">Since in principle everything forbidden on the Sabbath is forbidden on the holiday except the preparation of food, the House of Shammai forbid carrying in the public domain of anything which is not food nor needed for the preparation of food. The House of Hillel hold that since carrying is permitted for the purpose of preparing or serving food, it is permitted for any purpose legitimate on the holiday.</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> Therefore not an adult<sup class="footnote-marker">181</sup><i class="footnote">Since the Mishnah could be read that the House of Hillel only permit to carry a baby but not anybody able to walk by himself.</i>? Rebbi Samuel, the son of Rebbi Yose ben Rebbi Abun said, even an adult is permitted. For what purpose was stated “a small child”? It comes to inform you of the strength of the House of Shammai, how far they are restrictive.
