Samuel was carried from bed to bed<sup class="footnote-marker">182</sup><i class="footnote">It is not clear whether these were his own beds or, since he was a medical man, whether he was carried in a litter on his visits to the sick, as explicitly endorsed in the next paragraph.</i>. Rebbi Ze`ira said before Rebbi Yasa, let us say that he was asthenic<sup class="footnote-marker">183</sup><i class="footnote">Greek ʼασθενής.</i>. He told him, he was healthier than I and you. It was stated, Rebbi Ismael ben Rebbi &lt;yose&gt;<sup class="footnote-marker">184</sup><i class="footnote">Added from <b>G.</b>Here starts a fragment of an Ashkenazic text edited by J. Sussman in <i>Kobez al Yad</i> xii (xxii) 1994 (A).</i> says, the stones on which we sat on in our youth make war against us in our old age. Rebbi Jonah ordered the colleagues, do not sit on the [outside]<sup class="footnote-marker">185</sup><i class="footnote">Corrector’s addition, unsupported by any source, to be deleted.</i> stone benches of the assembly<sup class="footnote-marker">186</sup><i class="footnote">The house of study.</i> of Bar Ulla because they are cold. Rav commanded his students, do not sit on the outside table of the assembly of Issy because it is cold. Rebbi Abba went down to bathe in the public baths of Tiberias, leaning on two Goths<sup class="footnote-marker">187</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Ketubot 62a" href="/Ketubot.62a">Babli <i>Ketubot</i> 62a</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Berakhot 60a" href="/Berakhot.60a"><i>Berakhot</i> 60a</a>.</i>. They fell down and he lifted them up, they fell down and he lifted them up. One asked him, what is this? He said to them, I preserved my strength for my old age. Rav Huna did not come to the house of assembly. Rav Qatina asked, was it not stated that one carries the asthenics?
Rav Huna instructed the Head of the Dispersion<sup class="footnote-marker">188</sup><i class="footnote">A: “the wife of the Head of the Diaspora.” Since it is not clear whether this text is a source or a compilation, and the statement contradicts the following discussion, it should not be accepted.</i> to go out in a chair<sup class="footnote-marker">189</sup><i class="footnote">To be carried on the holiday. Since the Head of the Diaspora was the quasi-king of the Jews of Babylonia, his actions can be classified as public needs.</i>. Rav Ḥisda asked, was it not stated, one does not go out in a chair, whether men or women? Even a student of the Sages does not err in this matter, and Rebbi Huna erred<sup class="footnote-marker">190</sup><i class="footnote">But certainly for public needs it is permitted to use a chair. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Beitzah 25b" href="/Beitzah.25b">Babli 25b</a>.</i>? Rebbi Jeremiah instructed Bar Gerontios the healer to be carried on a sheet when going visiting the sick on the Sabbath<sup class="footnote-marker">191</sup><i class="footnote">If he is carried on an improvised contraption he can take his medical equipment with him, which he could not do were he walking. Since the way of transportation is not a common one, it is only rabbinically forbidden. Since the carrying needs several carriers, it is a Sabbath infraction depending on the action of several persons and neither prosecutable nor generating liability in the sense of the Sabbath laws.</i>. Miasha the grandson of Rebbi Joshua ben Levi was carried on a sheet when going preaching in public on the Sabbath. Rebbi Zeriqan said to Rebbi Ze`ira, when you go to the South, ask about this. It was asked from Rebbi Simon. Rebbi Simon told them in the name of Rebbi Joshua ben Levi, not only for the needs of the public but also for a possible need of the public<sup class="footnote-marker">192</sup><i class="footnote">A general instruction that for urgent public needs the Sabbath (and holiday) restrictions on transportation may be circumvented as described in the previous Note.</i>. Example: Rebbi Eliezer, Rebbi Abba Mari, and Rebbi Mattaniah instructed {to bring}<sup class="footnote-marker">193</sup><i class="footnote">Ginzberg’s reading in G; the edition of the Jerusalem Talmud by the Academy of the Hebrew Language, 2001, reads “to bake”. This is the expression used by the Yerushalmi in Ševi`it 4:2 (Note 28) and its copy in Sanhedrin 3:6, where it is reported that R. Jonah and R. Yose, greater authorities than the three rabbis mentioned here, permitted baking bread for the army of Ursicinus (the legate of the Emperor Gallus in Syria starting 351 C. E.) Since the paragraph deals with transport, Ginzberg’s reading seems to be more appropriate. The rabbis instructed the people to bring bread to the Roman army on the Sabbath, probably by having several people doing the carrying, and to prepare for it even though no request was made before the start of the Sabbath.</i> bread to Ursicinus on the Sabbath, for a possible need of the public.
It was stated: And not the key; the House of Hillel permit<sup class="footnote-marker">193</sup><i class="footnote">In <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Beitzah (Lieberman) 1" href="/Tosefta_Beitzah_(Lieberman).1">Tosephta 1</a>:11 there is a statement that any key may be carried in one’s hand (but not tied to the belt, which is the usual weekday dress.)</i>. And Rav Hoshaia the son of Rav Isaac<sup class="footnote-marker">194</sup><i class="footnote">No such sage is known otherwise. Read with <b>G</b>: “Rav Hoshaia in the name of Rebbi Isaac.”</i> said, this refers to a key of edibles, but not to a key of implements. But did not Rebbi Abbahu sit and teach, and a key of a jewel box<sup class="footnote-marker">195</sup><i class="footnote">Greek διπλωματάριον.</i> in his hand? He had pepper inside.

Halakhah 8

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> The House of Shammai are saying, one may not bring <i>ḥallah</i><sup class="footnote-marker">196</sup><i class="footnote">The priest’s part of bread dough (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 15:20" href="/Numbers.15.20"><i>Num</i>. 15:20</a>).</i> and priests’ parts<sup class="footnote-marker">197</sup><i class="footnote">The priest’s part of profane slaughter, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 18:3" href="/Deuteronomy.18.3"><i>Deut</i>. 18:3</a>.</i> to a Cohen on the holiday, whether they have been separated the day before or on the day itself, but the House of Hillel permit. The House of Shammai said to the House of Hillel, it is an equal cut. <i>Ḥallah</i> and priests’ parts are a gift to the Cohen, and heave<sup class="footnote-marker">199</sup><i class="footnote">The priest’s part in agricultural harvest, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 18:12" href="/Numbers.18.12"><i>Num</i>. 18:12</a>.</i> is a gift to the Cohen. Just as one may not bring heave to the Cohen, so one may not bring these parts. The House of Hillel said to them, no. If you mention heave where he has no right to separate, what can you say about the gifts which he may separate<sup class="footnote-marker">200</sup><i class="footnote">Since harvest is forbidden on the holiday, clearly it must be finished before the holiday. Since it is a deadly sin to eat from grain, wine, or olive oil from which heave was not taken, separating heave is an indispensable part of the harvest and must be finished before the holiday. Therefore one may ask that the priests’ parts in agricultural produce must be delivered before the holiday. But since baking and slaughtering for meat is permitted on the holiday, <i>ḥallah</i> and priest’s parts may be separated on the holiday and there is no reason to forbid their delivery.</i>?
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> It was stated<sup class="footnote-marker">201</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Beitzah (Lieberman) 1:12" href="/Tosefta_Beitzah_(Lieberman).1.12">Tosephta 1:12</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Beitzah 12b" href="/Beitzah.12b">Babli 12b</a>.</i>: “Rebbi Jehudah said, the House of Shammai and the House of Hillel did not disagree that one may bring parts which were separated on the holiday, as well as parts which were separated before the start of the holiday with parts separated on the holiday. Where did they disagree? About parts which were separated before the start of the holiday by themselves, where the House of Shammai forbid and the House of Hillel permit.”
It is difficult for the House of Hillel, could he not have the right to separate heave? Think of it, if he made a stipulation<sup class="footnote-marker">202</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Demai</i> 7:5: “R. Isaac ben Eleazar said, a person can say on Friday, this shall be heave tomorrow, but nobody can say on the Sabbath, this shall be heave tomorrow.” Therefore it is possible to actually separate heave on the Sabbath or a holiday if the modality of separation was stipulated the day before.</i>! One because of the other<sup class="footnote-marker">203</sup><i class="footnote">Since in general heave may not be delivered to the Cohen on the holiday, one makes no exemption for the rare case in which delivery would be possible.</i>. And why was <i>ḥallah</i> stated? Because of <i>ḥallah</i> which was separated on the holiday. As it was stated:<sup class="footnote-marker">204</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Beitzah (Lieberman) 1:14" href="/Tosefta_Beitzah_(Lieberman).1.14">Tosephta 1:14</a>. The text from here to the end of the Halakhah also is in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Pesachim 3:3:2" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Pesachim.3.3.2"><i>Pesaḥim</i>3:3, Notes 68–72</a>.</i> “if one kneads dough on a holiday, he separates its <i>ḥallah</i> on the holiday. If he kneaded it before the holiday but forgot to take its <i>ḥallah</i>, it is forbidden to move it; it is unnecessary to say, to take <i>ḥallah</i> from it.” If he mixed water and flour? He only mentioned “kneaded”, so not when he mixed<sup class="footnote-marker">205</sup><i class="footnote">Even though <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Challah 3:1:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Challah.3.1.1">Mishnah <i>Ḥallah</i> 3:1</a> permits separating <i>ḥallah</i> immediately after mixing the flour with water.</i>? Rebbi Samuel, brother of Rebbi Berekhiah, said: explain it if the dough was impure where he takes <i>ḥallah</i> only at the end. Rebbi Yose ben Rebbi Abun said, it should have been the rule that for pure dough one should take <i>ḥallah</i> only at the end. They instituted that one should take it at the start, lest the dough become impure<sup class="footnote-marker">206</sup><i class="footnote">Since then only the <i>ḥallah</i> has to be guarded from impurity but not the dough itself.</i>. The Mishnah is about the holiday of Passover; therefore on Pentecost and Tabernacles it is permitted<sup class="footnote-marker">207</sup><i class="footnote">The remark applies both to <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Beitzah (Lieberman) 1:14" href="/Tosefta_Beitzah_(Lieberman).1.14">Tosephta <i>Yom Ṭov</i> 1:14</a> and <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Pesachim 3:3:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Pesachim.3.3.1">Mishnah <i>Pesaḥim</i> 3:3</a>.</i>. Rebbi Yose ben Rebbi Abun, Rebbi Ḥuna in the name of Rebbi Aḥa, even on Pentecost and Tabernacles it is prohibited, because of <i>no work shall be done on them</i><sup class="footnote-marker">208</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 12:16" href="/Exodus.12.16"><i>Ex.</i> 12:16</a>. Since impure <i>ḥallah</i> may not be eaten, it may not be baked on a holiday.</i>.

Halakhah 9

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> The House of Shammai say that spices are pounded in a wooden mortar, and salt in a container or with a cooking spoon. But the House of Hillel say that spices are pounded normally in a stone mortar and salt in a wooden mortar<sup class="footnote-marker">209</sup><i class="footnote">The House of Shammai hold that since pounding spices and salt could have been done the day before, the general permission to cook cannot be extended to these preparations; they must be done in a way different from working day usage to avoid a biblical transgression. The House of Hillel hold that pounded spices quickly use their taste (in the absence of containers which can be tightly closed); therefore pounding spices is an integral part of the cooking process and may be done as on a weekday, but for taking salt from a block they agree with the reasoning of the House of Shammai.</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> Could he not pound the day before? The colleagues in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan, because they taste bitter; Rebbi Ze`ira in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan, because they lose their taste<sup class="footnote-marker">210</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Beitzah 14a" href="/Beitzah.14a">Babli 14a</a>.</i>.
Samuel pounded on the side of the mortar<sup class="footnote-marker">211</sup><i class="footnote">If he had to grind some spices he turned the mortar on its side to make it different from weekday pounding. Since this is a statement of what he did, not what he taught, it seems that this was a personal precaution since in his medical practice he might also pound some materials for compounding a medicine which if done in the ordinary way would be a biblical infraction, as mentioned later in this paragraph.</i>. Rav said, anything that is pounded may be pounded in its ordinary way<sup class="footnote-marker">212</sup><i class="footnote">If it may be ground into fine particles at all, it may be done the way it is done on weekdays. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Beitzah 14a" href="/Beitzah.14a">Babli 14a</a> in the name of Samuel.</i>. Rav Huna<sup class="footnote-marker">212</sup><i class="footnote">If it may be ground into fine particles at all, it may be done the way it is done on weekdays. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Beitzah 14a" href="/Beitzah.14a">Babli 14a</a> in the name of Samuel.</i>, Rebbi Jeremiah, Rebbi Immi in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan: Garlic, and cress, and mustard seed are pounded in their ordinary way<sup class="footnote-marker">213</sup><i class="footnote">If this name belongs to the chain of transmission it would have to be Rebbi Huna. But since <b>G</b>also has the title “Rav”, and Rav Huna was the leader of the second generation of Amoraim in Babylonia, one has to read the statement as indicating two sources. Both Rav Huna and R. Jeremiah quoting R. Immi in the name of R. Joḥanan state .…</i>. Rebbi Abbahu in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan: He who makes oenanthium<sup class="footnote-marker">214</sup><i class="footnote">A wine-based medicine (Galen 13. 540). In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Shabbat 140a" href="/Shabbat.140a">Babli <i>Šabbat</i> 140a</a> defined as a suspension of balsamum in old wine.</i> on a Sabbath is liable because of compounding. Is it therefore permitted on a holiday<sup class="footnote-marker">215</sup><i class="footnote">Since it is stated to be forbidden only on a Sabbath and at least the wine base of the medicine is food.</i>? Rebbi Ḥizqiah in the name of Rebbi Jeremiah: Making <i>oenanthium</i> on a holiday is forbidden<sup class="footnote-marker">216</sup><i class="footnote">The way it is formulated for the Sabbath it cannot be formulated for the holiday. Compounding (spices or medicines) is one of the 39 categories of work forbidden on the Sabbath, an unintended infraction makes the person liable for a purification sacrifice since the intentional infraction is a capital crime or deadly sin. But work on holidays is a simple prohibition which never can trigger liability for a purification sacrifice, and intentional infraction is not a deadly sin. Therefore “liable” fits for the Sabbath and “forbidden” for the holiday.</i> because of compounding. Isaac Gold asked Rebbi Joḥanan, may one pound for spice wine<sup class="footnote-marker">217</sup><i class="footnote">Latin <i>conditum (vinum)</i>, usually used for the finished product, here used for the spices to be put into the wine. Even though spice wine might be used for medical purposes it also is a drink for healthy people and therefore its preparation is permitted on the holiday.</i> on a holiday? He said to him, it is permitted. &lt;rebbi isaac from atosha asked rebi simeon be laqish, may one pound to make spice wine on a holiday? he said to him, it is permitted;&gt;<sup class="footnote-marker">218</sup><i class="footnote">Sentence missing in the ms. and printed edition. Since the sentence is both in <b>G</b>and A, it belongs to the text, and was dropped by the scribe because of homeoteleuton.</i> give it to me and I shall drink. Rebbi Ze`ira asked before Rebbi Abbahu: Would one not act well in pounding it the day before? He said to him, yes. The argument of Rebbi Abbahu is inverted. Here he says permitted, and there he says forbidden<sup class="footnote-marker">219</sup><i class="footnote">While R. Abbahu did not explicitly say that pounding spices for spice wine is forbidden on the holiday, he agreed with R. Ze`ira that it was better to pound the day before, implying that on the holiday it must be done in a way different from weekday action, against Rav’s ruling at the start of the paragraph.</i>. But since Rebbi Abbahu knew that Rebbi Ze`ira was restrictive, and that they<sup class="footnote-marker">220</sup><i class="footnote">The Babylonians, including R. Ze`ira the immigrant from Babylonia.</i> are restrictive, therefore he agreed with them. Some want to say that so he said to him: If one wants that it be good he will not pound it the day before. Rebbi Ze`ira asked Kallidromos<sup class="footnote-marker">221</sup><i class="footnote">Reading of S. Liebermann, <i>Tarbiz</i> 3, p. 209.</i>, the slave of Rebbi Judah the Prince, does your master pound to make spice wine on a holiday? He said to him yes, and all kinds of cuts of meat<sup class="footnote-marker">222</sup><i class="footnote">Mincing the meat with the knife, <i>sica</i>. Probably this means to make sausage on the holiday.</i>. Rebbi Isaac ben Rebbi Elazar in the name of Rebbi Immi, the father of Rebbi Eudaimon of Sepphoris. Where do they disagree<sup class="footnote-marker">223</sup><i class="footnote">The disagreement of the Houses of Shammai and Hillel about pounding salt. When it is used sparingly, both agree that it may be pounded in a wooden mortar in the regular way. If great quantities are needed the House of Shammai is restrictive. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Beitzah 14a" href="/Beitzah.14a">Babli 14a</a>.</i>? For grilling. Put for the pot it is permitted. Rebbi Naḥum said, Rebbi Samuel bar Abba asked about noodle dough. To let it dry is forbidden, for the pot it is permitted, part and part is questionable<sup class="footnote-marker">224</sup><i class="footnote">Making noodle dough for use after the holiday clearly is a biblical violation and forbidden. Making soup noodles for immediate use clearly is permitted. Making both cannot be biblically forbidden; whether it is rabbinically forbidden is left undecided.</i>.

Halakhah 10

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> If one selects out legumes<sup class="footnote-marker">225</sup><i class="footnote">He separates the edible from the inedible parts of peas, beans, or lentils.</i> on the holiday, the House of Shammai say he picks out the food<sup class="footnote-marker">226</sup><i class="footnote">Since separating the chaff from the grain is a biblical Sabbath violation, and on the holiday may be classified as preliminary to the preparation of food, the House of Shammai require that on the holiday one follows the Sabbath rules, which let you pick out the food but not the chaff.</i> and eats. But the House of Hillel say, he picks out normally into his chest or a basket, but not on a tablet, not with a fine sieve nor with a coarse sieve<sup class="footnote-marker">227</sup><i class="footnote">They hold that the activity is included in the permitted preparation of food but require that it be done in a way which does not resemble commercial preparation of the harvest for sale.</i>. Rabban Gamliel says, also he drenches and scoops off<sup class="footnote-marker">228</sup><i class="footnote">He proposes to dump the entire material into a vat or barrel filled with water. The chaff will float on the surface and can easily be swept out. Since the legumes will be wet, it is not a commercial procedure.</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> <sup class="footnote-marker">229</sup><i class="footnote">This and the following paragraphs are from <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Shabbat 7:2:36-37" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Shabbat.7.2.36-37"><i>Šabbat</i> 7, Notes 314–342</a>. The Genizah text ends here; the text A brings only the first few lines.</i> Rebbi Jonah asked, if he did this on the Sabbath, in the opinion of the House of Shammai would he be liable? Rebbi Yose said to him, why not? If he did it on the Sabbath would he not be liable according to the House of Hillel? And here he is liable. Rebbi Mana said, my father Rebbi Jonah said it correctly. It is a problem only for the House of Shammai. Why? Because the category of selecting was permitted on the holiday, nothing of the category of selecting was permitted on the Sabbath<sup class="footnote-marker">230</sup><i class="footnote">The objection of R. Yose is pointless. There is no problem for the House of Hillel since they hold that selecting as a category is permitted on the holiday but forbidden on the Sabbath. But we do not know whether the House of Shammai hold the same and are rabbinically restrictive on the holiday more than the House of Hillel or whether they hold that selecting does not belong to the preparation of food but to preliminaries to preparation which are not exempted on the holiday and for which, therefore, the rules are identical on Sabbath and holiday. Since in his days, in the middle of the Fourth Century, the House of Shammai had disappeared for 250 years, no answer can be given.</i>.
<sup class="footnote-marker">231</sup><i class="footnote">The paragraph has a parallel in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Shabbat 74" href="/Shabbat.74">Babli, <i>Šabbat</i> 74a/b</a>.</i> If one selected food out of food, Ḥizqiah said, one is liable; Rebbi Joḥanan said, one is not liable<sup class="footnote-marker">232</sup><i class="footnote">It is somewhat difficult to understand Ḥizqiah’s position. What is biblically forbidden on the Sabbath is removing chaff from food, not food from chaff (except, as mentioned later in this paragraph, if the entire batch was cleaned, when there is no difference what was taken from where.) Biblically Ḥizqiah would have to forbid to remove the food one does not want to eat from the food one wants to eat; the other way would only be rabbinically forbidden.</i>. A <i>baraita</i> disagrees with Ḥizqiah: He selects and eats, he selects and puts on the table<sup class="footnote-marker">233</sup><i class="footnote">For immediate consumption. There is no difference whether one puts food in his own mouth or in others’.</i>. Rebbi Abun bar Ḥiyya in the name of Rebbi Samuel bar Rav Isaac: explain it if guests were eating what was served. But was it not stated: On condition that he did not select all of its kind? In the opinion of Ḥizqiah, because one who selects on the Sabbath is liable. In the opinion of Rebbi Joḥanan, because one who selects normally at another place<sup class="footnote-marker">234</sup><i class="footnote">The “other place” is the Sabbath. Liability on the Sabbath can always be avoided by doing things in a decidedly unprofessional way; the mention of doing things “normally” is appropriate here.</i> is liable. In the opinion of Ḥizqiah, even rings among rings<sup class="footnote-marker">235</sup><i class="footnote">String figs from a heap of string figs.</i>, even pomegranates among pomegranates. Or is it so, even people among people<sup class="footnote-marker">236</sup><i class="footnote">Then it would be forbidden on the Sabbath to call people to read the Torah unless they had been selected beforehand. This we never heard.</i>? How is this? Everybody agrees with that of Rebbi Immi. For Rebbi Immi had guests; he brought before them lupines<sup class="footnote-marker">237</sup><i class="footnote">Greek θερμός.</i> and beans<sup class="footnote-marker">238</sup><i class="footnote">Greek φάσηλος.</i>. He told them, be careful to eat the artichokes<sup class="footnote-marker">239</sup><i class="footnote">Greek κινάρα. On a holiday it is permitted to select anything for immediate consumption, even if there are no remainders, and eat a different dessert at the end.</i> at the end.
It was stated: One does neither select, nor grind, nor sift<sup class="footnote-marker">240</sup><i class="footnote">Translation of the corrector’s text and the text in <i>Šabbat</i>. The scribe’s text “one is not pedantic” would leave open the possibility that coarse selection and grinding was not prosecutable.</i>. He who selects, or grinds, or sifts, on the Sabbath is stoned. On a holiday he absorbs the 40<sup class="footnote-marker">241</sup><i class="footnote">The 39 lashes which are the standard punishment for breaking biblical prohibitions for which no other biblical punishment is specified. The Babli disagrees and declares these activities only rabbinically prohibited on a holiday, cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosafot on Shabbat 95a:2:2" href="/Tosafot_on_Shabbat.95a.2.2">Tosaphot <i>Šabbat</i> 95a, <i>s. v</i>. והרודה</a>.<br>While preparing food is biblically permitted on a holiday as shown later in the paragraph, there is a dispute between the anonymous majority and R. Jehudah whether this includes preparations which could have been made the day before without impairing the quality of the food, which the majority prohibits and R. Jehudah and Rabban Gamliel permit. It is stated here that for the majority the prohibition is biblical, at least concerning preparations for baking.</i>. But did we not state: “he picks out normally into his chest or a basket”? Rebbi Ḥanina from Antonia said, this is Rabban Gamliel’s, for “Rabban Gamliel says, also he drenches and scoops off.” And was it not stated, in the household of Rabban Gamliel they were grinding pepper in their mills<sup class="footnote-marker">242</sup><i class="footnote">Rabban Gamliel will agree that milling flour is biblically forbidden on a holiday; he will hold that grinding pepper in a peppermill is not professionally grinding and not something which may be done the day before without impairing the quality of the spice.</i>? It is permitted to grind but forbidden to select. Rebbi Yose ben Rebbi Abun: Normal grinding was not permitted. And from where that one may neither select, nor grind, nor sift? Rebbi Aḥa in the name of Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish: <i>No work shall be done on them up to and you shall guard the unleavened bread</i><sup class="footnote-marker">244</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 12:16-17" href="/Exodus.12.16-17"><i>Ex.</i> 12:16–17</a>. The text omitted by the quote “up to” permits preparation of food on a holiday, as quoted later in the paragraph.</i>. Rebbi Yose asked, but did one not infer cooking only from there? Rebbi Yose did not say so, but Rebbi Yose in the name of Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish: <i>Only what can be eaten by every person this alone may be made by you, up to and you shall guard the unleavened bread</i><sup class="footnote-marker">245</sup><i class="footnote">There is nothing missing between the two quotes, so that the note “up to” seems to be superfluous. The meaning is explained in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosafot on Beitzah 3a:1:1" href="/Tosafot_on_Beitzah.3a.1.1">Tosaphot <i>Beṣah</i> 3a s.v. גזרה</a> (at the end): vv. 16,17 form a unit: <i>what can be eaten by every person this alone may be made by you, and you shall guard the unleavened bread</i>. Any preparation of <i>mazzah</i> which requires guarding against possible leavening is permitted on the holiday, anything preceding this, i. e., mixing flour with water to make dough, is forbidden.</i>. Ḥizqiah stated in disagreement<sup class="footnote-marker">246</sup><i class="footnote">Against the Mishnah where the House of Hillel permit selecting.</i>: <i>only, every</i>, <i>person</i>, are diminutions, not to select, nor to grind, nor to sift on a holiday.
<sup class="footnote-marker">247</sup><i class="footnote">This text also appears in <i>Šabbat</i> Chapter 20 (17c line 35). The parallel in the Babli is in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Shabbat 138a" href="/Shabbat.138a"><i>Šabbat</i> 138a</a> where the argument of R. Ze`ira is quoted in his name but the introductory statement is in the name of Rav Cahana. In <i>Šabbat</i>, the original author is Samuel, not Rav. This may be a <i>lectio difficilior</i> since Rav Ḥiyya bar Ashi was a companion of Rav; but if Rav Cahana is Cahana, the stepson of Rav, it would represent a Babylonian tradition.</i> Rebbi Ze`ira, Rav Ḥiyya bar Ashi in the name of Samuel: One who filters is liable because of selecting. Rebbi Ze`ira said, it is more reasonable that it should be because of sifting. Rebbi Jonah and Rebbi Yose both said, at the start we were saying that Rebbi Ze`ira said it correctly, since as in sifting the flour is below and the farina<sup class="footnote-marker">248</sup><i class="footnote">The coarser pieces.</i> on top, so in filtering wine the wine is at the bottom and the yeast on top; but we were not saying anything. Why? Because the category of selecting was permitted, the category of filtering was permitted<sup class="footnote-marker">249</sup><i class="footnote">On a holiday, as shown later from Mishnaiot.</i>. The category of selecting was permitted: “he selects normally, on his chest, or from a pot”. Also the category of filtering was permitted, “on a holiday one puts into one which was hanging<sup class="footnote-marker">250</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Shabbat 20:1:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Shabbat.20.1.1">Mishnah <i>Šabbat</i> 20:1</a>. According to the anonymous majority on a holiday one may not put a filter on top of a barrel because this is an activity not covered by the general permission to prepare food, but if the filter already was in place one may filter wine on a holiday.</i>”. But the category of sifting was not permitted. As Rebbi Ḥanina bar Yaqe said in the name of Rav Jehudah, One does not re-sift the flour but one may pass it through the back of the sieve<sup class="footnote-marker">252</sup><i class="footnote">Filtering.</i>. If you say it is because of sifting, it<sup class="footnote-marker">253</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Megillah (Lieberman) 1:7" href="/Tosefta_Megillah_(Lieberman).1.7">Tosephta <i>Megillah</i> 1:7</a>.</i> should be forbidden. Rebbi Yose ben Rebbi Abun said, it does not follow Rebbi Jehudah, for it was stated in the name of Rebbi Jehudah, also preparations for making food are permitted<sup class="footnote-marker">253</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Megillah (Lieberman) 1:7" href="/Tosefta_Megillah_(Lieberman).1.7">Tosephta <i>Megillah</i> 1:7</a>.</i>. There is a question about the following: following the rabbis, may one re-sift the flour through the back of the sieve?

Halakhah 11

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> The House of Shammai say, on a holiday one sends only portions<sup class="footnote-marker">254</sup><i class="footnote">Either a prepared meal or a single piece of meat which can be cooked as is.</i>, but the House of Hillel say one sends domestic animals, wild animals, and birds, whether alive or slaughtered. One sends wines, oils, and flour, and legumes, but no grains<sup class="footnote-marker">255</sup><i class="footnote">Since it was stated in the previous Halakhah that grinding grains is forbidden on the holiday; therefore grains are not food available on the holiday.</i>. Rebbi Simeon permits grains<sup class="footnote-marker">256</sup><i class="footnote">Grains can be used as cold or hot cereal and also as animal feed without being ground. There is no reason not to treat them as ready food on the holiday.</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> It is difficult for the House of Shammai. A large thigh one is permitted to send, a small kid goat one is not permitted to send. Rebbi Yudan said, it only is understood if he carries it<sup class="footnote-marker">256</sup><i class="footnote">Grains can be used as cold or hot cereal and also as animal feed without being ground. There is no reason not to treat them as ready food on the holiday.</i>. But if he draws it and it walks, what is the difference if he draws it to deliver it or he draws it to let it drink? The Great Rebbi Hoshaia asked Rebbi Jehudah the Prince, did you hear from your father whether one is permitted to move a chicken<sup class="footnote-marker">257</sup><i class="footnote">If the House of Hillel do not allow an egg laid on the holiday to be moved, would it not be reasonable to conclude that a chicken raised to produce eggs cannot be moved either.</i>? Certainly if it was ready for slaughter it is permitted, otherwise it is forbidden. But are not all chickens ready for slaughter? The question is, maybe even if it is not ready<sup class="footnote-marker">258</sup><i class="footnote">If the chicken explicitly is raised to lay eggs, not to be used as meat.</i>. He told him in a whisper<sup class="footnote-marker">259</sup><i class="footnote">If it is to be a private ruling, not to be made public, it must be a permission to move such a chicken for which the prior argument was made that it may not be moved. The permission to deliver live animals on a holiday spelled out in the House of Hillel’s Mishnah is not restricted.</i>. He asked him, why are you telling me in a whisper? He said to him, just I heard it in a whisper, so I am telling you in a whisper.
It was stated<sup class="footnote-marker">260</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Beitzah (Lieberman) 1:23" href="/Tosefta_Beitzah_(Lieberman).1.23">Tosephta 1:23</a>, a different text <a class="refLink" data-ref="Beitzah 14b" href="/Beitzah.14b">Babli 14b</a>. In the Tosephta, (and the quote in <i>Or Zarua</i> II 341) instead of נדיות one reads לודיות “from Lydda”. This also would make עססיות (Tosephta spelling) a toponymic. But since the Genizah text confirms the scribe’s, it is impossible here to emend the text, in particular because the Tosephta is not certain as a Palestinian text. Here it is tentatively interpreted as from Arabic נד֗י “to be moist, covered with dew”.</i>: “One sends wheat because when crushed it is food, beans because when wet they are food, barley because it is animal feed.” Did we not state so in the name of Rebbi Simeon? <i>For all breathing</i><sup class="footnote-marker">261</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 12:16" href="/Exodus.12.16"><i>Ex.</i> 12:16</a>. The verse permits preparing food “for all breathing things” on the holiday. <i>Mekhilta Bo Pisḥa</i> 9.</i>, also breathing animals are included. The argument of Rebbi Simeon parallels that of his teacher Rebbi Aqiba. Just as Rebbi Aqiba said<sup class="footnote-marker">262</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Beitzah 21b" href="/Beitzah.21b">Babli 21b</a>.</i>, <i>for all breathing</i>, also breathing animals are included, so Rebbi Simeon said, <i>for all breathing</i>, also breathing animals are included.

Halakhah 12

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> One may send garments whether sewn or not sewn<sup class="footnote-marker">263</sup><i class="footnote">The sewn garments may be worn, the unsewn pieces of cloth may be used to cover items, in particular food.</i>, and even if they do not respond to a holiday need<sup class="footnote-marker">264</sup><i class="footnote">Not for a specified need of the holiday, only a possibility of legitimate use.</i>. But not a sandal with nails<sup class="footnote-marker">265</sup><i class="footnote">Nails in the soles, which are forbidden to wear on the Sabbath (Mishnah <i>Šabbat</i> 6:2) and holidays.</i> nor a shoe which is not sown. Rebbi Jehudah says, also no white shoe<sup class="footnote-marker">266</sup><i class="footnote">Not colored white but undyed leather, which needs expert dying.</i> because it needs a craftsman. This is the principle: Anything which one may use on the holiday one may send.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> So is the Mishnah: Because it needs the shoe-maker’s form<sup class="footnote-marker">267</sup><i class="footnote">Blackening a shoe is not really professional work; it can be done by everybody, but it needs professional tools. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Beitzah 15a" href="/Beitzah.15a">Babli 15a</a>.</i>. Is the Mishnah Rebbi Eliezer’s? As we have stated there<sup class="footnote-marker">268</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Mishnah Kelim 26:4" href="/Mishnah_Kelim.26.4">Mishnah <i>Kelim</i> 26:4</a>.</i>, “the shoe on the shoe-maker’s last, Rebbi Eliezer declares pure but the Sages declare impure<sup class="footnote-marker">269</sup><i class="footnote">In this context, “pure” means “impervious to impurity”, “impure” means “possibly impure”. For R. Eliezer a shoe still on the last is not an implement, therefore it does not fall under any of the categories of things that may become impure. For the Sages, the moment the shoe is fully sewn and may be worn immediately after being removed from the last, it is a shoe and therefore subject to impurity. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Shabbat 141b" href="/Shabbat.141b">Babli <i>Šabbat</i> 141b</a>.</i>.” It is the opinion of everybody. It is different here since he is like finishing an implement on the holiday<sup class="footnote-marker">270</sup><i class="footnote">In the case of the Mishnah, while a person may be embarrassed when wearing a shoe with leather in its natural color, the shoe is not less wearable than a regularly dyed one. Therefore blackening the shoe is not really “hitting with a hammer”, the biblically prohibited action on the Sabbath and holidays of turning a work-piece into an implement, but it looks like it and therefore must be rabbinically forbidden.</i>. It was stated<sup class="footnote-marker">271</sup><i class="footnote">Since on the intermediate days one may prepare clothing for immediate use.</i>: One does not dismantle a shoe from the last on the holiday, but one dismantles a shoe from the last on the intermediate days of the holiday<sup class="footnote-marker">271</sup><i class="footnote">Since on the intermediate days one may prepare clothing for immediate use.</i>.
May one make cheese from milk on the holiday<sup class="footnote-marker">272</sup><i class="footnote">There can be no biblical prohibition to make soft cheese which can be used on the same day it is made, since this is no different from cooking a dish, presuming the milk was available from before the holiday. The prohibition is purely rabbinical. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Shabbat 95a" href="/Shabbat.95a">Babli <i>Šabbat</i> 95a</a>.</i>? If you are saying so, he will milk and make cheese on the holiday for the workday.
<sup class="footnote-marker">273</sup><i class="footnote">This text is copied in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Shabbat 6:1:7" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Shabbat.6.1.7"><i>Šabbat</i> 6 (Notes 60–65</a>).</i> Rebbi Ḥalaphta ben Shaul stated: It is forbidden to send jewelry. Rebbi Mana said, they said only to send, therefore to wear is permitted. It was stated: “One may move the <i>shofar</i><sup class="footnote-marker">274</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Shabbat (Lieberman) 13:16" href="/Tosefta_Shabbat_(Lieberman).13.16">Tosephta <i>Šabbat</i> (13:16</a> ed. Liebermann) The ram’s horn. As a musical instrument it cannot be used on a holiday other than New Year’s Day. Since writing is forbidden on the holiday, a writing tablet cannot be used. The mirror is discussed in <i>Šabbat</i> 6. The Tosephta applies to the Sabbath as well as to holidays since one speaks of moving in a private domain (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Shabbat 35b-36a" href="/Shabbat.35b-36a">Babli <i>Šabbat</i> 35b/36a</a>).</i> to let a child drink, the writing tablet, and the bell, and the mirror, to cover vessels.” Rebbi Abun said, a Mishnah says that it is forbidden to wear it, as we have stated: “Anything which one may use on the holiday one may send.” If you say that it is permitted to wear it should be permitted to send. And what is that which was stated: “One may move the <i>shofar</i> to let a child drink, the writing tablet, and the bell, and the mirror, to cover vessels”? If they are called implements<sup class="footnote-marker">275</sup><i class="footnote">They must have permitted use. For example, the <i>shofar</i> must have been used on a preceding weekday as bottle for a toddler; the other things mentioned must have been used as covers.</i>. So far gold jewelry. Also even silver jewelry? They said in the name of Rebbi Jeremiah forbidden, and they said in the name of Rebbi Jeremiah permitted. Rebbi Ḥizqiah said, I know the beginning and the end. Small girls were growing up in Rebbi Jeremiah’s dwelling. He went and asked Rebbi Ze`ira, who told him: do not forbid and do not permit<sup class="footnote-marker">276</sup><i class="footnote">Since in principle silver jewelry is forbidden to wear on a holiday as much as gold jewelry, he cannot permit. But since the girls would not listen to him if he would forbid, he should not prohibit. Since this is a rabbinic prohibition only, it is better that people should be ignorant of the prohibition than violate it knowingly.</i>.

Chapter 2



Halakhah 1

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> If the holiday falls on a Friday a person may not cook on the holiday especially for the Sabbath<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">Since the sanctity of the holiday is distinct from that of the Sabbath, the permission to prepare food on a holiday does not imply to prepare food for a different day.</i> but he may cook for the holiday and if he left over he left over; or<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">This is the preferred way and is called <i>eruv tavshilin</i>, “and <i>eruv</i> for dishes.” One cooks for the Sabbath on Wednesday or Thursday, and declares that the dish is cooked especially for the Sabbath. Since the preceding sentence had implied that even without the <i>eruv</i> there is a possibility to cook for the Sabbath on the holiday, the prohibition can be only rabbinic in character and can be removed by the rabbinic institution of the <i>eruv</i>.</i> he cooks a dish before the holiday and relies on it for the Sabbath. The House of Shammai say two dishes, but the House of Hillel say one dish.<br>But they agree about a fish and the egg on it<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">According to most early commentators, this refers to a raw egg smeared on the outside of the fish as coating and then fried. Since this takes only a short time, it is the preferred dish for <i>eruv tavshilin</i> to the extent that “fish with egg” may be used as synonym for <i>eruv tavshilin</i>.</i> that they are two dishes. If he ate it or it was lost, he may not start cooking based on it, but if even a minute amount was left<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">This implies that no minimal amount for the validity of <i>eruv tavshilin</i> is established. (But cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Beitzah 2:1:7" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Beitzah.2.1.7">Note 34</a>.)</i>, he relies on it for the Sabbath.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “If the holiday falls on a Friday,” etc. It is written<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 16:23" href="/Exodus.16.23"><i>Ex.</i> 16:23</a>. The simple translation of the verse would be: <i>Bake what you want to bake, and cook what you want to cook</i>.</i>, <i>what you baked bake, and what you cooked cook</i>. Rebbi Eliezer says, one bakes on the basis of the baked, and one cooks on the basis of the cooked<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Mekhilta dR. Ismael Beshallaḥ Wayassa` Parashah</i> 4. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Beitzah 15b" href="/Beitzah.15b">Babli 15b</a>; partially <i>Mekhilta dR. Simeon ben Yoḥai</i> p. 113.<br>As explained later, the argument presupposes that the basic rules of the Sabbath had not been explained on the occasion of the Manna but earlier at Mara where it is said (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 15:25" href="/Exodus.15.25"><i>Ex.</i> 15:25</a>): <i>There He gave him law and rules and there He tried him</i> (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Sanhedrin 56b" href="/Sanhedrin.56b">Babli <i>Sanhedrin</i> 56b</a>). In that case the rules given on the first Sabbath with the manna may apply to a special case, which is interpreted that of a holiday falling on a Friday, and R. Eliezer reads the verse as “if you baked for the Sabbath before the holiday, you may bake on the holiday for the Sabbath; if you cooked for the Sabbath before the holiday, you may cook on the holiday for the Sabbath;” explaining the position of the House of Shammai. R. Joshua, the exponent of the House of Hillel, reads, “if either you baked or cooked for the Sabbath before the start of the holiday, you may bake or cook on the holiday for the Sabbath.”</i>. Rebbi Joshua says, one bakes and cooks on the basis of the cooked. What is the reason of Rebbi Eliezer? <i>What you baked bake, and what you cooked cook</i>. What is the reason of Rebbi Joshua? <i>What you will bake and what you will cook cook</i>. Rebbi Eliezer<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">Either “R. Eliezer” is a misreading for “R. Eleazar” (the Amora) or, more likely, a word is missing and one has to understand “the argument of R. Eliezer (and R. Joshua) presupposes that the rules of the Sabbath were given at Mara.”</i> said, this follows him who said that the Sabbath was given at Mara. But following him who said that at Alush<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote">Which according to <a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 33:13" href="/Numbers.33.13"><i>Num</i>. 33:13</a> was the station just preceding Rephidim which in <i>Ex.</i> is mentioned as the next resting place after the introduction of the manna.</i> the Sabbath was given, they are at Alush and the warning is given at Alush<sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote">If <a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 16:23" href="/Exodus.16.23"><i>Ex.</i> 16:23</a> is the first explanation of the rules of the Sabbath, it must refer to a common Sabbath and not the rare case of a Sabbath preceded by a holiday. In that case the verse must be read as in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Beitzah 2:1:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Beitzah.2.1.1">Note 3</a>.</i>. Come and look, from the Torah it is forbidden and an <i>eruv tavshilin</i> makes it permitted<sup class="footnote-marker">10</sup><i class="footnote">If one accepts that the rules of the Sabbath were given at Alush one is forced to accept that one may not prepare on any day something for a day of lesser holiness, but may prepare for a day of greater holiness. Then by biblical standards one is forbidden on a holiday to prepare for a weekday but is permitted to prepare for the Sabbath, and the requirement of an <i>eruv tavshilin</i> is a restriction, not a leniency. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Pesachim 46b" href="/Pesachim.46b">Babli <i>Pesaḥim</i> 46b</a>.</i>? Rebbi Abbahu said, it would be logical that one should bake and cook on the holiday for the Sabbath<sup class="footnote-marker">11</sup><i class="footnote">The <i>eruv tavshilin</i> is a rabbinic “fence around the Law.”</i>. If you would say so, one would bake and cook on the holiday for a weekday<sup class="footnote-marker">12</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Shabbat 15:1:2-3:6" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Shabbat.15.1.2-3.6">Mishnah <i>Šabbat</i> 15:3</a>. An action permitted on the Sabbath itself then cannot be forbidden in preparation for the Sabbath.</i>. Come and look, one makes the beds on the holiday for the Sabbath, but one does not bake and cook on the holiday for the Sabbath. Rebbi Ila said, why may one make the beds on the holiday for the Sabbath? Since one may make the beds on Friday night for the Sabbath day<sup class="footnote-marker">13</sup><i class="footnote">Since it is not obvious that preparing food on the holiday for the Sabbath is permitted, the institution of the <i>eruv tavshilin</i> is justified.</i>. Could one bake and cook on the holiday for the Sabbath? One may not bake and cook on the night of the Sabbath for the Sabbath day<sup class="footnote-marker">12</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Shabbat 15:1:2-3:6" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Shabbat.15.1.2-3.6">Mishnah <i>Šabbat</i> 15:3</a>. An action permitted on the Sabbath itself then cannot be forbidden in preparation for the Sabbath.</i>.
Rebbi Cahana the son of Rebbi Ḥiyya bar Abba said, on condition that he not act craftily<sup class="footnote-marker">14</sup><i class="footnote">This does not refer to the discussion of the <i>eruv tavshilin</i> but to the statement in the Mishnah that a person without an <i>eruv</i> may cook more than his immediate needs are may keep the excess for the Sabbath. It is required that in that case the meal be cooked for an actual need of the holiday; when the quantities may be increased. One may not invent an imaginary need for this purpose. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Beitzah 17b" href="/Beitzah.17b">Babli 17b</a>.</i>. <sup class="footnote-marker">15</sup><i class="footnote">The following text is also in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Challah 1:5:7" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Challah.1.5.7"><i>Ḥallah</i> 1:9, Notes 215–217</a>.</i> The Mishnah follows Rebbi Simeon ben Eleazar as it was stated: “One does not make {food} on a holiday for use after the holiday.” Also, it was stated<sup class="footnote-marker">16</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Beitzah (Lieberman) 2:5" href="/Tosefta_Beitzah_(Lieberman).2.5">Tosephta 2:5</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Beitzah 17a" href="/Beitzah.17a">Babli 17a</a>.</i>: “A woman may fill a pot with meat even if one eats only one piece from it, a kettle<sup class="footnote-marker">17</sup><i class="footnote">Latin <i>cocuma, -ae</i>.</i> with hot water even if one drinks only one cup from it. But baking, she should bake only what she needs.” For it is stated<sup class="footnote-marker">18</sup><i class="footnote">Since neither the Mishnah nor R. Cahana in his interpretation of the Mishnah exclude baking bread from the general permission; it must follow R. Simeon ben Eleazar. Since the Mishnah is anonymous, it must be presumed to be teaching to be followed.</i>: “Rebbi Simeon ben Eleazar says, a woman may fill the entire oven with bread because the bread turns out well if the oven is filled.<sup class="footnote-marker">16</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Beitzah (Lieberman) 2:5" href="/Tosefta_Beitzah_(Lieberman).2.5">Tosephta 2:5</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Beitzah 17a" href="/Beitzah.17a">Babli 17a</a>.</i>” Rav Ḥuna preached following Rebbi Simeon ben Eleazar. Rebbi Joḥanan preached to the Tiberians following that of Rebbi Simeon ben Eleazar. He preached this as practice but they took it as to act on<sup class="footnote-marker">19</sup><i class="footnote">In this context, “practice” means a valid opinion in the sense that anybody who follows it cannot be accused of acting illegally. “To act on” is the same as the expression “to instruct”, representing best practice, and one which should be recommended by the rabbi to anybody asking about such a situation.</i>. Some wanted to say that he preached to act on. They came and asked him and he preached as practice, and they preached again but we do not know whether as practice or to act on. Rebbi Abba bar Zavdi said, Rebbi Abbahu instructed<sup class="footnote-marker">20</sup><i class="footnote">While we do not know R. Joḥanan’s exact position, the endorsements by Rav Ḥuna and R. Abbahu are enough to validate the opinion of R. Simeon ben Eleazar as practice to be recommended. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Beitzah 17a" href="/Beitzah.17a">Babli 17a</a>.</i> following Rebbi Simeon ben Eleazar.
Rebbi Ḥiyya in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan: He has to say, for me and for him who made no <i>eruv</i><sup class="footnote-marker">21</sup><i class="footnote">It is not quite clear what this means. In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Beitzah 16b" href="/Beitzah.16b">Babli 16b</a> it is spelled out that the rabbi of a congregation makes his <i>eruv</i> and invites every member of his congregation to prepare food based on his <i>eruv</i>. But the following discussion indicates that the Yerushalmi disagrees with the Babli, that nobody may cook for the Sabbath on a holiday which falls on a Friday without his own <i>eruv</i>. The statement of R. Ḥiyya (bar Abba) then means that one makes the <i>eruv</i> for himself and for permission to cook another person’s meal in preparation for the Sabbath.</i>. If somebody made no <i>eruv</i>, others who made an <i>eruv</i> are allowed to prepare for him from his own {provisions}. Rebbi Zeriqan in the name of Rebbi Ze`ura: Only if he transfers his property to them, for otherwise it would turn out that they prepare for him from his own. If somebody did not make an <i>eruv</i> and neither did others, he may fry a fish<sup class="footnote-marker">22</sup><i class="footnote">Which is done very quickly. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Beitzah 22a" href="/Beitzah.22a">Babli 22a</a>.</i>. Rav Ḥuna said, he may heat hot water. Samuel said, he may light a candle<sup class="footnote-marker">23</sup><i class="footnote">In theory, if we hold that the sanctity of the holiday implies that one may not prepare from the holiday to the next day, even if that be a Sabbath, one could not light a candle for Friday evening and would have to sit in the dark for the Friday evening meal. This in itself may be taken as proof that by biblical standards one may prepare and that the requirement of <i>eruv</i> is a rabbinic restriction.</i>. Rebbi Ḥiyya stated<sup class="footnote-marker">24</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Beitzah (Lieberman) 2:3" href="/Tosefta_Beitzah_(Lieberman).2.3">Tosephta 2:3</a>. All parallel sources confirm that the corrector’s version is a required correction of a scribal error.</i>, “he fills an amphora of (wine) [water] and lights his candle”. “It happened that somebody put a vow on his son to study Torah<sup class="footnote-marker">25</sup><i class="footnote">He made a vow not to profit from anything his son does until the latter receives a rabbinic degree.</i>. The case came before Rebbi Yose ben Ḥalaphta who permitted him to fill him an amphora with water and to light the candle for him<sup class="footnote-marker">26</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Bekhorot 6:3" href="/Tosefta_Bekhorot.6.3">Tosephta <i>Bekhorot</i> 6:11</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Bikkurim 3:6:3" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Bikkurim.3.6.3"><i>Bikkurim</i> 3:7 Note 102</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Nedarim 38b" href="/Nedarim.38b">Babli <i>Nedarim</i> 38b</a>. These are not considered work subject to separate remuneration.</i>.”
Rebbi Ḥiyya the Elder came to his house<sup class="footnote-marker">27</sup><i class="footnote">He came back from evening service in the synagogue on a Thursday evening which was the start of a holiday.</i>. They told him, we forgot to make <i>eruv</i>. He asked them, are there peas left from yesterday<sup class="footnote-marker">28</sup><i class="footnote">Since the dish could be eaten on the Sabbath, he permitted it to be used as <i>eruv</i>.</i>? They said to him, yes. Does this imply, even one kind? It contained spinach. Does this imply even if it is less than the volume of an olive? It was a full measure. Does that imply that it does not need a declaration? Rav Ḥisda said, the Great Rebbi Ḥiyya had made a stipulation<sup class="footnote-marker">29</sup><i class="footnote">Since he had planned to come home only in the evening, he already made the declaration for the future <i>eruv</i> before he left. The only implication one can draw from this story is that such a stipulation is valid.</i>.
“<sup class="footnote-marker">30</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Beitzah (Lieberman) 2:4" href="/Tosefta_Beitzah_(Lieberman).2.4">Tosephta 2:4</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Beitzah 17b" href="/Beitzah.17b">Babli 17b</a>.</i> It was stated: Rebbi Simeon ben Eleazar said, the House of Shammai and the House of Hillel agree that there are two dishes. Where do they disagree? About the fish and the egg on it<sup class="footnote-marker">31</sup><i class="footnote">Cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Beitzah 2:1:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Beitzah.2.1.1">Note 3</a>.</i>, where the House of Shammai are saying it is one dish while the House of Hillel are saying they are two dishes. Everybody agrees that if he applied the egg on salted fish<sup class="footnote-marker">32</sup><i class="footnote">A boiled egg is used to fill a salted herring. This is easily recognized as two dishes.<br>The expression פרפר is that of the corrector, following Babli and the Tosephta. It is no longer recognizable what was the original word written by the scribe.</i>, or cut leeks under the fish, or cooked two kinds in a pot, that they are counted as two dishes.” And practice follows the student<sup class="footnote-marker">33</sup><i class="footnote">In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Beitzah 17b" href="/Beitzah.17b">Babli 17b</a> practice is decided as following the Mishnah according to the House of Hillel, i. e., that only one dish is needed for <i>eruv</i>. Since the anonymous Mishnah is supposed to be Rebbi Meïr’s, and R. Simeon ben Eleazar everywhere appears as student of Rebbi Meïr (and colleague of Rebbi), the Yerushalmi decides that practice follows the Tosephta against the Mishnah.</i>.
Rav said, I am saying not less than the volume of an olive<sup class="footnote-marker">34</sup><i class="footnote">A valid <i>eruv tavshilin</i> must have a minimal volume of an olive.</i>. Does this not disagree with Rebbi Joḥanan, since Rebbi Joḥanan said, for me and for him who did not make <i>eruv</i><sup class="footnote-marker">35</sup><i class="footnote">If an <i>eruv tavshilin</i> is made for an indeterminate number of persons, according to Rav it should need an indeterminate volume. This differs from the statement in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Beitzah 16b" href="/Beitzah.16b">Babli 16b</a> where Rav requires the volume of an olive “whether for one person or for a hundred.” Cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Beitzah 2:1:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Beitzah.2.1.1">Note 4</a>.</i>? A <i>baraita</i> supports Rebbi Joḥanan: An <i>eruv</i> needs a measure neither at the start nor at the end.<sup class="footnote-marker">36</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Beitzah 16b" href="/Beitzah.16b">Babli 16b</a>. The start is when it is made and the end the time it is eaten on the Sabbath.</i> <sup class="footnote-marker">37</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Beitzah (Lieberman) 2:1" href="/Tosefta_Beitzah_(Lieberman).2.1">Tosephta 2:1</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Beitzah 16b" href="/Beitzah.16b">Babli 16b</a>.</i>“On a holiday which falls on a Friday one makes <i>eruv</i> neither for courtyards nor for domains, the words of Rebbi Meïr<sup class="footnote-marker">38</sup><i class="footnote">In the Tosephta, the word Meïr is missing. In the Babli, the opinion attributed here to R. Meïr is anonymous, the one of the Sages is Rebbi’s.</i>. But the Sages are saying, one makes <i>eruv</i> for courtyards but does not make <i>eruv</i> for domains”. One makes <i>eruv</i> for courtyards, because it allows something which is allowed to him<sup class="footnote-marker">39</sup><i class="footnote">Since on a holiday one is permitted to carry from one domain to the other, one is permitted to use food prepared on the holiday to make an <i>eruv ḥaṣerot</i>, to be consumed on the Sabbath and to permit carrying from courtyard to courtyard in a dead-end alley.</i>, but does not make <i>eruv</i> for domains, because you would permit him something which is prohibited to him<sup class="footnote-marker">40</sup><i class="footnote">Since on the holiday one is not permitted to leave his Sabbath domain, the definition of that domain may not be changed on the holiday.</i>. There are Tannaim who switch. Rebbi Eleazar said, since there are Tannaim who switch, we have to take notice<sup class="footnote-marker">41</sup><i class="footnote">And in effect follow R. Meïr even according to the Sages.</i>. Rebbi Jeremiah in the name of Rebbi Ze`ura: He who wants to do it right makes the <i>eruv</i> the day before<sup class="footnote-marker">42</sup><i class="footnote">Everybody agrees that making all kinds of <i>eruv</i> on a weekday is preferred and unproblematic.</i>. Rebbi Yudan said, what you are saying is when he did not make <i>eruv tavshilin</i>, but if he made <i>eruv tavshilin</i> it is permitted. Rebbi Yose ben Rebbi Abun asked, does <i>eruv tavshilin</i> permit dividers<sup class="footnote-marker">43</sup><i class="footnote">If there is no <i>eruv tavshilin</i>, everybody agrees that no other <i>eruv</i> can be made on the holiday. If there is an <i>eruv tavshilin</i>, the argument of <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Beitzah 2:1:7" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Beitzah.2.1.7">Note 39</a> will permit making <i>eruv ḥaṣerot</i> on a holiday. What R. Yudan said, cannot possibly include <i>eruv teḥumin</i>, since an <i>eruv tavshilin</i> cannot move boundaries. R. Yudan’s statement only confirms the statement of the Sages in the formulation given in the text.</i>?

Halakhah 2

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> If it falls after the Sabbath, the House of Shammai say that one immerses everything before the start of the Sabbath, but the House of Hillel say implements before the Sabbath<sup class="footnote-marker">44</sup><i class="footnote">Since the essence of a holiday is the pilgrimage to the Temple, even in absence of the Temple there is an obligation to be pure on the holiday. This includes household implements to be used on the holiday; in Temple times obligatory for everything connected with the preparation and consumption of food.</i> and humans on the Sabbath<sup class="footnote-marker">45</sup><i class="footnote">Since in principle immersion in a <i>miqweh</i> is permitted on the Sabbath, it is permitted in this particular case.</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> The Mishnah is about large implements, but for small implements one may use a trick and immerse them. Rebbi Hoshaya stated, a person may fill am impure vessel from the cistern, and be tricky about it and immerse it<sup class="footnote-marker">46</sup><i class="footnote">The problem is not the immersion; if a vessel is used to draw water from the cistern and is totally immersed in the water, the technical aspects of the immersion are satisfied without any infringement of Sabbath or holiday rules. The problem is that a purifying immersion needs intent, and this is possible on the Sabbath or holiday only by treating the purification as an after-thought. One may not immerse the pail in water in order to purify it, but one may immerse it to draw water and when it is immersed say, since already it is immersed it also shall be purified by this immersion. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Beitzah 18b" href="/Beitzah.18b">Babli 18b</a>.</i>. If his pail fell into the cistern, his vessel fell into the cistern, he may be tricky about it and immerse it. Two Amoraim, one said about implements impure in original purity<sup class="footnote-marker">47</sup><i class="footnote">Purity by biblical standards.</i>; the other said, about implements impure in derivative impurity. The one who said impure in derivative impurity objected to the one who said impure in original purity, even on a weekday would it not need sundown<sup class="footnote-marker">48</sup><i class="footnote">By biblical standards, while after immersion in water an implement is no longer impure, it becomes ritually pure only after the next sundown (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 22:6" href="/Leviticus.22.6"><i>Lev.</i> 22:6</a>.) Since sundown marks the end of the holiday, the immersion would be too late to make the vessel usable on the holiday.</i>? He said to him, about one who wants to eat his profane food in purity<sup class="footnote-marker">49</sup><i class="footnote">Then the vessel can not be impure, but it does not have to be pure.</i>.
All who are obligated to immerse themselves immerse themselves normally on the Ninth of Av and on the Day of Atonement<sup class="footnote-marker">50</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Beitzah 18b" href="/Beitzah.18b">Babli 18b</a>, <i>Ta`anit</i> 13a. In most other Yerushalmi sources one reads; “Those who are obliged to immerse themselves immerse themselves normally in secret on the Day of Atonement” (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Berakhot 3:4:5" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Berakhot.3.4.5"><i>Berakhot</i> 3:4 Note 167</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Yoma 8:1:10" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Yoma.8.1.10"><i>Yoma</i> 8:1 Note 44</a>, <i>Ta`anit</i> 1:6 64dl.4). For the Day of Atonement (without the remark “in secret”) the statement is in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Yoma (Lieberman) 4:5" href="/Tosefta_Yoma_(Lieberman).4.5">Tosephta <i>Yoma</i> 4:5</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Yoma 88a" href="/Yoma.88a">Babli<i>Yoma</i> 88a</a>.</i>. Rebbi Ḥanina the second-in-command of the Cohanim said, Our God’s House is worth that the Cohanim should lose one immersion because of it. Rebbi Levi preached according to the statement of Rebbi Ḥanina the second-in-command of the Cohanim<sup class="footnote-marker">51</sup><i class="footnote">Cohanim are forbidden to immerse themselves on the 9th of Av, and therefore are unable to eat heave at the end of the fast, but women who are obligated have to immerse themselves.</i>.
“And humans on the Sabbath<sup class="footnote-marker">45</sup><i class="footnote">Since in principle immersion in a <i>miqweh</i> is permitted on the Sabbath, it is permitted in this particular case.</i>.” Since a man after an emission of semen immerses himself on the Sabbath.

Halakhah 3

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> Also they agree that one lets the water in a stone vessel touch but not immerse<sup class="footnote-marker">51*</sup><i class="footnote">If one has impure water one may put it into a stone vessel which is impervious to impurity and immerse it in a <i>miqweh</i>. When the surface of the water in the vessel comes into contact with the water of the <i>miqweh</i>, it becomes part of the <i>miqweh</i> (of at least 40 <i>seah</i> of water) and therefore is pure. If then the vessel is removed from the <i>miqweh</i> it is filled with pure water even though it practically is unchanged. One cannot do that with a ceramics vessel since ceramics are not purified by immersion in a <i>miqweh</i>; the water after removal from the <i>miqweh</i> would be made impure by the vessel as before. One is not permitted to use a wooden or metal vessel, since then the dipping of the vessel into the <i>miqweh</i> would not be for touching the water but for immersion of the vessel and this is forbidden by the Mishnah.</i>. One immerses from stage to stage and from group to group on the holiday<sup class="footnote-marker">52</sup><i class="footnote">Since immersion for purification needs intent (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Beitzah 2:2:2" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Beitzah.2.2.2">Note 46</a>), the effect of the purification depends on the intent. If he immersed his vessel with the intent to eat profane food in purity and then changed his mind to eat heave, he has to re-immerse his vessel for the newly intended use (since profane food admits two stages of derivative impurity but heave three.) Since the vessel was pure at the start and will be pure at the end, the procedure of purifying “from stage to stage” is possible on the holiday. Similarly, if he wanted to eat with a group eating profane food in purity but then changed his mind to eat with a group consuming Second Tithe, profane food to be consumed under the rules of purity applying to heave and <i>ḥallah</i>, a new immersion “from group to group” is needed as in the previous case.<br>In the independent Mishnah mss. this is <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Beitzah 2:2:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Beitzah.2.2.1">Mishnah 3</a>, in Maimonides’s autograph Mishnah part of <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Beitzah 2:1:1-7" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Beitzah.2.1.1-7">Mishnah 2</a>.</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> The Mishnah does not follow Rebbi, as it was stated<sup class="footnote-marker">53</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Beitzah (Lieberman) 2:9" href="/Tosefta_Beitzah_(Lieberman).2.9">Tosephta 2:9</a>, <i>Šabbat</i> 16:11 (ed. Liebermann). <a class="refLink" data-ref="Beitzah 18b" href="/Beitzah.18b">Babli 18b</a>.</i>: “One does not immerse from stage to stage on the holiday and one does not let the water touch in a stone vessel to purify it on the Sabbath, the word of Rebbi. But the Sages say one immerses from stage to stage on the holiday and one lets the water touch in a stone vessel in purity on the Sabbath.” It only said, in a stone vessel. Therefore not in a wooden vessel<sup class="footnote-marker">51</sup><i class="footnote">Cohanim are forbidden to immerse themselves on the 9th of Av, and therefore are unable to eat heave at the end of the fast, but women who are obligated have to immerse themselves.</i>.
How is from stage to stage? If he wants to work his dough but then his wine press, or his wine press and then his dough; he has to immerse his implements<sup class="footnote-marker">54</sup><i class="footnote">If he first intended the vessels for his dough to separate <i>ḥallah</i> in purity but then changed his mind to press his grapes in the purity of sacrifices, he has to re-immerse his vessels. The same holds if he wanted his wine to be pure and profane and then decided to use his vessels for dough made in the purity of <i>ḥallah</i>.</i>. How is from group to group? He was eating with one group and then changed his mind to eat with another group, then he has to immerse<sup class="footnote-marker">52</sup><i class="footnote">Since immersion for purification needs intent (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Beitzah 2:2:2" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Beitzah.2.2.2">Note 46</a>), the effect of the purification depends on the intent. If he immersed his vessel with the intent to eat profane food in purity and then changed his mind to eat heave, he has to re-immerse his vessel for the newly intended use (since profane food admits two stages of derivative impurity but heave three.) Since the vessel was pure at the start and will be pure at the end, the procedure of purifying “from stage to stage” is possible on the holiday. Similarly, if he wanted to eat with a group eating profane food in purity but then changed his mind to eat with a group consuming Second Tithe, profane food to be consumed under the rules of purity applying to heave and <i>ḥallah</i>, a new immersion “from group to group” is needed as in the previous case.<br>In the independent Mishnah mss. this is <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Beitzah 2:2:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Beitzah.2.2.1">Mishnah 3</a>, in Maimonides’s autograph Mishnah part of <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Beitzah 2:1:1-7" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Beitzah.2.1.1-7">Mishnah 2</a>.</i>.

Halakhah 4

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> The House of Shammai say, one brings well-being offerings without leaning on them, but not elevation offerings; but the House of Hillel say one brings both well-being and elevation offerings and one leans on them<sup class="footnote-marker">55</sup><i class="footnote">The pilgrimage to the Temple requires that the worshipper bring both an elevation offering for his appearance in the Temple and a well-being offering for the family celebration (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Chagigah 1:2:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Chagigah.1.2.1">Mishnah <i>Ḥagigah</i> 1:2</a>). Both an elevation offering (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 1:4" href="/Leviticus.1.4"><i>Lev.</i> 1:4</a>) and a well-being offering (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 3:2" href="/Leviticus.3.2"><i>Lev.</i> 3:2</a>) require that the offerer lean on the head of the sacrifice. The House of Shammai say that on the holiday one may bring a well-being offering since it is the basis of a family meal and therefore appropriate for the holiday, but do not allow for the leaning since the leaning has to be done as hard as possible, which they declare is using the animal comparable to riding it, which is rabbinically forbidden on the holiday. Since by biblical standards the sacrifice cannot be brought without being leaned on, they require the leaning being done before the holiday (after the dedication of the animal as sacrifice and in the Temple precinct.) They do not permit elevation offerings on the holiday itself since it is completely burned and does not represent food. The House of Hillel hold that both kinds of sacrifices are biblically required for holidays of pilgrimage and therefore cannot be excluded on the holiday itself. For them the biblical requirement of leaning-on overrides rabbinical restrictions.</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> <sup class="footnote-marker">56</sup><i class="footnote">This Halakhah also appears as <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Chagigah 2:3:2-5" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Chagigah.2.3.2-5"><i>Ḥagigah</i>2:3</a> (<b>ח</b>).</i> The House of Shammai say, irregular leaning-on was permitted. But the House of Hillel say, irregular leaning-on was not permitted. What is irregular leaning-on? From the day before. Rebbi Ze`ira said, everybody agrees that he did not fulfill his obligation regarding the reparation offering of a sufferer from skin disease if he leaned on the day before<sup class="footnote-marker">57</sup><i class="footnote">While the ritual of rehabilitating the healed sufferer from skin disease nowhere explicitly requires leaning on the sacrifice, it is written there (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 14:13" href="/Leviticus.14.13"><i>Lev.</i> 14:13</a>) that “the reparation sacrifice follows the rules of the purification sacrifice”, and for the latter (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 4" href="/Leviticus.4"><i>Lev.</i> 4</a>) it is written repeatedly “he leans-on and he slaughters”, implying first that leaning on is required and second that the two actions have to be close in time.</i>; he fulfilled his obligation with voluntary well-being offerings for which he leaned on the day before<sup class="footnote-marker">58</sup><i class="footnote">Since these are sacrifices in satisfaction of a voluntary vow, no fixed time table is given here.</i>. Where do they disagree? The festival well-being offerings<sup class="footnote-marker">59</sup><i class="footnote">These are religious obligations, not voluntary vows. As well-being offerings they have no fixed time table (but they cannot be brought before the holiday), as obligatory offerings at fixed times, on the holidays of pilgrimage, they are more like the offering of the healed sufferer from skin disease whose sacrifice is required on the eighth day of his procedure.</i>. The House of Shammai treat them like voluntary well-being offerings; the House of Hillel treat them like the reparation offering of a sufferer from skin disease. Rebbi Yasa said, that what you are saying, he did not fulfill his obligation regarding the reparation offering of a sufferer from skin disease if he leaned on the day before, {only} on time. If its time has passed it becomes like voluntary well-being offerings<sup class="footnote-marker">60</sup><i class="footnote">If the eighth day has passed, the sacrifice can be brought at any time. This emphasizes that the essential point in the disagreement is the role of the fixed time frame for the festival offerings.</i>.
<sup class="footnote-marker">61</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Chagigah (Lieberman) 2:10" href="/Tosefta_Chagigah_(Lieberman).2.10">Tosephta <i>Ḥagigah</i> 2:10</a>; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Chagigah 20b" href="/Chagigah.20b">Babli <i>Ḥagigah</i>20b</a>.</i>“The House of Hillel said to the House of Shammai: Since at a time when I forbid to the private person<sup class="footnote-marker">62</sup><i class="footnote">Greek ʼιδιώτης.</i> I am permitting it for Heaven, when it is permitted for the private person is it not logical that it be permitted for Heaven? The House of Shammai said to them, vows and voluntary gifts are proof, since they are permitted to the private person but forbidden to Heaven. The House of Hillel said to them, no. If you are speaking about vows and voluntary gifts which have no fixed time, what does this imply for the festival offering which has a fixed time? The House of Shammai answered them, the festival offering has no fixed time since if he did not offer it on the first day, he may offer it on the second day, if he did not offer it on the second day, he may offer it on the third day. The House of Hillel told them, the festival offering has a fixed time, for if he did not offer it on the holiday of pilgrimage he may not offer it after the holiday. The House of Shammai said to them, is it not already said: <i>only what can be eaten by any person this alone may be made for you</i><sup class="footnote-marker">63</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 12:16" href="/Exodus.12.16"><i>Ex.</i> 12:16</a>.</i>? The House of Hillel answered them, is that a proof? <i>For you</i> it may not be made, [but] it is made for Heaven. Abba Shaul says it was based on another reason: Since when your stove is closed down, your Master’s stove is open, when your stove is open it only is logical that your Master’s stove should be open. Another explanation: It is not in order that your table should be full but the table of your Creator be empty.<sup class="footnote-marker">64</sup><i class="footnote">While there are two disputes between the Houses of Hillel and Shammai, about leaning on animals and about bringing elevation offerings on a holiday proper, it is clear that once it is decided that elevation offerings can be brought with the required leaning-on, well-being offerings also may be brought with all required ceremonies. Therefore the argument reduces to one about the possibility of offering private elevation offerings on a full holiday. The first argument of the House of Hillel is that on the Sabbath, when all cooking or other use of fire is forbidden to humans, the Temple altar is in full action, burning two daily and two <i>musaf</i> offerings. Therefore on a holiday when cooking is permitted, it must be possible for private persons to offer sacrifices to be burned on the altar. The argument is refuted by the House of Shammai since it is agreed that sacrifices not brought as festival offerings but in fulfillment of a vow (either as “vow”, i. e., a promise of <i>an</i> animal, or as “voluntary offering”, a promise of <i>this</i> animal) cannot be brought on the full holiday but only on the intermediate days (or for Pentecost, the week following.)
