The House of Hillel then point out that vows may be brought any time during the year, it only is a convenience to bring them when one has to visit the Temple anyhow; but festival offerings which are tied to the festival must be brought on the festival. The House of Shammai answer that the festival offerings may be brought on the intermediate days (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Chagigah 1:6:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Chagigah.1.6.1">Mishnah <i>Ḥagigah</i> 1:6</a>), when all work is permitted; but the House of Hillel retort that still after the end of the holiday period there is no possibility of making up for a missed holiday sacrifice. The inconclusive discussion is cut short by Abba Shaul and the “other explanation”, that the position of the House of Shammai leads to the Temple being empty of people on full holidays, crowded on the intermediate days, whereas Pentateuch, Prophets, and Psalms all indicate that the essence of a holiday of pilgrimage is that the Temple precinct be filled to capacity, and that therefore practice must follow the House of Hillel, as explained in the last paragraph of the Halakhah.</i>”
Rebbi Yose ben Rebbi Abun said, Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish was passing by the school when he heard them reciting the verse<sup class="footnote-marker">65</sup><i class="footnote">1<i>Chr</i>. 29:21.</i>, <i>they sacrificed animal sacrifices and brought elevation sacrifices the next day</i>. He said, he who interrupts follows the House of Shammai<sup class="footnote-marker">66</sup><i class="footnote">If one reads <i>they sacrificed animal sacrifices, but they brought elevation sacrifices the next day</i>, one implies that the day when Solomon was definitively installed as King in an assembly of all of Israel was a holiday, a natural assumption. While זֶבַח simply means “slaughtered offering”, in the Talmudim the expression always is interpreted to refer to family sacrifices, well-being or thanksgiving offerings. The verse then implies that it was permitted to bring well-being sacrifices on the same day, but not elevation offerings, which seems to follow the House of Shammai. (Since the House of Shammai require that the leaning-on ceremony be performed the day before, this interpretation presupposes that everybody knew in advance what David was going to do; unlikely but not impossible.)</i>; he who reads it as one sentence follows the House of Hillel<sup class="footnote-marker">67</sup><i class="footnote">If one reads the verse following the masoretic accents it is one unit and implies that well-being and elevation offerings were brought on the same day, but since the next day was supposed to be a workday, what one should formulate that the verse describe a situation equally acceptable to both Houses.</i>. One understands elevation sacrifices the next day; but well-being sacrifices the next day? Are not well-being sacrifices brought following the House of Shammai<sup class="footnote-marker">68</sup><i class="footnote">This is an objection to the first statement, that the verse may be read supporting the House of Shammai. As usual, the argument refers to the part of the verse not quoted. In the second part of the verse it first is stated that the official offerings were 1’000 bulls, 1’000 rams, 1’000 sheep and their libation, in addition to <i>animal sacrifices</i> (i. e., well-being sacrifices) of all of Israel. This implies that all elevation sacrifices were State sacrifices; following the House of Shammai the well-being sacrifices of all of Israel would have to be mentioned before, not after, the elevation sacrifices.</i>? Rebbi Yose ben Rebbi Abun said, David died on Pentecost<sup class="footnote-marker">69</sup><i class="footnote">Since the obituary notice for David is immediately following (vv. 26–28), it is implied that he died immediately after handing over to Salomon the blueprint for the Temple and the money to cover the expenses.</i>; all of Israel were deep mourners<sup class="footnote-marker">70</sup><i class="footnote">People who are obligated to take care of the burial of any Jew are barred from sacral acts. It is difficult but not totally impossible to combine R. Yose ben R. Abun’s statement with v. 22, that on that day they ate and drank in great joy.</i> and they brought the next day.
<sup class="footnote-marker">71</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Chagigah (Lieberman) 2:11" href="/Tosefta_Chagigah_(Lieberman).2.11">Tosephta <i>Ḥagigah</i> 2:11</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Chagigah 20a" href="/Chagigah.20a">Babli <i>Ḥagigah</i>20a</a>.</i>“It happened that Hillel the Elder brought his elevation offering<sup class="footnote-marker">72</sup><i class="footnote">Which has to be a male.</i> to the Temple courtyard<sup class="footnote-marker">73</sup><i class="footnote">On a holiday where the House of Shammai forbid offering private elevation offerings.</i> and leaned on it. The students of Shammai ganged up on him. He started to wag its tail, said to them, look, it is a female and I brought it as well-being sacrifice, and kept them talking about other matters until they left. After some time the House of Shammai became strong and they wanted to fix practice following their words. There was there Bava ben Bitay<sup class="footnote-marker">74</sup><i class="footnote">At other places he is called ben Buta; he can be dated to Herod’s time.</i> from the students of the House of Shammai who realized<sup class="footnote-marker">75</sup><i class="footnote">Not in theory but by the experience mentioned in the sequel. He accepts the ruling of the House of Hillel in this particular case only.</i> that practice followed the House of Hillel. Once he entered the Temple courtyard and found it desolate<sup class="footnote-marker">76</sup><i class="footnote">Since nobody wanted to bring his festival well-being offering without the equally required elevation offering. In addition nobody wanted to forgo the leaning on the animal just preceding the slaughter.</i>. He said, the house of those who make our God’s House desolate shall be desolate. What did he do? He sent and brought 3’000 sheep from Qedar sheep<sup class="footnote-marker">77</sup><i class="footnote">A biblical allusion to the best quality of sheep, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Isaiah 60:7" href="/Isaiah.60.7"><i>Is.</i> 60:7</a>.</i>, checked them for defects<sup class="footnote-marker">78</sup><i class="footnote">For defects which would disqualify them as sacrifices.</i>, and displayed them on the Temple Mount. He said to them, listen to me my brothers, the House of Israel. Anybody who wants to bring an elevation sacrifice may bring it and lean on it, to bring a well-being sacrifice may bring it and lean on it. At that moment, practice was fixed following the House of Hillel; and nobody said anything<sup class="footnote-marker">79</sup><i class="footnote">The other followers of the House of Shammai were silenced by the evidence. “Practice” here means Temple usage which the serving priests were instructed to follow.</i>.” <sup class="footnote-marker">80</sup><i class="footnote">This comment by popular sayings appears not only here and in <i>Ḥagigah</i> but also in <i>Ma`aser Šeni</i> 5:6 (Note 108). The text here (in parentheses) is obviously corrupt; the reasonable text is that of <i>Ḥagigah</i> &lt;in brackets&gt;.</i> Rebbi Isaac ben Rebbi Eleazar said, (this implies) &lt;this cup&gt; for breakfast has to be full<sup class="footnote-marker">81</sup><i class="footnote">A controversy arouses passions only at the start. Since by the time it was settled by Bava ben Bitay it was an old matter, one had to expect that nobody was interested anymore.</i>; the (poison) &lt;worm&gt; in the wood comes from itself<sup class="footnote-marker">82</sup><i class="footnote">Practice was decided against the House of Shammai by one of their own students.</i>; any coal which does not cause a burn at the start will not cause one<sup class="footnote-marker">83</sup><i class="footnote">Nobody is excited if a matter not of intense current interest is decided one way or the other.</i>. <sup class="footnote-marker">84</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Chagigah (Lieberman) 2:12" href="/Tosefta_Chagigah_(Lieberman).2.12">Tosephta <i>Ḥagigah</i> 2:12</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Chagigah 20a" href="/Chagigah.20a">Babli <i>Ḥagigah</i>20a</a>.</i>“Again it happened that a student of Hillel brought his elevation offering to the Temple courtyard and leaned on it. A student of Shammai saw him and said to him, what is this leaning-on? He said to him, what about being silent? He silenced him by a reprimand and he left.”

Halakhah 5

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> The House of Shammai are saying that a person may prepare hot water for his feet only if it is drinkable<sup class="footnote-marker">85</sup><i class="footnote">Since only the preparation of food is permitted on the holiday.</i>, but the House of Hillel permit<sup class="footnote-marker">86</sup><i class="footnote">Since cooking was permitted for the purpose of preparing food it is permitted for any purpose similar to preparing food. (For understanding the Babli the last four words are deleted.)</i>. One may make a pyre and warm himself by it<sup class="footnote-marker">87</sup><i class="footnote">In the Halakhah interpreted differently for the Houses of Shammai and Hillel.</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> One explains this by two explanations: in the opinion of the House of Shammai if he drank from it; in the opinion of the House of Hillel if it is drinkable<sup class="footnote-marker">88</sup><i class="footnote">The House of Shammai only permit the use of hot water if some of it was used as a drink; the House of Hillel permit heating if the water is drinkable, according to their principle (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Beitzah 2:5:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Beitzah.2.5.1">Note 86</a>). This seems incompatible with the language of the Mishnah since it seems to put the words of the House of Shammai into the mouth of the House of Hillel, but is a necessary interpretation if we hold that the House of Shammai permit heating only for the purpose of preparing food. The Babli ignores the problem, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosafot on Beitzah 21b:12:1" href="/Tosafot_on_Beitzah.21b.12.1"><i>Tosaphot</i> 21b, <i>s. v</i>. לא</a>.</i>.
“One may make a pyre and warm himself by it.” This is everybody’s opinion on condition that there be a kettle by the pyre. One may explain it by another explanation: In the opinion of the House of Shammai, on condition that there be a kettle by the pyre. In the opinion of the House of Hillel, even if there is no kettle by the pyre<sup class="footnote-marker">89</sup><i class="footnote">The <a class="refLink" data-ref="Beitzah 21b" href="/Beitzah.21b">Babli 21b</a> disagrees and holds that the statement must be by the House of Hillel alone.</i>.
<sup class="footnote-marker">90</sup><i class="footnote">This paragraph also is in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Shabbat 3:3:12-13" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Shabbat.3.3.12-13"><i>Šabbat</i> 3, Notes 121–128</a>.</i> Hot water heated on a holiday and similarly hot water heated on Friday for the Sabbath, Rav and Samuel, one said, one uses it to wash his face, hands, and feet, while the other said, one uses it to wash his body limb by limb. We did not know who said what but since Samuel stated, one uses it to wash his face, hands, and feet, it follows that it was [Rav] who said, one uses it to wash his entire body limb by limb. A philosopher asked Bar Qappara; Ablat asked Levi the eunuch: Is it permitted for drinking but forbidden for taking a bath? He told him, if you saw an eunuch embracing your wife, would you not feel badly about it? He said, yes. He asked him, can he squeeze her<sup class="footnote-marker">91</sup><i class="footnote">Translation of the text in <i>Šabbat</i>. Text here: “him”. </i> in any way? He said, that she should not get loose morals. He told him, here also that they should not get loose morals. After he left, his students told him, this one you pushed away with a stick; what do you answer us? He said to them: Is there not already written<sup class="footnote-marker">63</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 12:16" href="/Exodus.12.16"><i>Ex.</i> 12:16</a>.</i> <i>only what is being eaten by everybody, this alone may be made by you</i>.

Halakhah 6

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> In three matters Rabban Gamliel is restrictive following the words of the House of Shammai: One does not store away hot water from the holiday to the Sabbath<sup class="footnote-marker">92</sup><i class="footnote">In their times, hot water was not often used as drink. Therefore on one hand it is questionable whether one may not store hot water from the holiday which falls on a Friday to the Sabbath without <i>eruv tavshilin</i> (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Beitzah 2:1:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Beitzah.2.1.1">Note 2</a>); on the other hand if water is considered food under all circumstances whether hot water can be stored even with an <i>eruv tavshilin</i> since its nature is not changed by cooking.</i>, one does not raise the candelabrum on the holiday<sup class="footnote-marker">93</sup><i class="footnote">If the candelabrum came apart one may not put it together again.</i>, and one does not bake his bread as large loaves but as thin cakes<sup class="footnote-marker">94</sup><i class="footnote">Those are eaten quickly and there is no danger that one may bake on a holiday also for the following weekday.</i>. Rabban Gamliel said, never in my father’s house did they bake their bread as large loaves but as thin cakes<sup class="footnote-marker">95</sup><i class="footnote">He infers that this is practice to be followed by everybody.</i>. They said to him, what can we do about your father’s house who were restrictive for themselves but permissive for all of Israel<sup class="footnote-marker">96</sup><i class="footnote">Restrictive practices by outstanding religious authorities cannot be taken as rules for everybody. Since the majority objected to his statement about baked goods, only that statement is not practice.</i> to bake their bread as large loaves and on charcoal<sup class="footnote-marker">97</sup><i class="footnote">Gen. 40:16, the story of Pharao’s baker. It is not clear whether the root is חרר “to be roasted on coals”, or (Aramaic, Arabic) חור “white”, baked goods made from very white flour. In any case, it seems to designate baked goods made in larger quantities, of which therefore some may be left over for the next day which is not a holiday.</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> Abba bar Rav Huna<sup class="footnote-marker">98</sup><i class="footnote">In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Beitzah 21b" href="/Beitzah.21b">Babli 21b</a> in the name of Rav Huna himself.</i> said, [this is]<sup class="footnote-marker">99</sup><i class="footnote">Corrector’s (unnecessary) addition.</i> if he did not make an <i>eruv tavshilin</i> for himself. But if he made an <i>eruv tavshilin</i> for himself it is permitted. What is the difference between a dish and hot water? From a dish one usually eats, from hot water one usually does not drink.
“One does not raise the candelabrum on the holiday.” What you are saying is about a composite candelabrum, but not about a candelabrum which is not composite. As the following: Rebbi Yose’s students were sitting together. The candelabrum fell down before them; each single one slipped off by himself and went away.
“One does not bake his bread as large loaves but as thin cakes.” Since you tire him<sup class="footnote-marker">100</sup><i class="footnote">Since making single small loaves is more work that making one large loaf.</i> he bakes only what is needed. Rebbi Aḥa understood it from the following: <i>what is this great burning rage</i><sup class="footnote-marker">101</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 29:23" href="/Deuteronomy.29.23"><i>Deut</i>. 29:23</a>, root חרר.</i>. The Sages understand it from the following, <i>and behold, three bags of white-flour baked goods on my head</i><sup class="footnote-marker">102</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Genesis 40:16" href="/Genesis.40.16"><i>Gen</i>. 40:16</a>, root חרר.</i>.

Halakhah 7

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> He also said three matters of leniency: On the holiday one sweeps between the couches<sup class="footnote-marker">103</sup><i class="footnote">The couches are in the dining hall, <i>triclinium</i>, which may be swept after the meal to collect the morsels of food which fell down during the dinner. If the dining hall has a dirt floor, sweeping with a broom will have the side effect of filling uneven spots in the floor, which is a building activity and therefore forbidden. There would be no problem on a stone floor.</i> and puts down incense<sup class="footnote-marker">104</sup><i class="footnote">One puts spices on burning charcoal to cover any bad smell. This cannot be considered cooking food, and even for the House of Hillel it is forbidden since it is available only to the rich and the verse <a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 12:16" href="/Exodus.12.16"><i>Ex.</i> 12:16</a> only permits work for things available to <i>everybody</i>.</i>; and one makes a helmeted kid-goat<sup class="footnote-marker">105</sup><i class="footnote">A kid-goat roasted whole. Since this is the way the pascal lamb has to be roasted as sacrifice in Temple times, it is forbidden in absence of a Temple.</i> in the Passover night, but the Sages forbid.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> <sup class="footnote-marker">106</sup><i class="footnote">Tosephta 2:13-14, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Beitzah 22b" href="/Beitzah.22b">Babli 22b</a>.</i>“In Rabban Gamliel’s house one used to sweep between the couches. Rebbi Eleazar ben Rebbi Ṣadoq said, I ate many times at Rabban Gamliel’s and they did not sweep between the couches, but they had spread sheets on the ground<sup class="footnote-marker">107</sup><i class="footnote">“On the earth”. This proves that one speaks of a dirt floor.</i> and when the guest left they were folding them. They said to him, this he is permitted to do even on the Sabbath. In Rabban Gamliel’s house one used to put down incense in a barrel. Rebbi Eleazar ben Rebbi Ṣadoq said, I ate many times at Rabban Gamliel’s and they did not put down incense in a barrel, but they were burning incense in chests<sup class="footnote-marker">108</sup><i class="footnote">Late vulgar Latin <i>pyrdiscos</i> “chest”, from Greek πυργίσκος. The sources are given by Liebermann, <i>Tosephta kiFshutah Moed</i> p. 957.</i> on holiday eve, and when the guests arrived they were opening them. They said to him, this he is permitted to do even on the Sabbath.”
“And one makes a helmeted kid-goat in the Passover night, but the Sages forbid.” <sup class="footnote-marker">109</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Beitzah (Lieberman) 2:15" href="/Tosefta_Beitzah_(Lieberman).2.15">Tosephta 2:15</a>. The same text in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Pesachim 7:1:7-8" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Pesachim.7.1.7-8"><i>Pesaḥim</i> 7:1 (Notes 21–24</a>); <a class="refLink" data-ref="Pesachim 74a" href="/Pesachim.74a">Babli <i>Pesaḥim</i> 74a</a>.</i>“What is a helmeted kid-goat? Entirely roasted, <i>its head, on its hooves, and on its innards</i>. If he parboiled part of it, or cooked part of it, this is not a helmeted kid-goat. One serves helmeted kid-goat in the evening of the first day of Tabernacles, (and on the first day of) [and in the night of the last day of] Passover<sup class="footnote-marker">110</sup><i class="footnote">The scribe’s text in parentheses and the corrector’s in brackets are both acceptable; the roasted kid-goat is forbidden only during the first night. Therefore the corrector’s change of the text is unwarranted, but cf. <i>Pesaḥim</i> Note 22.</i>. One serves helmeted calf on the first night of Passover but not helmeted kid-goat<sup class="footnote-marker">111</sup><i class="footnote">Roast meat is acceptable as long as it is not sheep or goat.</i>.” <sup class="footnote-marker">112</sup><i class="footnote">In addition to the <i>Pesaḥim</i> text, this paragraph also is in <i>Mo`ed qatan</i> 3:1 (81d line 27,). <a class="refLink" data-ref="Beitzah 23a" href="/Beitzah.23a">Babli 23a</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Berakhot 19a" href="/Berakhot.19a"><i>Berakhot</i> 19a</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Pesachim 53a" href="/Pesachim.53a"><i>Pesaḥim</i> 53a</a>.</i> It was stated: Rebbi Yose said, Theudas of Rome led the people of Rome to eat helmeted kid-goat in the Passover nights. The Sages sent and said to him, if you were not Theudas, would we not put you in the ban? Who was Theudas? Rebbi Ḥananiah said, because he was providing for the rabbis. Are you not causing the public to eat <i>sancta</i> outside the Temple? And anybody causing the public to eat <i>sancta</i> outside the Temple has to be put in the ban.

Halakhah 8

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> Three things did Rebbi Eleazar ben Azariah permit but the Sages forbid. His cow was going out with a strip between her horns<sup class="footnote-marker">113</sup><i class="footnote">On a Sabbath, when the owner of an animal is biblically required to let his animals rest.</i>, and one grooms an animal with combs with small teeth on a holiday<sup class="footnote-marker">114</sup><i class="footnote">To remove parasites from the animal’s skin.</i>, and one grinds peppers in their proper mill<sup class="footnote-marker">115</sup><i class="footnote">While biblically it is permitted (even desirable) to prepare food on the holiday, the permission does not extend to the preparation of ingredients; cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Beitzah 1:10:4" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Beitzah.1.10.4">Chapter 1, Notes 241–242</a>. R. Eleazar must hold that ground pepper loses much of its strength if kept overnight; for him the grinding is part of the preparation of the meal while for the majority is it preparation of an ingredient.</i>. Rebbi Jehudah says, one does not groom an animal on the holiday with a comb with small teeth because it produces bruises, but one may groom with a comb with large teeth. <sup class="footnote-marker">116</sup><i class="footnote">This text is obvious nonsense and has to be replaced by the text quoted in the Halakhah: But the Sages are saying that one may comb neither with small teeth nor with large teeth. </i> But the Sages are saying one uses a comb with small teeth but not with large teeth.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> <sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">Since the sanctity of the holiday is distinct from that of the Sabbath, the permission to prepare food on a holiday does not imply to prepare food for a different day.</i> Rebbi Abba: Both Rav and Samuel are saying, the words of the Sages are that even pulling her with them is forbidden. Rebbi Abba in the name of Samuel: If its horns were drilling it is permitted<sup class="footnote-marker">118</sup><i class="footnote">If the horns are dangerously sharp and the strips are not a decoration but a necessary precaution they do not have to be removed.</i>. Rebbi Yose said, I pointed out the difficulty before Rebbi Abba and he said, we only stated: “the female camel with a nose ring.<sup class="footnote-marker">119</sup><i class="footnote">Anything which is only for control of the animal must be of the kind mentioned inMishnah <i>Šabbat</i> Chapter 5; the only other permitted loads are those to protect the lives of humans and animals. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Shabbat 52a" href="/Shabbat.52a">Babli <i>Šabbat</i>52a</a>.</i>” Rebbi Ze`ira in the name of Samuel: A badly behaved ox goes out with his bridle; our teachers in the Diaspora do this. Rebbi Aḥa, Rebbi Jacob bar Idi in the name of Rebbi Jonathan, Rebbi Tanḥum bar Ḥiyya: A badly behaved donkey goes out with his bridle. Rebbi Illa, Rav Jehudah in the name of Rebbi Simeon ben Rebbi Ḥiyya: A dog goes out with his muzzle<sup class="footnote-marker">120</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Shabbat 51b" href="/Shabbat.51b">Babli <i>Šabbat</i>51b</a>.</i>. If to punish him it is forbidden, if so he should not eat his bridle it is permitted.
Ganiva said, he was continuing teaching as ruling. Rebbi Jonah from Bostra asked, if he was continuing teaching as ruling, is that what we were stating: “against the will of the Sages”<sup class="footnote-marker">122</sup><i class="footnote">A theoretical disagreement is an everyday occurrence; as long as the teacher does not advocate acting against the received majority opinion he should be commended, not rebuked.</i>? Rebbi Jehudah bar Pazi: Bar Delaya stated, they told him, either remove yourself from between us or remove the strip from between its horns. Rebbi Yose ben Rebbi Abun said, he was shielding himself against them<sup class="footnote-marker">123</sup><i class="footnote">He actively indicated that the majority opinion was wrong.</i>. Rebbi Ḥananiah said, it went out once and his teeth became black from the fasts and mortification. Rebbi Idi from Ḥotra said, it was his wife<sup class="footnote-marker">124</sup><i class="footnote">This explains why “his cow” is mentioned in the singular when he was very rich and the owner of large herds.</i>. And from where that his wife is called “calf”? “<i>Had you not ploughed with my calf you would not have solved my riddle</i><sup class="footnote-marker">.</sup><sup class="footnote-marker">125</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Judges 14:18" href="/Judges.14.18"><i>Jud</i>. 14:18</a>.</i>” There<sup class="footnote-marker">126</sup><i class="footnote">In Babylonia; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Beitzah 23a" href="/Beitzah.23a">Babli 23a</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Shabbat 54b" href="/Shabbat.54b"><i>Šabbat</i>54b</a>.</i>, they are saying, it was his neighbor. Is anybody punished for his neighbor’s behavior? Rebbi Qiris from Irma<sup class="footnote-marker">127</sup><i class="footnote">Irma possibly is Urmia in Armenia.</i>: To teach you that if anybody has the possibility to protest and he does not protest, the damage is attached to him.
<sup class="footnote-marker">117</sup><i class="footnote">This paragraph and the next are also in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Shabbat 5:4:7-8" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Shabbat.5.4.7-8"><i>Šabbat</i> 5, Notes 73–83</a>.</i> What is קִידוּר? {Combs} with small {teeth} which cause bruises. קִירְצוּף, {combs} with large {teeth} which do not cause bruises<sup class="footnote-marker">128</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Beitzah (Lieberman) 2:17" href="/Tosefta_Beitzah_(Lieberman).2.17">Tosephta 2:17</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Beitzah 23a" href="/Beitzah.23a">Babli 23a</a>.</i>.
“But the Sages are saying one uses neither a comb with small teeth nor one with large teeth.”<sup class="footnote-marker">116</sup><i class="footnote">This text is obvious nonsense and has to be replaced by the text quoted in the Halakhah: But the Sages are saying that one may comb neither with small teeth nor with large teeth. </i> Not one with small teeth, that one not come to make a bruise, nor with large teeth, that one not come to tear out hair.
<sup class="footnote-marker">129</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Beitzah (Lieberman) 2:16" href="/Tosefta_Beitzah_(Lieberman).2.16">Tosephta 2:16</a>, cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Beitzah 1:10:4" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Beitzah.1.10.4">Chapter 1 Note 242</a>. This argument refers to the statement of R. Eleazar ben Azariah in the Mishnah who agrees with Rabban Gamliel.</i>“In the household of Rabban Gamliel they were grinding pepper in their mills. Rebbi Eleazar ben Rebbi Ṣadoq said, my father once ate at Rabban Gamliel’s when they brought before him oily fish sauces<sup class="footnote-marker">130</sup><i class="footnote">Greek ʼελαιόγαρον; cf. <i>Ševi`it</i> 8:2, Note 24.</i> containing ground pepper. When he tasted this, he put it away. They said to him, do not worry, they were ground the day before.” But would not Rebbi Ṣadoq be as in error with Rabban Gamliel and could eat<sup class="footnote-marker">131</sup><i class="footnote">It is implied here that food prepared in violation of an implied holiday prohibition is permitted to persons not involved in the preparation of this food. This is stated explicitly only in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Beitzah 25a" href="/Beitzah.25a">Babli 25a</a>.</i>? But not to permit work by the example of Rabban Gamliel<sup class="footnote-marker">132</sup><i class="footnote">It is agreed that R. Ṣadoq could have eaten Rabban Gamliel’s sauce, and he might have eaten had he not been a respected rabbinical authority. He did not want to endorse Rabban Gamliel’s action by eating his food in a semi-public place since the common authority of Rabban Gamliel and R. Ṣadoq would have permitted the routine grinding of pepper on the holiday, which for him is biblically forbidden.</i>.

Halakhah 9

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> The pepper mill is impure<sup class="footnote-marker">133</sup><i class="footnote">This statement is an appendix to the previous Mishnah where a pepper mill was mentioned. In all statements about purity, “impure” means “susceptible to impurity”; “pure” means “impervious to impurity”.</i> because of three vessels, because of a container, and as a metal vessel, and as sieve<sup class="footnote-marker">134</sup><i class="footnote">As the Halakhah explains, the mill is composed of three parts. Each of the parts may become impure for a different reason but since they are connected the impurity of one part causes the impurity of the whole. Any metal vessel can become impure since metal vessels are mentioned as impure in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 6:21" href="/Leviticus.6.21"><i>Lev.</i> 6:21</a>. Implements which can be used as containers are impure (unless made of stone which is impervious to impurity) on basis of <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 11:33" href="/Leviticus.11.33"><i>Lev.</i> 11:33</a>. A sieve is an implement made for work and is susceptible to impurity by <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 11:32" href="/Leviticus.11.32"><i>Lev.</i> 11:32</a>.</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> The lowest part as container, the uppermost as metal vessel, and the middle part as sieve<sup class="footnote-marker">134</sup><i class="footnote">As the Halakhah explains, the mill is composed of three parts. Each of the parts may become impure for a different reason but since they are connected the impurity of one part causes the impurity of the whole. Any metal vessel can become impure since metal vessels are mentioned as impure in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 6:21" href="/Leviticus.6.21"><i>Lev.</i> 6:21</a>. Implements which can be used as containers are impure (unless made of stone which is impervious to impurity) on basis of <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 11:33" href="/Leviticus.11.33"><i>Lev.</i> 11:33</a>. A sieve is an implement made for work and is susceptible to impurity by <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 11:32" href="/Leviticus.11.32"><i>Lev.</i> 11:32</a>.</i>.

Halakhah 10

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> A baby carriage is impure as support<sup class="footnote-marker">135</sup><i class="footnote">Any implement used to sit or lay on becomes biblically impure if it supports a person impure by gonorrhea (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 15:9" href="/Leviticus.15.9">Lev. 15:9</a>,<a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 15:26" href="/Leviticus.15.26">26</a>) or menstruation (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 20" href="/Leviticus.20">Lev. 20</a>). This impurity is transmitted by the load; no direct contact is necessary. If the princess has her period, even if she touched only the uppermost of her ten mattresses, the entire bedstead is severely impure.</i>, may be used on the Sabbath, but is drawn<sup class="footnote-marker">136</sup><i class="footnote">As the parallels in the Babli and the independent Mishnah mss. as well as logical necessity show, there is a word missing and one must read “but it is only d rawn on implements.” As the Halakhah explains, the Mishnah follows Rebbi Jehudah who holds that on the Sabbath and holidays one is liable also for unintended or undesirable effects of his action, in contrast to R. Simeon who holds liable only for prohibited actions whose prohibited effect was intended. The problem here is that drawing anything on a dirt floor will make a groove. Since pieces of dirt are moved to the side of the groove, this is forbidden digging. Therefore for R. Jehudah and his followers one may drag things on a floor only if the dirt is covered.</i> on implements. Rebbi Jehudah said, no vessels are drawn except a carriage because it compresses<sup class="footnote-marker">140</sup><i class="footnote">Under a wheel the dirt is moved only vertically, not horizontally, and this is missing an essential ingredient of biblically forbidden digging. </i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> We have stated<sup class="footnote-marker">138</sup><i class="footnote">In the Babli version (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Beitzah (Lieberman) 2:18" href="/Tosefta_Beitzah_(Lieberman).2.18">Tosephta <i>Yom Ṭov</i> 2:18</a>; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Shabbat 22a" href="/Shabbat.22a">Babli <i>Šabbat</i> 22a</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Shabbat 29b" href="/Shabbat.29b">29b</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Shabbat 46a" href="/Shabbat.46a">46a</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Pesachim 101a" href="/Pesachim.101a"><i>Pesaḥim</i> 101a</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Menachot 41b" href="/Menachot.41b"><i>Menaḥot</i> 41b</a>) only R. Simeon’s opinion is quoted.</i>: “A person may not drag a bed, or a chair, or a footstool<sup class="footnote-marker">139</sup><i class="footnote">Latin <i>subsellium</i>.</i>, or a fauteuil<sup class="footnote-marker">140</sup><i class="footnote">Under a wheel the dirt is moved only vertically, not horizontally, and this is missing an essential ingredient of biblically forbidden digging.</i>, because he makes a groove, but Rebbi Simeon permits.” <sup class="footnote-marker">141</sup><i class="footnote">This text also is in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Shabbat 3:3:3-7:14" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Shabbat.3.3.3-7.14"><i>Šabbat</i> Chapter 3, Notes 74–78</a>; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Kilayim 1:9:3" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Kilayim.1.9.3"><i>Kilaim</i>1:9 (Notes 167–178</a>).</i> Rebbi Abba in the name of Rav Ḥuna, Rebbi Ḥaggai in the name of Rebbi Ze`ira, Rebbi Yose in the name of Rebbi Ila: The Sages admit to Rebbi Simeon that a chair whose legs are stuck in mud one is permitted to move on the Sabbath<sup class="footnote-marker">142</sup><i class="footnote">Since the mud is soft, the groove will disappear by itself; it was not “made”.</i>. Similarly it is permitted to return it. Rebbi Yose said, we also have stated<sup class="footnote-marker">143</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Kilayim 1:9:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Kilayim.1.9.1">Mishnah <i>Kilaim</i>1:9</a> referring to turnips and radishes partially hidden after harvest.</i>: “and they may be removed on the Sabbath.” Rebbi Yose ben Rebbi Abun said, this is Rebbi Simeon’s. Rebbi Yose said, a Mishnah says so, “one may not drag any implement except a carriage because it compresses<sup class="footnote-marker">144</sup><i class="footnote">Mishnah 10. The wheels of the carriage will press the dust down, not move it sideways as would be the case if one drags anything without wheels. The Sages opposing R. Simeon forbid moving the “unprepared” dust even if this was not the intention of the person acting. This Mishnah cannot follow R. Simeon; it contradicts R. Simeon’s Mishnah in <i>Kilaim</i>.</i>.”

Chapter 3



Halakhah 1

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> One does not catch fish from vivaria<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">This vivarium is not a container kept in the house but a pond in which fish are grown. Since the pond is rather large, the fish cannot simply be taken out; they must be caught and this is forbidden on the holiday. On the other hand, the fish can feed from the vegetation that grows in the pond and do not have to be fed every day; feeding is forbidden since the fish will congregate at the spot of feeding where they can easily be taken out; but taking a fish out still would be considered catching it.</i> and one does not feed them. But one catches wild animals and fowl from vivaria<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">This vivarium is an enclosure in which the animals are kept and must be fed every day.</i> and feeds them. Rabban Simeon ben Gamliel says, not all vivaria are equal. This is the principle: any which still requires catching is forbidden<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">Taking the animal is not catching if and only if the enclosure is small enough that one does not have to run after an animal.</i>, but any which does not require catching is permitted.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “One does not catch fish,” etc. <sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">The Halakhah is copied in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Shabbat 13:5:2-3" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Shabbat.13.5.2-3"><i>Šabbat</i> Chapter 13 (Notes 60–74</a>), referring to <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Shabbat 13:5:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Shabbat.13.5.1">Mishnah <i>Šabbat</i> 13:5</a>.</i> Rebbi Ḥinena said, our Mishnah does not follow Rebbi Jehudah, as we have stated there: “<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Shabbat 13:5:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Shabbat.13.5.1">Mishnah Šabbat 13:5</a>, a statement of R. Jehudah.</i> one who catches a bird for a cage or a deer for a house is liable;” therefore for a garden or a vivarium he is not liable<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">But the animals still can run around in the enclosure and need to be caught another time. It seems that the anonymous (generally accepted) <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Beitzah 3:1:1-3" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Beitzah.3.1.1-3">Mishnah 3:1</a> is incompatible with R. Jehudah’s statement in <i>Šabbat</i>.</i>. The argument of Rebbi Jehudah is inverted since we have stated: “one does not catch fish from vivaria and one does not feed them,” therefore for a garden or a vivarium he is not liable? The argument of the rabbis is inverted, as we have stated there: “but the Sages say, a bird for a cage and a deer for a garden, or a courtyard, or a vivarium,” and we have stated, “but one catches wild animals and fowl from vivaria and feeds them,” therefore not for a garden or a vivarium<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote">How can the rabbis forbid catching a deer in a vegetable garden as a Sabbath violation but allow it on a holiday? This contradicts the statement (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Megillah 1:3:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Megillah.1.3.1">Mishnah <i>Megillah</i> 1:5</a>) that the only difference between prohibited work on Sabbath and Holiday refers to the preparation of food. It is explained in <i>Yom Ṭov</i> that preparation of meat starts with slaughter; anything preceding this is not exempt from the prohibition of work.</i>? One for a covered courtyard, the other for a courtyard which is not covered<sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote">A courtyard covered by a roof is a house. The <a class="refLink" data-ref="Beitzah 24a" href="/Beitzah.24a">Babli 24a</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Shabbat 106b" href="/Shabbat.106b"><i>Šabbat</i>106b</a>, restricts this answer to vivaria.</i>. But did we not state “a garden”? Can you say a covered garden<sup class="footnote-marker">10</sup><i class="footnote">Nothing would grow in a vegetable garden devoid of sunlight. The answer cannot be correct.</i>? But here a large one, there a small one<sup class="footnote-marker">11</sup><i class="footnote">A small garden is one where the deer can be taken without effort, a large one where one has to use tools to catch the deer; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Beitzah 24a" href="/Beitzah.24a">Babli 24a</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Shabbat 106b" href="/Shabbat.106b"><i>Šabbat</i> 106b</a>. The question now arises how to define “small” and “large” for practical application.</i>. Rebbi Ulla said, they asked before Rebbi Aḥa, how should one state? In any case where catching is still missing one is liable, but if catching is not missing one is not liable<sup class="footnote-marker">12</sup><i class="footnote">This discussion refers to the last statement in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Beitzah 3:1:1-3" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Beitzah.3.1.1-3">Mishnah 1</a>. Should one say that for large vivaria, taking animals on a holiday is a biblical violation even though this seems to contradict the formulation of the first part of <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Beitzah 3:1:1-3" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Beitzah.3.1.1-3">Mishnah 1</a>?</i>? He answered them, do we not deal with the case that he corrals them into it? But “in any case where catching is still missing it is forbidden, but if catching is not missing it is permitted”<sup class="footnote-marker">13</sup><i class="footnote">This is the traditional formulation of the last statement in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Beitzah 3:1:1-3" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Beitzah.3.1.1-3">Mishnah <i>Beṣah</i> 3:1</a> in the name of Rabban Simeon ben Gamliel, in contrast to the formulation in <i>Šabbat</i> 13:5. The prohibition is rabbinic since animals in a corral are no longer wild. The next statement translates the criterion into a practical rule. The <a class="refLink" data-ref="Beitzah 24a" href="/Beitzah.24a">Babli 24a</a> has a different rule.</i>. Rebbi Samuel, Rebbi Berekhiah’s brother, said: any which needs nets needs catching, what does not need nets does not need catching.
“One may slaughter from containers, one may not slaughter from fishermen’s nets or from traps.<sup class="footnote-marker">14</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Beitzah (Lieberman) 3:1" href="/Tosefta_Beitzah_(Lieberman).3.1">Tosephta 3:1</a>; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Beitzah 24b" href="/Beitzah.24b">Babli 24b</a>.</i>” Rebbi Yose bar Abun in the name of Rebbi Immi: a net of warp<sup class="footnote-marker">15</sup><i class="footnote">It does not have to be a real net with threads knotted crosswise; even a net of parallel threads or wires spanned between two rods is a tool whose use on a holiday is forbidden.</i>. Samuel said, one may catch with a barrel<sup class="footnote-marker">16</sup><i class="footnote">Greek πίθος, ὁ. Scooping up fish from an aquarium using a wide-belly clay vessel is not catching.</i>. Rav said, the sluice of a water canal is permitted. Rebbi Yudan said, those between the plates<sup class="footnote-marker">17</sup><i class="footnote">While one may not take fish from an open water canal, if part of it is closed off by planks on either side it was transformed into an aquarium from which fish may be taken.</i>

Halakhah 2

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> One may not take from traps for wild animals, or fowl, or fish which he set before the holiday unless it was known<sup class="footnote-marker">18</sup><i class="footnote">This implies that if there is a doubt whether the catch was available for consumption on the holiday before the start of the holiday, the use is forbidden.</i> that they were caught before nightfall. It happened that a Gentile brought fish to Rabban Gamliel who said they are permitted<sup class="footnote-marker">19</sup><i class="footnote">He disagrees and holds that as a rule if there is a possibility that the catch was available for consumption on the holiday before the start of the holiday, the use is permitted.</i>, but I do not want to accept from him<sup class="footnote-marker">20</sup><i class="footnote">He personally follows the ruling of Note 18 but he gave his public declaration to the contrary that practice not be following the restrictive rule.</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> <sup class="footnote-marker">21</sup><i class="footnote">The entire Halakhah was copied in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Shabbat 1:7:2-5" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Shabbat.1.7.2-5"><i>Šabbat</i> 1 (Notes 369–379</a>) relating to Mishnah <i>Šabbat</i> 1:10.</i> Who informs? If the trap was sprung, it is certain that they were caught when it still was daytime. But if the trap was not sprung, it is certain that they were not caught when it still was daytime. And even if the trap was sprung, in worry one may say that they were not caught when it still was day<sup class="footnote-marker">22</sup><i class="footnote">The Mishnah requires that it be known that an animal was trapped before the start of the holiday. How can this be ascertained unless one go there and take it out before the holiday? Does the first part of the Mishnah have any practical application? The answer is that by observation from afar one may determine what happened.<br>If an animal is trapped before the onset of the holiday it is prepared to be food on the holiday and may be taken and processed on the holiday. Another question then is whether the extension of <a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 16:5" href="/Exodus.16.5"><i>Ex.</i> 16:5</a> to holidays is considered biblical (when cases of doubt have to be resolved restrictively) or rabbinic (when cases of doubt have to be resolved leniently).</i>. Rebbi Yose ben Rebbi Abun said, if it was set in a forest. You should know that it is so since we have stated “fish”. Are fish not there where they are found? Also here at a place where game and birds are found<sup class="footnote-marker">23</sup><i class="footnote">The rules prescribed are a legal fiction (in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Beitzah 24b" href="/Beitzah.24b">Babli, 24b</a>, ascribed to the last generation of Tannaïm). They can be relied on only in situations where the probability of correctness is significantly larger than 50%. (Quoted by <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosafot on Shabbat 17b:7:2" href="/Tosafot_on_Shabbat.17b.7.2">Tosaphot <i>Šabbat</i>17b <i>s.v</i>. אלא</a>).</i>.
Rebbi Ze`ira in the name of Rav, they wanted to say, they are permitted the next day<sup class="footnote-marker">24</sup><i class="footnote">This now refers to the fish brought by a Gentile to Rabban Gamliel. Did he want to say that they can be handled by Jews on the holiday but not eaten on that day or are they as if prepared by a Jew beforehand (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Beitzah 24a" href="/Beitzah.24a">Babli <i>Beṣah</i> 24a</a>)?</i>. Rebbi Ḥizqiah and Rebbi Uziel the son of Rebbi Onias of Barat Hauran: They are really permitted. They wanted to say, its doubt made it permitted. Rebbi Ḥanina and Rebbi Jonathan both say, if in doubt whether it was prepared it is forbidden<sup class="footnote-marker">26</sup><i class="footnote">As explained earlier, the disagreement is whether the requirement of prior preparation is biblical or rabbinic.</i>. But Rebbi Joḥanan said, if in doubt whether it was prepared it is permitted.
The Elder Rebbi Ḥiyya and Rebbi Simeon ben Rebbi, one said a Gentile needs preparation, the other said, a Gentile does not need preparation<sup class="footnote-marker">26</sup><i class="footnote">As explained earlier, the disagreement is whether the requirement of prior preparation is biblical or rabbinic.</i>. We did not know who said this and who said that. Since Rav once refrained from coming before the Elder Rebbi Ḥiyya who asked him, where have you been? He told him: a caravan passed by and I ate figs from them<sup class="footnote-marker">27</sup><i class="footnote">The Gentile caravan arriving on the holiday.</i>; this implies that he said, a Gentile does not need preparation.
A student of Rebbi Simai went to Antipatris<sup class="footnote-marker">28</sup><i class="footnote">This is generally accepted as explanation of the otherwise unexplained and diverse names reported for the place.</i>; they brought him Morocco-plums<sup class="footnote-marker">29</sup><i class="footnote">According to H. L. Fleischer, New Greek δαμάσκηνος “Damascus plum”; δαμάσκηνα “Damascus plum tree”.</i> and he ate. A student of Rebbi Joshua ben Levi went there; they brought him Morocco-plums and he did not eat. He came and told it to his teacher who said to him, this one follows the argument of Rebbi Simai, as Rebbi Simai said, a Gentile does not need preparation. Rebbi Abbahu in the name of Rebbi Joshua ben Levi: The red grapes in a vineyard are forbidden. Rav Huna in the name of Rav: The hard dates on palm leaves are permitted<sup class="footnote-marker">30</sup><i class="footnote">Grapes which still are hard at harvest time and left to ripen in late autumn do not fall down by themselves; one must assume that they were plucked from the vine on the day they were brought; one may not accept them from a Gentile who brings them on a holiday. But dates in a similar case will fall down, one may assume that they were collected from the ground and may be accepted.</i>.

Halakhah 3

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> A dangerously sick animal<sup class="footnote-marker">31</sup><i class="footnote">One is afraid the animal might die and be lost as food. The animal can be slaughtered only if it is possible to use it as food on the holiday.</i> one should not slaughter unless it be known that one may eat from it a roasted piece in the size of an olive during daytime. Rebbi Aqiba says, even raw in its slaughter house. If he slaughtered in the fields he should not bring it by a yoke or a small yoke<sup class="footnote-marker">32</sup><i class="footnote">A yoke is carried by two people, a small yoke by a single person. Both are commercially used and therefore not appropriate for the holiday.</i>, but brings limb by limb in his hands.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> Rebbi Abba in the name of the rabbis there: If he slaughtered it and wolves ate its intestines it is qualified<sup class="footnote-marker">33</sup><i class="footnote">Even though punctured intestines make the animal <i>terefa</i> and forbidden for consumption, this is when the defect has been noted. The only organ which <i>must</i> be checked is the lung.</i>, for the presumption of intestines is qualification<sup class="footnote-marker">34</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Chullin 9a" href="/Chullin.9a">Babli <i>Ḥulin</i> 9a</a>.</i>. Should one not worry that maybe they were punctured? The presumption of intestines is that they are qualified. It was stated: But one may bring it on its hide. What does he do? He leaves one limb on it and brings it together with it<sup class="footnote-marker">35</sup><i class="footnote">While in Chapter 1 it was permitted to give the hides to the tanner, this refers to hides of animals destined from the start as food on the holiday and therefore prepared for the holiday. But the animal in question here was not prepared and the raw hide is not an implement; there is no reason to permit its movement on the holiday. The expedient to use is not to skin the animal completely but leave one ;piece of meat on the hide. Then this meat is potential food and may be moved; the hide is moved as an appendix of that meat.</i>.

Halakhah 4

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> If a firstling<sup class="footnote-marker">36</sup><i class="footnote">The unblemished firstling has to be given to a Cohen who has to sacrifice it in the Temple. In the absence of a Temple no use can be had of an unblemished firstling; if it develops a defect it may be slaughtered and its meat consumed by everybody, pure or impure (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 15:22" href="/Deuteronomy.15.22"><i>Deut</i>. 15:22</a>). Since the development of a defect by a firstling is an eagerly awaited event, by rabbinic usage the existence of a defect must be verified by an expert. If no defect is found, the firstling is not food and may not be moved from the cistern before the end of the holiday.</i> fell into a cistern, Rebbi Jehudah says, the expert should go down and inspect; if it has a defect he may lift it and slaughter; Rebbi Simeon says, any whose defect was not recognizable on the eve of the holiday is not prepared<sup class="footnote-marker">37</sup><i class="footnote">He holds that what was not food before the holiday cannot become food on the holiday; therefore the firstling may be inspected by the expert only after the end of the holiday.</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> The argument of Rebbi Jehudah seems inverted since we stated there<sup class="footnote-marker">38</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Shabbat 24:4:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Shabbat.24.4.1">Mishnah <i>Šabbat</i> 24:4</a>.</i>, “Rebbi Jehudah says, if it was not a cadaver before the Sabbath it is forbidden since it is not prepared<sup class="footnote-marker">39</sup><i class="footnote">He does not permit dog food if it was not available before the start of the Sabbath, how can he permit human food which was not available on the the eve of the holiday?</i>.” And here he says so? Rebbi Jehudah follows his own opinion since Rebbi Jehudah said, the expert is not from the Torah<sup class="footnote-marker">40</sup><i class="footnote">In <a class="refLink" data-ref="Mishnah Bekhorot 4:3" href="/Mishnah_Bekhorot.4.3">Mishnah <i>Bekhorot</i>4:3</a>, R. Jehudah permits to slaughter a damaged firstling without prior certification by an expert. He must hold that by biblical standards it is only the fact of the blemish that counts, not its certification by an expert. In addition, any kosher animal is potential food at all times.</i>. Rebbi Ḥuna in the name of Rebbi Abba: It is Rebbi Jehudah’s since Rebbi Jehudah gives the same rules for inspecting a firstling as inspecting <i>ṭerefah</i>. As you are saying there, one inspects for <i>ṭerefah</i> on a holiday<sup class="footnote-marker">41</sup><i class="footnote">An animal suffering from a life-threatening defect is prohibited as food. While there is a long list of such defects, these disqualify as food only if they are found, but one does not have to examine every slaughtered animal for all of these defects except for the lung for which it is obligatory to check for tubercular lesions which would make the animal unfit for human consumption. Since slaughter is permitted on the holiday, inspection for <i>ṭerefah</i> automatically is not only permitted but in fact required to legitimize the slaughter.</i>, similarly one inspects a firstling on the holiday. Rebbi Yudan asked: <i>Demay</i><sup class="footnote-marker">42</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Demay</i> is produce of which it is not known whether tithes were taken, cf. Introduction to Tractate <i>Demay</i>.</i> is from their words. and inspecting for <i>terefah</i> is from their words. Since you are saying that one inspects for <i>ṭerefah</i> on a holiday, does one separate <i>demay</i> on a holiday<sup class="footnote-marker">43</sup><i class="footnote">Rebbi Huna’s argument is disproved since it would imply that <i>demay</i> can be removed on the holiday. But it is stated in Mishnah <i>Šabbat</i>2:7 that <i>demay</i> may be tithed only during twilight on Sabbath (and holiday) eve, therefore not on the holiday itself.</i>? Rebbi Abbahu in the name of Rebbi Yose ben Ḥanina: Any whose defect was visible on the eve of a holiday but the expert permitted it only on the holiday is not prepared<sup class="footnote-marker">44</sup><i class="footnote">This statement clearly follows the opinion of R. Simeon in the Mishnah.</i>. But was it not stated, a [firstling]<sup class="footnote-marker">45</sup><i class="footnote">Corrector’s addition, based on Rav Ḥisda’s remark in the following sentence, but the scribe’s original text seems preferable. An animal which is known to have a <i>ṭerefah</i> defect is not potential food. As part of the mother’s body, the calf is not potential food. But if a healthy calf is born on the holiday, it is food at least for R. Jehudah.</i> calf which was born from a <i>terefah</i> animal on the holiday is permitted. Rav Ḥisda said, explain it if the expert transgressed and saw it<sup class="footnote-marker">46</sup><i class="footnote">If the corrector’s addition is deleted, then R. Ḥisda explains R. Jehudah’s opinion in the Mishnah since the latter also will agree that rabbinically the expert may not inspect firstlings on the holiday.</i>. There came a case<sup class="footnote-marker">47</sup><i class="footnote">Of a firstling falling into a cistern.</i> before Rebbi Immi who wanted to say, between Rebbi Jehudah and Rebbi Simeon, practice follows Rebbi Jehudah<sup class="footnote-marker">48</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Terumot</i>3:1, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Eruvin 46b" href="/Eruvin.46b">Babli <i>Eruvin</i>46b</a>.</i>. Rav Hoshaia brought a <i>baraita</i> of Bar Qappara from the South and stated: But the Sages say, any whose defect was visible on the eve of a holiday but the expert permitted it only on the holiday is not prepared<sup class="footnote-marker">49</sup><i class="footnote">In this <i>baraita</i>, the opinion of R. Simeon is quoted as that of “the Sages”, implying that it is practice to be followed. All the rules of precedence are subject to exceptions.</i>. He<sup class="footnote-marker">50</sup><i class="footnote">R. Immi.</i> changed his opinion.
<i>It and its young</i><sup class="footnote-marker">51</sup><i class="footnote">The Halakhah also appears in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Pesachim 3:3:10" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Pesachim.3.3.10"><i>Pesaḥim</i> 3:3 (Notes 90–97</a>) with the references to “here” and “there” switched correctly.<br><a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 22:28" href="/Leviticus.22.28"><i>Lev.</i> 22:28</a> states that it is prohibited to slaughter an animal and its young on the same day. Therefore, if both an animal and its young fell into a cistern on a holiday, only one of them can be potential food. The other one is <i>muqṣeh</i> and cannot be moved by humans. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Beitzah 37a" href="/Beitzah.37a">Babli 37a</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Pesachim 117b" href="/Pesachim.117b"><i>Pesaḥim</i>117b</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Beitzah (Lieberman) 3:2" href="/Tosefta_Beitzah_(Lieberman).3.2">Tosephta 3:2</a>.</i> fell into a cistern. Rebbi Eliezer said, he should lift the first one for the purpose of slaughtering it and slaughter it. The second one has to be provided for at its place so it should not die. Rebbi Joshua says, he should lift the first one for the purpose of slaughtering it and not slaughter it, and be cunning and lift the second one<sup class="footnote-marker">52</sup><i class="footnote">As long as it is not determined which animal is to be turned into food, both are potential food and can be moved.</i>. Even though he had no intention of slaughtering either of them, he is permitted<sup class="footnote-marker">53</sup><i class="footnote">He is permitted to declare both animals as potential food even though he had no intention of slaughtering either one.</i>. Rebbi Abun bar Ḥiyya asked: Is the argument of Rebbi Eliezer not inverted? Here he says, one is prohibited from cunning but there he says, one is permitted to be cunning<sup class="footnote-marker">54</sup><i class="footnote">In the matter of an animal and its young he requires strict adherence to the rules. By the biblical rules, a piece of bread dough has to be given to the Cohen under the rules of heave (cf. Introduction to Tractate <i>Ḥallah</i>). Impure <i>ḥallah</i> must be burned but unusable <i>sancta</i> may not be burned on the holiday and dough kneaded on the holiday would become leavened if stored for the next day. Therefore R. Eliezer instructs the baker to bake the <i>mazzah</i> completely and then remove a small part as <i>ḥallah</i>, a method frowned upon on any other day of the year. R. Joshua instructs to follow all the usual rules of <i>ḥallah</i> and burn the dough after the holiday since he holds that leavened matter is forbidden only to Jews, not to Heaven, and <i>ḥallah</i> is Heaven’s property.</i>. There it is because “not to be seen nor found”; here, what do you have<sup class="footnote-marker">55</sup><i class="footnote">In matters of dough on Passover there is no other way out (short of not making food on the holiday); in the matters of animals it is possible to follow all the rules.</i>? Is the argument of Rebbi Joshua not inverted? Here he says, one is permitted to be cunning, but there he says, one is prohibited from cunning<sup class="footnote-marker">56</sup><i class="footnote">For the animals in the cistern he allows a fake declaration which permits their rescue; for impure <i>ḥallah</i> he removes the prohibition by declaring it inapplicable.</i>. Rebbi Idi said, here it is a rabbinic Sabbath prohibition, but there liability for a purification offering<sup class="footnote-marker">57</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Muqṣeh</i> is rabbinic; eating bread without taking <i>ḥallah</i> is a deadly sin.</i>. Rebbi Yose ben Rebbi Bun said, here it is to protect Jews’ money; there what do you have<sup class="footnote-marker">58</sup><i class="footnote">The animals in the cistern are valuable; impure <i>ḥallah</i> is worthless.</i>? Rebbi Isaac and Rebbi Joshia, one like one of them, the other like the other<sup class="footnote-marker">59</sup><i class="footnote">Since R. Isaac and R. Josia are last generation Tannaim, their disagreement must refer to the difference between the first generation RR. Eliezer and Joshua.</i>.

Halakhah 5

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> An animal which dies<sup class="footnote-marker">60</sup><i class="footnote">And no longer is food; it became <i>muqṣeh</i> and may not be moved on the holiday.</i> one may not move from its place. It happened that Rebbi Tarphon was asked about this and about <i>ḥallah</i> which became impure<sup class="footnote-marker">61</sup><i class="footnote">Which may not be eaten anymore but cannot be burned on the holiday (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Beitzah 3:6:3" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Beitzah.3.6.3">Note 74</a>); therefore it is <i>muqṣeh</i>.</i>. He entered the study hall<sup class="footnote-marker">62</sup><i class="footnote">He must have been young, that he needed the instruction from elders. Therefore this decision has to be dated to Temple times.</i> and asked. They told him that one may not move them from their place.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> Samuel said, the five Elders who instructed Rebbi Tarphon in Lydda erred<sup class="footnote-marker">63</sup><i class="footnote">Since in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Shabbat 24:4:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Shabbat.24.4.1">Mishnah <i>Šabbat</i>24:4</a> the anonymous majority (following R. Simeon) permits to use the carcass of an animal which died on the Sabbath as dog food, while R. Jehudah permits that only for animals having died before the Sabbath, it is established that on the Sabbath practice follows R. Simeon who in most cases denies that the rules of <i>muqḥeh</i> apply. He holds in this statement that therefore on the holiday also one has to follow R. Simeon and treat the animal carcass as permitted dog food.</i>. Rav Mattanah said, the case concerned a firstling<sup class="footnote-marker">64</sup><i class="footnote">He holds that the Elders ruled correctly but that the case says nothing about the disagreement between RR. Jehudah and Simeon since a firstling, as <i>sanctum</i>, may not be used as dog food but must be buried (after the holiday). <a class="refLink" data-ref="Beitzah 27b" href="/Beitzah.27b">Babli 27b</a>.</i> Rebbi Abbin said, the Mishnah says so: “about this and about <i>ḥallah</i> which became impure.” Since that one was holy<sup class="footnote-marker">65</sup><i class="footnote">Impure <i>ḥallah</i> cannot be used for any profane purpose; in this it is equal to a dead firstling. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Beitzah 27b" href="/Beitzah.27b">Babli 27b</a>.</i>, so also this one was holy. They asked before Levi, may one inspect a torn animal in a dark house<sup class="footnote-marker">66</sup><i class="footnote">Whether the injuries make the animal unfit for human consumption.</i>? He said, bring it outside. Rebbi Immi asked, we heard that one does not inspect cases of skin disease in a dark house<sup class="footnote-marker">67</sup><i class="footnote">Mishnah <i>Nega`im</i>2:3. This rule is biblical since it is required that the Cohen see the diseased skin with his unaided eyes (<i>Lev.</i> 12:12).</i>. Did we hear that one does not inspect a torn animal in a dark house? Rebbi Yose ben Rebbi Abun said, it is not because of this reason, but lest it would be found <i>ṭerefah</i> and be forbidden to be moved<sup class="footnote-marker">68</sup><i class="footnote">Levi’s rule has no biblical root; it is not comparable to the inspection of skin disease. It is a practical advice to remove the animal from the house before it is declared unfit for human consumption and therefore <i>muqṣeh</i> for R. Jehudah.</i>. It turns out that Rav Mattanah follows Samuel, and Levi Rav<sup class="footnote-marker">69</sup><i class="footnote">Since R. Mattanah restricts the Mishnah to the case of a firstling, he holds that for profane animals one follows R. Simeon; which is Samuel’s position. But Levi who recommends that one take precautions that the animal not become disqualified as food must follow R. Jehudah.</i>. Rebbi Abba, Rav Jehudah in the name of Samuel said, practice follows Rebbi Jehudah<sup class="footnote-marker">70</sup><i class="footnote">Either Samuel changed his opinion, or one should read “Rav” instead of “Samuel”. In the Babli a statement of Rav Ḥisda, 28a.</i>.

Halakhah 6

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> One does not subscribe to an animal on the holiday but one subscribes to it on the eve of the holiday and then slaughters and distributes among them<sup class="footnote-marker">71</sup><i class="footnote">One may not say to the butcher on the holiday that he is willing to take so-and-so much meat from an animal which he slaughters on the holiday, but one may do so before the holiday for meat to be freshly slaughtered on the holiday.</i>. Rebbi Jehudah says a person may weigh against a vessel or against a dagger<sup class="footnote-marker">72</sup><i class="footnote">The weight of the meat cut for one person may not be determined professionally by scales and weight-stones but semi-professional by scales where instead of certified weights one uses articles of known weight. The Sages require that all distribution of the holiday be by estimate.</i>, but the Sages are saying that one does not use scales at all.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> Therefore adding one adds. If there were five they could be turned into ten<sup class="footnote-marker">73</sup><i class="footnote">It only is forbidden on the holiday to start lining up people to make it possible to slaughter an animal which is too much for one family to eat on the holiday; but if a group was formed to assure the butcher that all his meat will be taken on the holiday the subscribers may give of their part to others and so inform the butcher.</i>.
It was stated<sup class="footnote-marker">74</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Beitzah (Lieberman) 3:5" href="/Tosefta_Beitzah_(Lieberman).3.5">Tosephta 3:5</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Beitzah 27b" href="/Beitzah.27b">Babli 27b</a>.</i>: “A person may not say to another, I participate with you for a tetradrachma, I participate with you for a sheqel; but he may say to him, I am standing for half, third, or quarter<sup class="footnote-marker">75</sup><i class="footnote">No exact amounts may be mentioned on the holiday. Since “one half, a third, a quarter” of an animal or part of an animal are approximations, these expressions may be used.</i>.” The great Rebbi Ḥiyya and Rebbi Simeon ben Rebbi did weigh a firstling piece against piece<sup class="footnote-marker">76</sup><i class="footnote">As <i>sanctum</i> the firstling, slaughtered because it developed a defect and now permitted to everybody, may not be an object of trade. Therefore its meat may not be sold by weight. But after the meat was distributed, the parties may use scales to compare the weights of the pieces. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Beitzah 28a" href="/Beitzah.28a">Babli 28a</a>.</i>. When was this said? On a weekday. Therefore on a holiday it is forbidden because “one does not use scales at all.<sup class="footnote-marker">77</sup><i class="footnote">Since scales are tools of trade.</i>” Rav Avun, Rav Jehudah in the name of Samuel: Even to hang it on the scales because of the rats is forbidden<sup class="footnote-marker">78</sup><i class="footnote">In his interpretation, any use of scales, even in a non-professional way, is forbidden. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Beitzah 28a" href="/Beitzah.28a">Babli 28a</a>.</i>. Rebbi Yose ben Rebbi Abun said, not because of this reason but because we did state, “Rebbi Jehudah says a person may weigh against a vessel or against a dagger, but the Sages are saying that one does not use scales at all.” Therefore a first Tanna is of the opinion that one may use them, and therefore to hang it on the scales because of the rats is permitted<sup class="footnote-marker">79</sup><i class="footnote">He argues that the interpretation of Rav Jehudah in the name of Samuel is untenable. Since R. Jehudah finds it necessary to say that one may use scales to weigh in a non-professional way, the Sages say that one does not weigh using scales at all. Their statement says nothing about using scales for a use totally unconnected with weighing.</i>.
It was stated: Rabban Simeon ben Gamliel says, a person may calm himself by pounds<sup class="footnote-marker">80</sup><i class="footnote">Greek λίτρα, equivalent of Latin <i>libra</i>, “lb.”. While the use of scales in trade is forbidden, their use in private is permitted.</i>, to know what he got. As the following: Rebbi Mana bought Sabbatical carob fruit<sup class="footnote-marker">81</sup><i class="footnote">Sabbatical produce may not be sold by measure, weight, or count (Mishnah <i>Ševi`it</i>8:3), similar to the rules of holidays.</i>. He asked Rebbi Ḥizqiah, who answered him, the rabbis use to follow that of Rabban Simeon ben Gamliel<sup class="footnote-marker">82</sup><i class="footnote">The produce may be weighed in private, as long as no trade is involved. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Beitzah 28a" href="/Beitzah.28a">Babli 28a</a>.</i>.
It was stated<sup class="footnote-marker">83</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Beitzah (Lieberman) 3:5" href="/Tosefta_Beitzah_(Lieberman).3.5">Tosephta 3:5</a>, an Amoraic statement in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Beitzah 28a" href="/Beitzah.28a">Babli 28a</a>.</i>: “The butcher should not weigh in his hand and put down since his hand is like an lb. weight, but he may cut with a knife and give to each one<sup class="footnote-marker">84</sup><i class="footnote">He may give equal portions by sight but not by weighing even with his hands.</i>.”
It was stated<sup class="footnote-marker">85</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Bekhorot 33a" href="/Bekhorot.33a">Babli <i>Bekhorot</i>33a</a>.</i>: One does not strip by the feet on the holiday<sup class="footnote-marker">86</sup><i class="footnote">To keep the hide intact so it can be used after the holiday either to make bellows (or bagpipes) or to make them into water-, wine-, or oil skins.</i>, nor on a weekday of a firstling or disqualified <i>sancta</i><sup class="footnote-marker">87</sup><i class="footnote">Neither a disqualified firstling nor any other disqualified sacrificial animal may be used for work or trade. Using the skin is permitted since it is permitted even for qualified sacrifices (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 7:8" href="/Leviticus.7.8"><i>Lev.</i> 7:8</a>), but for the disqualified extra care has to be taken to treat them with respect.</i>. Rebbi Abun said, so he should not be like one who uses it for work when it is still living. Rebbi Justus bar Shunem said, they decided that one should not form herds from them<sup class="footnote-marker">88</sup><i class="footnote">For if these are raised in quantity they are done so with the idea of using their hides for commercial purposes and this is objectionable. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bekhorot 33b" href="/Bekhorot.33b">Babli <i>Bekhorot</i>33b</a>.</i>. Rebbi Mana said, everybody agrees that to make bellows is permitted, to beat them<sup class="footnote-marker">89</sup><i class="footnote">To make sure that all remaining particles of flesh can be removed before the entire hide is made into a container for water, wine, or oil.</i>, the Elder Rebbi Ḥiyya and Rebbi Simeon ben Rebbi, one said it is forbidden, the other said it is permitted. Rebbi Yose ben Rebbi Abun said, everybody agrees that to beat them is permitted, to make bellows, the Elder Rebbi Ḥiyya and Rebbi Simeon ben Rebbi, one said it is forbidden, the other said it is permitted<sup class="footnote-marker">90</sup><i class="footnote">Since there are two contradicting opinions, both actions are forbidden on the holiday.</i>.

Halakhah 7

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> One does not sharpen the knife<sup class="footnote-marker">91</sup><i class="footnote">Using a special grindstone to sharpen the butcher’s knife is workday procedure. Sharpening one knife by drawing it along another is not professional and therefore permitted. As noted in the Halakhah, this method also has the advantage of cleansing the knife from fat clinging to it which is a help in preparing new food and therefore permitted without question. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Beitzah 28a" href="/Beitzah.28a">Babli 28a</a>.</i> but he cleanses it<sup class="footnote-marker">92</sup><i class="footnote">Deriving the word not from Hebrew נשׂא “to lift” but Arabic מסי “to draw, to extract”, מס “to touch”.</i> on another. A person may not say to the butcher, weigh me meat for a denar, but he slaughters and they distribute among themselves<sup class="footnote-marker">93</sup><i class="footnote">The butcher gets paid for the entire animal (before or after the holiday); the distribution among the subscribers is not his business.</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “One does not sharpen the knife on the holiday but he cleanses it on another.” Rav Ḥisda said, this is Rebbi Jehudah’s<sup class="footnote-marker">114</sup><i class="footnote">Rav Ḥisda considers sharpening the knife not an action necessary to prepare food, but only an action preparatory to the preparation of food, and therefore for the opponents of Rebbi Jehudah not covered by the biblical instruction to prepare food on the holiday.<br>He must understand the text not as “he cleanses it” but “he draws it” (Note 112).</i>. Rav Jehudah in the name of Samuel said, it is everybody’s opinion, to remove the fat on it<sup class="footnote-marker">115</sup><i class="footnote">And therefore is action necessary for the preparation of food.</i>.

Halakhah 8

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> A person may say to the grocer, fill me this vessel, but he may not specify the measure<sup class="footnote-marker">96</sup><i class="footnote">In the absence of refrigerators, replenishing supplies from the grocery store on a holiday is permitted but it must be done in a non-professional way.</i>. Rebbi Jehudah says, if it was a measuring cup he shall not fill it. It happened that Shaul ben Botnit filled his measures on the eve of the holiday and gave it to the customers on the holiday<sup class="footnote-marker">97</sup><i class="footnote">He had vessels filled exactly to measures before the holiday when this was permitted, gave to his customers on the holiday, and was sure that he was not over-or undercharging them when settling the accounts after the holiday.</i>. Abba Shaul<sup class="footnote-marker">98</sup><i class="footnote">Abba Shaul the Sage is Shaul ben Botnit the grocer.</i> says, that is what one does on a holiday, but the Sages say one acts thus also on a weekday because of the emptying of the measuring vessels<sup class="footnote-marker">99</sup><i class="footnote">It is business practice recommended always to sell fluids in pre-filled containers since all fluids, even wine or milk, are somewhat viscous, and when the customer’s vessel is filled from the grocer’s measuring cup some drops for which the customer is charged will remain in the grocer’s possession. In a large business, these drops may add up to sizeable amounts as noted in the Halakhah (in particular for olive oil.)</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> Rav Huna said, either less or more. Rebbi Eleazar said to Zeura bar Ḥama, fill me this vessel and tomorrow we shall measure it<sup class="footnote-marker">100</sup><i class="footnote">Rav Huna forbids filling a vessel to the rim so that on the next day the amount given on the holiday can be exactly determined, with volume and price determined by estimation. R. Eleazar disagreed and instructed his grocer to fill to the rim.</i>.
It was stated<sup class="footnote-marker">101</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Beitzah (Lieberman) 3:8" href="/Tosefta_Beitzah_(Lieberman).3.8">Tosephta 3:8</a></i>: “Rebbi Eleazar ben Rebbi Ṣadoq and Abba Shaul ben Botnit were grocers in Jerusalem. They used to fill their measuring vessels on the eve of a holiday and give them to the customers on the holiday. Rebbi Ḥananiah ben Aqabiah says, they also acted in the same way on the intermediate days of the holiday because of the emptying of the measuring vessels<sup class="footnote-marker">102</sup><i class="footnote">In the Tosephta (by an other tradent, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Beitzah 29a" href="/Beitzah.29a">Babli 29a</a>) “not to miss the study house.” The meaning here seems to be the same, that they did not want to wait the long time until the last drop was fallen from their measuring vessel into the customer’s container.</i>. Also he collected 300 amphoras, and his colleague collected 300 amphoras<sup class="footnote-marker">103</sup><i class="footnote">Of olive oil remaining in the measuring cup.</i>. The Sages said to them, you did not have to do this, but since you were stringent with yourselves, they should be used for public needs.” Once he was sick and our teachers went to visit him. He said to them, you are seeing this right hand which was measuring in truth.
<sup class="footnote-marker">104</sup><i class="footnote">This text is from <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Shekalim 5:1:12" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Shekalim.5.1.12"><i>Šeqalim</i>5:2, Notes 39–41</a>.</i> Rebbi Ḥanina said, anybody who says that the All-Merciful is indulgent, his intestines shall dissolve themselves; for He is forbearing and then collects His due. Rebbi Aḥa said, it is written<sup class="footnote-marker">105</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Psalms 50:3" href="/Psalms.50.3"><i>Ps.</i> 50:3</a>.</i>, <i>His surroundings are very hairy</i>. He is exact with them like a hair’s width.
