Rebbi Yose said, not because of this reason, but because what is written<sup class="footnote-marker">106</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Psalms 89:8" href="/Psalms.89.8"><i>Ps.</i> 89:8</a>.</i>, <i>He is awesome on His surroundings</i>, His fear on those near Him is greater than those far away.

Halakhah 9

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> A person may say to another, give me eggs or walnuts by the count since it is the way of private persons to count these in his household<sup class="footnote-marker">107</sup><i class="footnote">Therefore mentioning the count in this case is not an act peculiar to trade and may be used on the holiday.<br>Mishnaiot 8 and 9 are different from those quoted in the Babli but agree with Maimonides’s autograph Mishnah.</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> It was stated: A person may say to another, give me a <i>kelah</i><sup class="footnote-marker">108</sup><i class="footnote">A measure of volume which is permitted to be used on the holiday since it is normal cooking practice to be very careful in the amount of spices used. This measure is not mentioned otherwise in the Talmudim but is defined in Kalir’s סִלּוּק לַפָרָשַׁת שְׁקָלִים as the difference between an <i>omer</i>(or <i>issaron</i>, the tenth of an <i>ephah</i>) and 7/4 of a <i>qab</i> or alternatively as a little less than one hundredth of a dry <i>seah</i> which is 2/3 of a fluid <i>seah</i>.</i> of spices since it is the way of private persons to give a <i>kelah</i> of spices into his dish.

Chapter 4



Halakhah 1

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> He who transports wine jugs from place to place may not transport them in a basket or box<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">On the holiday one may not transport the way it is done on a working day.</i>, but he may transport on his shoulder in front of him<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">In the other sources of this Mishnah: on his shoulder <i>or</i> in front of him. The sentence is not discussed in the Halakhah but it seems that here also this has to be understood.</i>. He who transports straw may not tie the box and throw it over his shoulder but has to take it into his hand<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">On the holiday one may not transport the way it is done on a working day.</i>. One may start from a pile of straw<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">Straw stored in an orderly way not particularly prepared for the holiday nevertheless always is considered prepared for the holiday, clean straw as animal feed, straw mixed with dirt and pebbles as fuel.</i> but not from wood in storage<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">This storage is not the original <i>muqṣeh</i>, the place on the flat roof where cut-up figs are spread out to dry, but a separate storage facility for wood, either building material or object of trade.</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “He who transports wine jugs,” etc. Rebbi Ḥanin bar Levi said, the Mishnah is about these little flasks<sup class="footnote-marker">11</sup><i class="footnote">A word of unknown etymology. Brüll reads Latin <i>calfar</i>, “wine container”.</i> of which one carries two or three in a hand. If it is one big one, in front of him it is usual, on the back it is usual<sup class="footnote-marker">12</sup><i class="footnote">Since the Mishnah prohibits transporting wine jars the way it is done on working days, it seems impossible to transport a large wine receptacle on the holiday. The <a class="refLink" data-ref="Beitzah 30a" href="/Beitzah.30a">Babli disagrees, 30a</a>.</i>. Just as you are saying, he brings it with its hide, similarly he brings it with its jar<sup class="footnote-marker">13</sup><i class="footnote">Since in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Beitzah 3:3:2" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Beitzah.3.3.2">Halakhah 3:3</a> it was stated that one may transport the hide because of the meat left on it, so one may add to the large wine receptacle a small serving pitcher, put both into a basket or chest, and transport them together.</i>. Just as you are saying, he brings it limb by limb, similarly he brings it vial by vial<sup class="footnote-marker">14</sup><i class="footnote">Since it was stated in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Beitzah 3:3:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Beitzah.3.3.1">Mishnah 3:3</a> that one may transport an entire carcass limb by limb, so one is permitted to distribute the wine in the amphora into several small pitchers and transport these. The remark is not trivial since it states that it is better to increase the exertion on the holiday (by repeating the trip many times) than to transport one time in the way it is done on weekdays.</i>.
Rebbi Yudan asked, may one move straw from a private domain to the public domain<sup class="footnote-marker">15</sup><i class="footnote">Since on the Sabbath transporting from a private to a public domain is a biblical Sabbath violation, on a holiday only transporting between domains for the purposes of preparing or serving food is permitted. (The Babli disagrees and holds that transporting for any legitimate purpose is permitted.)</i>? Is that not the Mishnah, “he who transports straw may not tie the box and throw it over his shoulder”? The Mishnah follows him who says, <i>for all breathing</i>, also animal’s breathing is included<sup class="footnote-marker">16</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Beitzah 1:11:3" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Beitzah.1.11.3">Chapter 1, Note 262</a>. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Beitzah 21" href="/Beitzah.21">Babli 21a/b</a>.</i>. What is his problem? Following him who says, animal’s breathing is not included. Also for him who says, animal’s breathing is included, the Mishnah is about transporting as feed. What is his problem? If he transports as fuel<sup class="footnote-marker">17</sup><i class="footnote">Since we follow the opinion that animal feed is equal to human food on the holiday, the first answer cannot be correct. If straw is transported as fuel for cooking food or feed, is the transporting preparation of food or only preliminary to the preparation of food? If it is transported to sustain an existing fire for warmth, is the fire eventually usable for cooking?</i>.
Rebbi Jacob bar Aḥa in the name of Rebbi Yasa: It is forbidden to touch dried <i>Muqṣeh</i><sup class="footnote-marker">18</sup><i class="footnote">This <i>Muqseh</i> is the original meaning of the word, cut-up figs lying on the roof to ferment and dry. If their status was not checked before the holiday they are not “prepared” and cannot be checked on the holiday.</i>.
<sup class="footnote-marker">19</sup><i class="footnote">The text also is in <i>Ma`serot</i>1:7, Notes 192–197 (<b>ע</b>).</i> Rebbi said to his son Rebbi Simeon, climb up and bring us dried figs from the upper floor. He said to him, is it not forbidden because of <i>muqṣeh</i>? He retorted, are you still holding that? Nothing but figs and grapes can be forbidden because of <i>muqzeh</i><sup class="footnote-marker">20</sup><i class="footnote">All other produce in drying becomes edible and permitted. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Shabbat 45a" href="/Shabbat.45a">Babli <i>Šabbat</i>45a</a>, a statement of Samuel in the name of R. Simeon.</i>! Rebbi Samuel bar Sosartai: Because they stink in between. Rebbi Ze`ira asked before Rebbi Yasa: Is it not reasonable to hold that the case involved split dates<sup class="footnote-marker">21</sup><i class="footnote">The original story involved figs which as stated become inedible in the process of drying and whose status therefore has to be checked. But unripe (or never ripe) dates which are spread out on the roof to mature and become sweet never pass through a phase during which they are inedible. They would be a prime example for food left to dry which is never <i>muqṣeh</i>.</i>? He said to him, that is my opinion also. Rebbi Yose ben Rebbi Abun, Rebbi Isaac bar Bisna in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan: The case involved split dates.
“He said to him, nothing but figs and grapes can be forbidden because of <i>muqṣeh</i>!” Rebbi Jacob bar Zavdi in the name of Rebbi Abbahu: that is, concerning the Sabbath. But concerning tithes, everything may pass through a stage of <i>muqṣeh</i>. Rebbi Yose said, the Mishnah said so<sup class="footnote-marker">22</sup><i class="footnote">Mishnah <i>Ma`serot</i>1:8. While dried figs can be used as soon as they are edible again, if he had the intention from the start to store the finished produce in an amphora or a bin, the drying process is irrelevant for tithes since any produce put out to dry is free from tithes until it is brought to its final storage.</i>: “Dried figs when he presses them and in a bin if he planes with a roller.”

Halakhah 2

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> One does not take wood from a hut<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">Any hut, not necessarily the <i>sukkah</i> of Tabernacles. A hut is a building and removing logs from it is wrecking it, a biblical violation both on the Sabbath and a holiday.</i> but only from what is leaning on it<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">Logs not permanently attached to the hut are not part of it and may be removed if needed.</i>. One brings wood from the field from what was collected<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">Logs arranged in orderly heaps are arranged by humans for use by humans and therefore prepared for use on a holiday. Logs strewn around in disorderly fashion on meadows or in forests are not arranged by humans and therefore may not be collected on the holiday.</i>, but from a corral even what is strewn about. What is a corral<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote">From which wood may be taken even if strewn around.</i>? Any close to the village, the words of Rebbi Jehudah. Rebbi Yose says, any into which one enters with a key<sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote">Everything in such a space is there for some use and may be used on the holiday without restriction.</i>, even inside the Sabbath domain<sup class="footnote-marker">10</sup><i class="footnote">Even if the corral is close to 2’000 cubits from the village and few people would venture so far on a holiday.</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “One does not take wood from a hut<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">Any hut, not necessarily the <i>sukkah</i> of Tabernacles. A hut is a building and removing logs from it is wrecking it, a biblical violation both on the Sabbath and a holiday.</i> but only from what is leaning on it<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">Logs not permanently attached to the hut are not part of it and may be removed if needed.</i>.” Samuel said, if he did not intend to fortify it<sup class="footnote-marker">23</sup><i class="footnote">The additional logs were not part of the plan in making the hut. But if e. g. the logs were needed to protect the hut from strong winds they become part of the building although they are not fastened to the walls. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Beitzah 30b" href="/Beitzah.30b">Babli 30b</a>.</i>. But if he intended to fortify it it is forbidden, unless he stipulated about it<sup class="footnote-marker">24</sup><i class="footnote">Even if the logs are needed to protect the walls, as long as they are not fastened to them they may be removed if they were put in place with the explicit stipulation that they may be removed on the holiday since then they do not become part of the building.</i>. Therefore if he stipulated it is permitted. Rav Naḥman bar Jacob asked, may a person stipulate to tear down tents<sup class="footnote-marker">25</sup><i class="footnote">Since tearing down a building is a biblical Sabbath violation and therefore also a biblical holiday desecration; no stipulation should be possible.</i>? One said before Samuel, if it was pasted on it<sup class="footnote-marker">26</sup><i class="footnote">Usually טפול is used for make-up or medicinal applications. Here it must mean that the logs may be removed even if they are lightly glued together or to the wall, if they may be removed without effort. In that case Rav Naḥman has a valid point; the additional statement has to be rejected.</i>, and Rav Naḥman bar Jacob was wondering about it.
It was stated<sup class="footnote-marker">27</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Beitzah (Lieberman) 3:10" href="/Tosefta_Beitzah_(Lieberman).3.10">Tosephta 3:10</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Beitzah 31a" href="/Beitzah.31a">Babli 31a</a>. There the name is R. Simeon ben Eleazar.</i>: “Rebbi Eleazar ben Rebbi Simeon said, the Houses of Shammai and Hillel did not disagree that one brings wood from the field from what was collected<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">Logs arranged in orderly heaps are arranged by humans for use by humans and therefore prepared for use on a holiday. Logs strewn around in disorderly fashion on meadows or in forests are not arranged by humans and therefore may not be collected on the holiday.</i>, but from a corral even what was strewn about. Where did they disagree? About what is strewn about in a field, where the House of Shammai forbid but the House of Hillel permit.
<sup class="footnote-marker">28</sup><i class="footnote">From here on there exists a Genizah text edited by L. Ginzberg (G, pp. 304–305).</i> Rebbi Nathan said, The House of Shammai agrees with the House of Hillel that one brings wood from the field from what was collected<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">Logs arranged in orderly heaps are arranged by humans for use by humans and therefore prepared for use on a holiday. Logs strewn around in disorderly fashion on meadows or in forests are not arranged by humans and therefore may not be collected on the holiday.</i>, and from a corral what was collected. Where did they disagree? About what is strewn about in a corral, where the House of Shammai forbid but the House of Hillel permit.” Rebbi Yose in the name of Rav Jeremiah, Rebbi Ḥaninah brings it in the name of Rav: practice follows the person who restricts, [including]<sup class="footnote-marker">29</sup><i class="footnote">Corrector’s addition, confirmed by G.</i> what was strewn about in a corral<sup class="footnote-marker">30</sup><i class="footnote">One follows the Mishnah. The <a class="refLink" data-ref="Beitzah 31a" href="/Beitzah.31a">Babli 31a</a> disagrees in the name of <i>Rebbi</i> Jeremiah.</i>. <sup class="footnote-marker">31</sup><i class="footnote">This parallels <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Eruvin 1:4:4" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Eruvin.1.4.4"><i>Eruvin</i> Chapter 1, Notes 193–194</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Beitzah 2:6:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Beitzah.2.6.1">Chapter 2 Note 93</a>.</i> The opinion of Rebbi Jehudah seems inverted, as we have stated there<sup class="footnote-marker">32</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Eruvin 2:6:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Eruvin.2.6.1">Mishnah <i>Eruvin</i>2:6</a>.</i>, “[Rebbi Jehudah says]<sup class="footnote-marker">29</sup><i class="footnote">Corrector’s addition, confirmed by G.</i>, even if it only contains a cistern, or an irrigation ditch, or a cave, one carries in it..” And here he says so? Rebbi Mana said, Rebbi Jehudah treated it as a house<sup class="footnote-marker">33</sup><i class="footnote">The cistern or cave is treated as a house and the corral as the house’s yard where carrying is permitted.</i>.

Halakhah 3

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> One does not split logs<sup class="footnote-marker">34</sup><i class="footnote">Building materials which are not intended to be used as fire wood and therefore are <i>muqṣeh</i>.</i> nor a log which broke on the holiday<sup class="footnote-marker">35</sup><i class="footnote">Since the log was whole and therefore <i>muqṣeh</i> at the start of the holiday, it remains in that state during the holiday even if the reason for it disappears.</i>. Also one does split neither with a hatchet, nor with a sickle, nor with a plane, but with a dagger. From a room full of produce which was blocked but became accessible one takes from the opening. Rebbi Meïr says, also one freshly makes accessible and takes<sup class="footnote-marker">36</sup><i class="footnote">He holds that on a holiday one needs food, one may open the storage facility in any way one sees fit.</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “One does not split logs nor a log which broke.” That is, a log which broke on the holiday. But a log which broke on the eve of a holiday is permitted if it cannot be called an implement. But if it can be called an implement even if it broke on the eve of a holiday it is forbidden<sup class="footnote-marker">37</sup><i class="footnote">Building material which broke before the holiday and cannot be used any more automatically is classified as fuel. Since preparing food on the holiday is permitted, fuel is needed and the broken log is usable on the holiday. But if the broken log still may be used in any way as an implement, it is worth more as an implement than as fuel and may be used as the latter only if it was designated as such before the start of the holiday.</i>.
It was stated<sup class="footnote-marker">38</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Beitzah (Lieberman) 4:2" href="/Tosefta_Beitzah_(Lieberman).4.2">Tosephta 4:2</a>.</i>: “Branches of a fig tree and on them figs, broomsticks {from a date palm}<sup class="footnote-marker">39</sup><i class="footnote">Added from G, not in the Tosephta. Palm branches with open leaves are called “broomsticks” since that is what they always are used for.</i> and on them dates, which he collected for food, he eats from them on the holiday. If he collected them as wood<sup class="footnote-marker">40</sup><i class="footnote">Then in his mind the fruits are not food at the start of the holiday and this status cannot change on the holiday.</i>, he may not eat from them on the holiday.”
Rav said, one heats with implements, one does not heat with broken pieces of implements<sup class="footnote-marker">41</sup><i class="footnote">Since implements are never <i>muqṣeh</i> (except expensive tools of trade, Mishnah <i>Šabbat</i>17:4–5), they can be used for any purpose on the holiday. Broken pieces usually are <i>muqṣeh</i>. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Shabbat 124b" href="/Shabbat.124b">Babli <i>Šabbat</i>124b</a>.</i>. That is, vessels which broke on the holiday. But vessels which broke on the eve of the holiday are permitted if they cannot be called implements<sup class="footnote-marker">42</sup><i class="footnote">Same argument as in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Beitzah 4:3:2" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Beitzah.4.3.2">Note 37</a>.</i>. But if they can be called implements even if they broke on the holiday they are permitted<sup class="footnote-marker">43</sup><i class="footnote">As long as the broken piece is still usable in some way, it is an implement and cannot be <i>muqṣeh</i>.</i>.
Rebbi Ḥiyya stated: One may heat with edibles, but not with their shells or pits<sup class="footnote-marker">44</sup><i class="footnote">Since food is not <i>muqṣeh</i> it may be used in any way one desires. But shells and pits are waste and have the same status as broken logs.</i>. Rav was sitting before the Elder Rebbi Ḥiyya and was eating. He was seen throwing into the stove and in front of the stove<sup class="footnote-marker">45</sup><i class="footnote">The pits and shells of the fruits he ate.</i>. The Elder Rebbi Ḥiyya said to him, throw in this way<sup class="footnote-marker">46</sup><i class="footnote">He required that all waste be thrown into the oven.</i>. He asked him, what is the difference between this way and that way? He said to him, pasha’s son, behind them, behind them<sup class="footnote-marker">47</sup><i class="footnote">Since food waste is unpleasant it has to be disposed of so it is not seen.</i>. Rebbi Ḥanina said, he was throwing food<sup class="footnote-marker">48</sup><i class="footnote">He only sucked the sap of the fruit and disposed of the dry pulp.</i>. The Babylonian Rebbi Immi<sup class="footnote-marker">49</sup><i class="footnote">He is R. Immi mentioned elsewhere without the predicate “Babylonian”.</i> said, he was sick<sup class="footnote-marker">50</sup><i class="footnote">And therefore could use the fruit only partially what for a healthy person would have been prohibited waste.</i>. He said to him, in this way it would be forbidden on the holiday<sup class="footnote-marker">51</sup><i class="footnote">Even if some pulp was left on the fruit, since it could be used neither by the eater nor by any other person its status on the holiday would not be different from shells and pits. The story is an illustration of R. Ḥiyya’s <i>baraita</i>.</i>.
“Neither with a hatchet.” In the House of Rebbi Yannai they are saying, one splits with a hatchet<sup class="footnote-marker">52</sup><i class="footnote">Here practice is decided against the Mishnah. In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Beitzah 31b" href="/Beitzah.31b">Babli 31b</a> using a hatchet is only permitted if it is done in an unprofessional way.</i>. Rebbi Hoshaia stated, one splits with a hatchet. Samuel said, practice follows him who said, one splits with a hatchet. It was stated: Rebbi Yose ben Kipper said in the name of Rebbi Eleazar ben Shamua, the House of Shammai forbid but the House of Hillel permit.
“Nor with a sickle.” There are Tannaim who state that one splits, and [there are Tannaim who state that one does not split. Rav Ḥisda said,]<sup class="footnote-marker">29</sup><i class="footnote">Corrector’s addition, confirmed by G.</i> he who said one splits, with a hand-held sickle; he who says one does not split, with a scythe<sup class="footnote-marker">53</sup><i class="footnote">Any tool which also is used by the non-professional may be used, but not a tool only used by professionals.</i>.
<sup class="footnote-marker">54</sup><i class="footnote">This paragraph, the only one in the present series, does not refer to the rules of holidays but to those of a <i>nazir</i>(<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Nazir 6:3:8" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Nazir.6.3.8"><i>Nazir</i>6:3 Note 169</a>). One of the obligations of the <i>nazir</i> is to let his hair grow; he is forbidden to tear out a single hair. Therefore it is a question whether he may rub his hair with coarse cloth (in this case Cilician, κιλίκιον). It is permitted only for persons with healthy hair.</i> There are Tannaim who state that one separates with Cilician cloth, and there are Tannaim who state that one does not separate. Rav Ḥisda said, he who said one separates, for healthy {hair}; he who said one does not separate, for weak {hair}.
There are Tannaim who state that one rubs aromatic wood<sup class="footnote-marker">55</sup><i class="footnote">Aromatic wood is not food; therefore it may not be processed on a holiday. But if it is possible to produce perfume simply by rubbing aromatic wood in one’s hand without applying pressure, it is permitted. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Beitzah 33b" href="/Beitzah.33b">Babli 33b</a>.</i>; there are Tannaim who state that one does not rub. He who said one rubs, moist ones; he who said one does not rub, dry ones.
There are Tannaim who state that one sweetens mustard seed on glowing coals; there are Tannaim who state that one does not sweeten. He who said one sweetens, if he puts it on the coals; he who said one does not sweeten, if he puts the coals on it<sup class="footnote-marker">56</sup><i class="footnote">A similar statement is in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Shabbat 134a" href="/Shabbat.134a">Babli <i>Šabbat</i>134a</a> but the explanation given there should not be applied to the statement here. It seems that putting coals on mustard seed will extinguish the coals while burning the mustard seed, not sweetening it.</i>.
There are Tannaim who state that one rubs silver with chalk<sup class="footnote-marker">57</sup><i class="footnote">This is the modern interpretation starting with Musaphia (Greek κρητυκόν). The Geonic interpretation is “potash”.</i>; there are Tannaim who state that one does not rub. He who said one rubs, if he puts it on before the start of the holiday; he who said one does not rub, if he puts it on during the holiday.
“Rebbi Meïr says, also one freshly makes accessible and takes<sup class="footnote-marker">36</sup><i class="footnote">He holds that on a holiday one needs food, one may open the storage facility in any way one sees fit.</i>.” Rebbi Abba, Rav Jehudah in the name of Samuel: But only at the place of access<sup class="footnote-marker">58</sup><i class="footnote">Still one is obligated to minimize the amount of work expended for providing the food.</i>. And so it was stated<sup class="footnote-marker">59</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Beitzah (Lieberman) 3:12" href="/Tosefta_Beitzah_(Lieberman).3.12">Tosephta 3:12</a>; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Beitzah 5:2:2-16" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Beitzah.5.2.2-16">Yerushalmi 5:2</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Shabbat 15:2:3" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Shabbat.15.2.3"><i>Šabbat</i> 15 (Note 22</a>), 20 (Note 66), <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Megillah 1:6:2-5" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Megillah.1.6.2-5"><i>Megillah</i>1:8</a>. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Beitzah 31b" href="/Beitzah.31b">Babli 31b</a>.</i> “The Sages agree with Rebbi Meïr about cachets in the ground that one pushes aside, and removes, and unties, and cuts. On the Sabbath one pushes aside but one does not remove nor untie nor cut<sup class="footnote-marker">60</sup><i class="footnote">If produce is stored in the ground and the door tied by a rope, the knot (“cachet”) is not permanent since produce is stored to be used eventually. If on a holiday one needs food, one may open the storage facility in any way one sees fit. In a similar case on the Sabbath one may push the rope to open a cleft through which one can take out some produce but rabbinically one may not otherwise remove the knot even though it is not permanent and removing it from an installation in the ground is not building or tearing down by biblical standards.</i>. For implements on the Sabbath it is permitted<sup class="footnote-marker">61</sup><i class="footnote">Since the notions of building and tearing down buildings does not apply to implements by biblical standards, there is no place for rabbinic restrictions.</i>; it is not necessary to mention on a holiday.”

Halakhah 4

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> One does not open the candle<sup class="footnote-marker">62</sup><i class="footnote">This candle is a clay vessel to be put on the top of a candelabrum. It consists of a container for oil and a neck through which the wick is drawn to burn at a safe distance from the oil. If it is new and the opening for the wick is not excavated it cannot be made to work on the holiday since this would be “hitting with hammer”, the formal end of a production process, and therefore a biblical violation both on the holiday and the Sabbath (Mishnah <i>Šabbat</i>7:4). Since making fire is necessary to prepare food and therefore permitted, for the House of Hillel it is permitted on the holiday for any legitimate reason.</i> because he would make it a vessel nor does one make charcoal<sup class="footnote-marker">63</sup><i class="footnote">Not even for use on the same day (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Beitzah (Lieberman) 3:14" href="/Tosefta_Beitzah_(Lieberman).3.14">Tosephta 3:14</a>) since preparation of fuel is not included in the permission to use fuel.</i>, and one does not cut a wick into two. Rebbi Jehudah says, one may cut it in the fire for two candles<sup class="footnote-marker">64</sup><i class="footnote">As explained in the Halakhah one may take a long wick, put each end into such a candle and light then in the middle. Then this one action creates two separate burning candles.</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> It was stated: Closed pans<sup class="footnote-marker">66</sup><i class="footnote">Greek λοπάς “pan, plate”.</i>, if he made them before the holiday he may not open them on the holiday since he would be like one who makes a vessel usable on the holiday<sup class="footnote-marker">67</sup><i class="footnote">The pan is a flat vessel made of clay. It is made together with its cover. It is not fired in a kiln but it is expected to become a permanent vessel by being used over the fire in the kitchen. Before it is used it is completely closed, the cover being connected to the pan by a very thin strip of clay which can be easily cut with a kitchen knife. For this Tanna, cutting the strip is a prime example of “hitting with hammer” and forbidden both on the holiday and on the Sabbath. In the slightly differently worded <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Beitzah (Lieberman) 3:13" href="/Tosefta_Beitzah_(Lieberman).3.13">Tosephta 3:13</a> (and Tosephta <i>Šabbat</i>, ed. Liebermann 16:13, in Liebermann’s opinion referring only to holiday) it is reported that Rabban Simeon ben Gamliel permits to separate the cover from the pan. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Beitzah 32a" href="/Beitzah.32a">R. Ḥananel (<i>Besah</i>32a</a>, 5 lines from bottom) and <i>Sefer Ha`iṭtur</i>(vol. 2, fol. 150a) read this also in the Yerushalmi text. The statement must have fallen out of our text by scribal error since in the next paragraph a minority opinion in this <i>baraita</i> is mentioned, referring to Rabban Simeon ben Gamliel’s opinion, who holds that the pan with its cover form a completed vessel and the separation of cover from pan is to be compared to opening a tightly closed door (explanation of S. Liebermann.)</i>.
There, we have stated<sup class="footnote-marker">68</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Mishnah Eduyot 2:5" href="/Mishnah_Eduyot.2.5">Mishnah <i>Idiut</i>2:5</a>, missing the introduction, that R. Ismael did not indicate his position regarding the difference between the anonymous Tanna and R. Eleazar ben R. Ṣadoq.</i>: “About rural pans<sup class="footnote-marker">69</sup><i class="footnote">Pans made of unfired clay with cover attached.</i>, that they are pure in a tent with a corpse<sup class="footnote-marker">70</sup><i class="footnote">A clay vessel cannot become impure from the outside. A clay vessel which does not have a cavity is immune to impurity (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Mishnah Kelim 2:7" href="/Mishnah_Kelim.2.7">Mishnah <i>Kelim</i>2:7</a>). Since the unused rural pan is totally closed, it is immune to the impurity of the dead by biblical decree, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 19:15" href="/Numbers.19.15"><i>Num</i>. 19:15</a>.</i> but impure if carried by a person afflicted with gonorrhea<sup class="footnote-marker">71</sup><i class="footnote">Any implement moved by a sufferer from gonorrhea is severely impure, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 15:10" href="/Leviticus.15.10"><i>Lev.</i> 15:10</a>, even if the sufferer never touches the implement and moves it on a flat piece of wood (or clay) impervious to impurity.</i>. Rebbi Eleazar ben Rebbi Ṣadoq says, even if carried by a person afflicted with gonorrhea they are pure since their production was not finished<sup class="footnote-marker">72</sup><i class="footnote">He holds that as long as the cover is not separated from the pan the manufacturing process is not finished and therefore the composite is not an implement to which the rules of impurity may apply.</i>.” It turns out that the minority opinion here corresponds to the majority opinion there and the minority opinion there corresponds to the majority opinion here<sup class="footnote-marker">73</sup><i class="footnote">R. Eleazar ben R. Sadoq must agree that separating cover from pot is “hitting with hammer”. Rabban Simeon ben Gamliel must agree that the unopened clay vessel is an implement and subject to impurity by being moved.</i>.
Rebbi Abba in the name of Rav Jehudah: A splinter one leans on the wall lest it scratches<sup class="footnote-marker">74</sup><i class="footnote">If a splinter of wood constitutes a danger it can be moved even though it is <i>muqṣeh</i>.</i>. It was stated: One does not extinguish a splinter to save it; if to prevent it filling the house or the pot with smoke it is permitted<sup class="footnote-marker">75</sup><i class="footnote">If the splinter is burning and represents a nuisance or a danger it may be extinguished even though in general extinguishing a fire is forbidden on the holiday (unless needed in a cooking process). <a class="refLink" data-ref="Beitzah 22a" href="/Beitzah.22a">Babli 22a</a>.</i>. Rebbi Ḥanania said, if there is no empty space<sup class="footnote-marker">76</sup><i class="footnote">Greek ʼάηρ.</i> there. But if there is empty space there one throws it into the empty space and this is enough<sup class="footnote-marker">77</sup><i class="footnote">If the fire can safely be left to burn out by itself, extinguishing it is forbidden.</i>. Rebbi Nasa said, is this correct? Are we saying so, salve<sup class="footnote-marker">78</sup><i class="footnote">Greek κολλύριον.</i> one is permitted to rub on to use kohl on the holiday<sup class="footnote-marker">79</sup><i class="footnote">Since this is forbidden on the Sabbath and is not connected with food it must be forbidden on the holiday.</i>? Rebbi Mana said, one is to guard, the other for healing<sup class="footnote-marker">80</sup><i class="footnote">The argument should not even have started. Since making a fire is required in the preparation of food, measures to guard against the dangers of fire must be permitted. But medical procedures other than those necessary to save lives are prohibited on Sabbath and holidays. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Beitzah 22a" href="/Beitzah.22a">Babli 22a</a>.</i>.
<sup class="footnote-marker">81</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Beitzah (Lieberman) 3:21" href="/Tosefta_Beitzah_(Lieberman).3.21">Tosephta 3:21</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Beitzah 32b" href="/Beitzah.32b">Babli 32b</a>.</i>“About a wick three things were said as restrictions and three as leniencies. The following three were said as restrictions: One does not make it new on the holiday, nor does one cut one into two pieces, nor does one singe it by fire<sup class="footnote-marker">82</sup><i class="footnote">To make it easier to be lit.</i>. The following three were said as leniencies: One softens it<sup class="footnote-marker">83</sup><i class="footnote">By working it with his fingers.</i>, and soaks it in oil, and divides it into two lights by fire.” Rebbi Ḥinena said, if he needs both of them. It was stated, Rebbi Jehudah bar Pazi in the name of Bar Delaia: He brings two candles, introduces it into both of them, and separates them by fire into two candles<sup class="footnote-marker">64</sup><i class="footnote">As explained in the Halakhah one may take a long wick, put each end into such a candle and light then in the middle. Then this one action creates two separate burning candles.</i>. Rebbi Jacob bar Aḥa, Ḥinena bar Idi in the name of Rav: One cleans the wick<sup class="footnote-marker">84</sup><i class="footnote">Cutting off the burned-out part.</i> on a holiday.

Halakhah 5

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> One does not break earthenware nor cut paper to grill on them salted fish<sup class="footnote-marker">85</sup><i class="footnote">One does not break a potsherd in two to use part of it on a grill to protect the fish from being burned by the irons of the grill. According to Maimonides, for the same purpose one uses a special kind of paper impregnated with oil which will not burn as long as it is still moist.</i>. One does not clean out stove or cooking range but one smooths<sup class="footnote-marker">86</sup><i class="footnote">One may not clean out all ashes from a stove but one may smooth the surface of the ashes to have a level place to put in new wood or charcoal.</i>. One does not arrange two amphoras to cook a pot on them<sup class="footnote-marker">87</sup><i class="footnote">If one does not have a stove, one may not on the holiday create one by placing two amphoras side by side with space between them for fire wood and then put the pot between them to be cooked by the fire started between the amphoras.</i>. One does not support a pot by a splinter, nor the door<sup class="footnote-marker">88</sup><i class="footnote">This statement is valid only for those who hold that pieces of wood are prepared only to be fuel but nothing else.</i>. One does not drive an animal with a stick on the holiday<sup class="footnote-marker">89</sup><i class="footnote">Since this is herders’ practice on working days.</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> The sausage maker cannot do it on the holiday since he is cutting bast<sup class="footnote-marker">90</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Shabbat 7:2:28" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Shabbat.7.2.28"><i>Šabbat</i>7, Note 292</a>.</i>.
<sup class="footnote-marker">91</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Beitzah 32b" href="/Beitzah.32b">Babli 32b</a>, reported about his wife (who did not have the scholarly reputation of her daughter.)</i> The daughter of the Elder Rebbi Ḥiyya went to bake in the stove. She found a heap in it. She came and asked her father who said to her, go and empty it. She said, I cannot do it<sup class="footnote-marker">92</sup><i class="footnote">Since it is forbidden in the Mishnah.</i>. He said to her, go and smoothen it. She had known this<sup class="footnote-marker">93</sup><i class="footnote">If she knew the Mishnah, she must have known that smoothing the ashes is permitted.</i> but she wanted to hear it from her father<sup class="footnote-marker">94</sup><i class="footnote">Whether he had any modifications of the Mishnah.</i>
Rav said, one transfers from oven to stove but not from stove to oven<sup class="footnote-marker">95</sup><i class="footnote">The oven is a clay vessel in the form of a truncated cone. In the draft generated from the fire, the temperature is higher than that on top of an open stove. One may transfer completely cooked meals on a lesser fire to keep them hot. No reason is given why the inverse transfer is not permitted.</i>. Rav Abba bar Ḥana<sup class="footnote-marker">96</sup><i class="footnote">Rav’s second cousin. The entire discussion is about their family’s common usage.</i> said, one transfers from oven to stove and from stove to oven but not from evening to morning. Rav Avun in the name of Rav: One transfers from oven to stove, from stove to oven, and from evening to morning, but not from holiday to weekday<sup class="footnote-marker">97</sup><i class="footnote">It is generally accepted that the permission (or obligation) to cook on the holiday refers only to holiday food. It is forbidden to intentionally cook on the holiday for a following weekday.</i>.
“One does not arrange two amphoras to cook a pot on them.” Because of building<sup class="footnote-marker">98</sup><i class="footnote">Not building a permanent structure but fabricating an implement, violating “hitting with hammer”.</i>. It was stated<sup class="footnote-marker">99</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Beitzah (Lieberman) 3:17" href="/Tosefta_Beitzah_(Lieberman).3.17">Tosephta 3:17</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Beitzah 25b" href="/Beitzah.25b">Babli 25b</a>.</i>: “A blind person may not go out with his stick, nor the shepherd with his bag, but Rebbi Simeon permits.” So was Rebbi Simeon saying: Since one thing is permitted to him, let him be permitted everything<sup class="footnote-marker">100</sup><i class="footnote">The anonymous author forbids to the blind and the shepherd to go in public the same way they do on weekdays. R. Simeon objects since the blind man and the shepherd can freely move their implements in the house; there is no reason to forbid the same in public.</i>.

Halakhah 6

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> Rebbi Eliezer says, a person takes a chip to use as a toothpick<sup class="footnote-marker">101</sup><i class="footnote">Even though the chip either was intended as fuel or was regarded as waste. One may not take a chip from any wood connected to the ground.</i> and collects from the courtyard and ignites<sup class="footnote-marker">102</sup><i class="footnote">Uses as firewood to start a fire. R. Eliezer permits to sweep the entire courtyard to collect the little pieces of wood which are useful to start fires.</i> since anything in the courtyard is prepared, but the Sages are saying one collects from what is in front of him<sup class="footnote-marker">103</sup><i class="footnote">They agree that slivers of wood in house or courtyard are never <i>muqṣeh</i> but they do not authorize sweeping large areas to collect them. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Beitzah 33b" href="/Beitzah.33b">Babli 33b</a>.</i> and ignites.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> What is between them? To make a heap. In Rebbi Eliezer’s opinion one may make a heap, in the rabbis’ opinion one may not make a heap.

Halakhah 7

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> One makes fire neither with stones<sup class="footnote-marker">106</sup><i class="footnote">One may not generate sparks by hitting flintstones one on the other or on a metal surface. It could have said that one does not start a new fire in any way since this is not included in the permission to prepare food.</i>, nor with wood<sup class="footnote-marker">107</sup><i class="footnote">By turning a wooden stick rapidly in a hole in a wooden log.</i>, nor with water<sup class="footnote-marker">108</sup><i class="footnote">By filling a round glass vessel with water and using it as a magnifying glass concentrating the sun’s rays to start a fire.</i>, and one does not heat tiles to fry on them. In addition, Rebbi Eliezer says, a person stands by the <i>Muqṣeh</i><sup class="footnote-marker">109</sup><i class="footnote">The place on the roof where fig cakes are spread out to ferment and dry. This presumes that there are parts which already are edible again.</i> on a Sabbath eve in a sabbatical year<sup class="footnote-marker">110</sup><i class="footnote">When there are no tithes. The same holds on every Sabbath in a common year if one tithes the drying figs even before they become subject to obligatory tithe. Since food for the Sabbath has to be prepared in advance it cannot be consumed on the Sabbath as a snack for which the rules of tithe do not apply.</i> and says, from here I shall eat tomorrow, but the Sages say, only if he delineates and says, from here to here.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “One does not heat tiles to fry on them.” There are Tannaim who state, one may heat. [And there are Tannaim who state, one may not heat.]<sup class="footnote-marker">111</sup><i class="footnote">Addition by the corrector, not absolutely necessary.</i> Rav Ḥisda said, he who said one may heat, by those already checked out. And he who said one may not heat, by those not checked out<sup class="footnote-marker">112</sup><i class="footnote">The tiles are roof tiles. If they are new and not hardened in a kiln they will be hardened by the procedure which is a prohibited side effect. Since the effect is automatic, the procedure is forbidden even for R. Simeon who forbids procedures only if the Sabbath or holiday infraction is intended. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Beitzah 33b-34a" href="/Beitzah.33b-34a">Babli 33b/34a</a>.</i>.
<sup class="footnote-marker">113</sup><i class="footnote">This paragraph is a reformulation of a passage in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Beitzah 4:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Beitzah.4.1">Halakhah 1:3, Notes 128–138</a>.</i>“In addition, Rebbi Eliezer says, a person stands by the <i>Muqṣeh</i> on a Sabbath eve in a sabbatical year and says, from here I shall eat tomorrow.” The argument of Rebbi Eliezer seems inverted, as we have stated there<sup class="footnote-marker">114</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Beitzah 1:4:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Beitzah.1.4.1">Mishnah 1:3</a>.</i>, “the House of Shammai say, one may not take unless one had moved when it was still daylight,” and is Rebbi Eliezer not a Shammaite<sup class="footnote-marker">115</sup><i class="footnote">This is a very common argument but one may question whether a student of the Hillelite Rabban Joḥanan ben Zakkai can be called a Shammaite even if he was sympathetic to Shammaite rules.</i>? One is restrictive if living things are involved. “But the Sages say, only if he delineates and says, from here to here.” The argument of the Sages seems inverted, since we have stated there<sup class="footnote-marker">114</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Beitzah 1:4:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Beitzah.1.4.1">Mishnah 1:3</a>.</i> “the House of Hillel say, he may stand there and say, this and that I shall take,” and here they are saying so? Still they hold that one is restrictive if living things are involved.

Chapter 5



Halakhah 1

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> One lowers produce through a skylight<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">If there is danger of rain one may remove the produce stored on the flat roof through the skylight.</i> on the holiday. Also one covers produce by vessels because of leaks; the same holds for jugs of wine and jugs of oil. On the Sabbath one may put a vessel under a leak<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">To collect the rain water leaking through the roof.</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “One lowers produce through a skylight on the holiday,” etc. The Mishnah is about this roof, but from another roof it is forbidden if it is not equal<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">All roofs of contiguous houses of equal height are considered as one roof. But if the roof of some house is of a different height, moving the produce from one roof to the other would be seen as workday exertion and is rabbinically forbidden on the holiday. Contradicted in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Beitzah 36a" href="/Beitzah.36a">Babli 36a</a>.</i>. But if it was equal it is permitted. It was stated<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">Contradicted in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Beitzah (Lieberman) 4:3" href="/Tosefta_Beitzah_(Lieberman).4.3">Tosephta 4:3</a></i>: If there was a window, one lowers<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">משחילין is the correct original form of משילין.</i> through the window.
<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">The following text is copied from here in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Shabbat 4:2:3-6" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Shabbat.4.2.3-6"><i>Šabbat</i>4, Notes 38–46</a>.</i> Rav Jeremiah in the name of Rav: One spreads a mat over rows of bricks on the Sabbath<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">Even though the bricks are there as building materials one may turn them into seats if needed since they are not moved. In the Babli’s theory, <i>muqṣeh</i> items may not be moved, but they may be touched. It does not seem that this is the Yerushalmi’s attitude, as expressed by Rav in the question of the hunters. Nevertheless here the bricks are not touched; people sit on the mat. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Beitzah 36a" href="/Beitzah.36a">Babli 36a</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Shabbat 43a" href="/Shabbat.43a"><i>Šabbat</i>43a</a>. In the reading of the Babli this is a Tannaitic statement and the bricks are not there for a future building project but are leftovers from a finished one.</i>.
Rebbi Simeon ben Rebbi Yannai said: I did not hear from my father; my sister told me in his name. An egg which was laid on a holiday one props up against a vessel so it should not roll off. But one does not cover it with a vessel<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote">He holds that a vessel may be moved only for something that itself may be moved. Since the egg was not laid before the holiday it is not prepared and may not be moved.</i>. But Samuel says, one may cover it with a vessel. Rebbi Mana said, only if the vessel not touch the body of the egg<sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote">Samuel holds that a vessel may be moved for any legitimate purpose. Babli Šabbat 42b. R. Mana requires that the vessel be moved in a way that certainly avoids moving the egg.</i>.
Rebbi Hoshaia stated: One may spread a sheet over a swarm of bees<sup class="footnote-marker">10</sup><i class="footnote">The real reason naturally is to catch the bees when they are swarming on a Sabbath or holiday. If it can be done in a way which also protects the bees it may be done; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Shabbat 43a" href="/Shabbat.43a">Babli <i>Šabbat</i>43a</a>.</i> in the summer because of the sun, in the rainy season because of the rain. Is this following Rav? Following Samuel? Here on top, there below<sup class="footnote-marker">11</sup><i class="footnote">Since Rav permits to cover the bricks he also will permit to cover the bees. But since Samuel prohibits touching the egg he might forbid here since the cloth necessarily will touch bees. Samuel also will agree here since the swarm is hanging on the branch of a tree, not lying on the ground and supported by it as in the case of the egg.</i>.
Rebbi Bisna in the name of Rebbi Yose bar Ḥanina: It is forbidden to move the weaver’s beam weaving fine cloth<sup class="footnote-marker">12</sup><i class="footnote">The translation is tentative. In <i>Is.</i> Chapter 6 appears לסוטה as Aramaic translation of Hebrew רְדִיד, a fancy outer garment of women. The identification of פחי as “weaver’s beam” follows a suggestion of Kohut (<i>Arukh completum s.v</i>. 2 פחה), accepted by Liebermann, that this is Arabic חפה.</i>. The hunters were spreading traps and these were damaged by the sun. They came to ask Rav, may one move them? He told them, [it is forbidden to touch them.]<sup class="footnote-marker">13</sup><i class="footnote">Corrector’s addition.</i> Intend to put them under your heads and it is permitted for you to move them<sup class="footnote-marker">14</sup><i class="footnote">If the traps are actually used as a support for the head they may be moved even though at nightfall this intended use is only in the owner’s mind.</i>.
It was stated: If an amphora of <i>ṭevel</i><sup class="footnote-marker">15</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Ṭevel</i> is produce subject to heave and tithe from which heave had not been taken. Since eating untithed produce is sinful, on a holiday <i>ṭevel</i> is <i>muqseh</i>. Since the produce is stored in an amphora, one speaks of wine or oil.</i> became defective one brings another vessel and puts it under it. If it is filled, it is forbidden to move it<sup class="footnote-marker">16</sup><i class="footnote">Since now the vessel is filled with <i>muqseh</i> produce.</i>. Rebbi Ze`ira said, this implies that one makes an uncommon arrangement<sup class="footnote-marker">17</sup><i class="footnote">One proceeds in a way which is not what one would have done on a working day, where one would have brought another amphora and poured the entire contents into the new vessel.</i> for <i>muqseh</i> to save it. But did we not state, from the vat of grapes, from the load of olives [which drew fluid] one may not take on the holiday, and is it not necessary to say on the Sabbath<sup class="footnote-marker">18</sup><i class="footnote">A vat with newly harvested grapes or a vessel with olives whose cover was loaded down with weights in order to start extracting the oil from the olives. If the grapes were harvested to make wine, any fluid in the vat oozing out of grapes will be loaded into the wine press. In both cases, the fluids are not intended for consumption on the holiday and therefore not “prepared”. (On a Sabbath, fluid oozing out of a fruit is <i>muqseh</i> in all cases.)</i>? Rav Ḥisda said, even though both of them are <i>tevel</i>, this is <i>muqṣeh</i> and that is not <i>muqṣeh</i><sup class="footnote-marker">19</sup><i class="footnote">In the case of vat and olive vessel the fluid is <i>muqseh</i> without question. In the case of <i>ṭevel</i> the produce is forbidden and it is forbidden to tithe on Sabbath and holiday. But this is a purely rabbinic prohibition; if tithe is given against the rules the produce is unquestionably food prepared for the holiday and permitted for consumption.</i>. The following implies that one makes an uncommon arrangement for <i>muqseh</i> to save it. [It was stated.] If a light burned out, one saves its oil<sup class="footnote-marker">20</sup><i class="footnote">Even though while the light was burning the oil was not available for other uses since extinguishing a light is a biblical violation.</i>. Rebbi Jacob bar Aḥa said, it is Rebbi Simeon’s since Rebbi Simeon said, if it burned out it is permitted to move it<sup class="footnote-marker">21</sup><i class="footnote">Mishnah <i>Šabbat</i>3:6, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Shabbat (Lieberman) 3:13" href="/Tosefta_Shabbat_(Lieberman).3.13">Tosephta <i>Šabbat</i>3:13</a>. Since for R. Simeon there is no <i>muqseh</i>, the quote proves nothing.</i>. Rebbi Samuel ben Rav Isaac said, he mentioned only “to move it,” therefore one is permitted to save it<sup class="footnote-marker">22</sup><i class="footnote">Since Mishnah and Tosephta were formulated to show the difference between R. Simeon and the anonymous Tanna (presumed to be R. Meïr), the restriction to moving in the sources shows that R. Jacob bar Aḥa’s argument is not conclusive and the quote may be used to support R. Ze`ira,</i>.
It was stated<sup class="footnote-marker">23</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Beitzah 36b" href="/Beitzah.36b">Babli 36b</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Shabbat (Lieberman) 3:11" href="/Tosefta_Shabbat_(Lieberman).3.11">Tosephta <i>Šabbat</i>3:11</a>.</i>: “One puts a vessel under a leak on the Sabbath. If it was filled, one pours out and repeats without hesitation.” Rebbi Ḥanania said, they made this like rivers and sources coming from outside the Sabbath domain, as it was stated: from rivers and sources coming from outside the Sabbath domain one fills on the Sabbath and it is not necessary to say on a holiday<sup class="footnote-marker">24</sup><i class="footnote">Cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Beitzah 39a" href="/Beitzah.39a">Babli 39a</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Shabbat 121b" href="/Shabbat.121b"><i>Šabbat</i>121b</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Eruvin 46a" href="/Eruvin.46a"><i>Eruvin</i>46a</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Eruvin 48a" href="/Eruvin.48a">48a</a>. The language of the Babli sources states that these waters are “according to everybody’s feet”, see <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Beitzah 5:3:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Beitzah.5.3.1">Mishnah 3</a>.</i>.

Halakhah 2

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> One is liable on the holiday for everything classified as Sabbath rest, classified as obligatory, classified as meritorious<sup class="footnote-marker">25</sup><i class="footnote">All the prohibitions listed in the Mishnah are rabbinic; therefore the expression “one is liable” is difficult to understand since usually it is reserved for severe biblical infractions. The activities classified as Sabbath rest are rabbinic restrictions, mostly to avoid possible biblical infractions. Those classified as obligatory may also be meritorious, nevertheless they are rabbinically forbidden on the holiday (cf. S. Liebermann, <i>Tarbiz</i>5, p. 96–99). Those classified as meritorious are deeds to the Temple. Most of the items are discussed in detail in the Halakhah. The reasons for the prohibitions given in the Babli cannot be presumed for the Yerushalmi.</i>. These are classified as Sabbath rest: One does not climb on a tree, nor ride on an animal, nor sail on water, nor clap the hands in anger, nor clap the hands in joy, nor dance. These are classified as obligatory, one does not judge, nor contract preliminary marriage, nor give <i>ḥaliṣah,</i> nor enter levirate marriage. These are classified as meritorious, one does not dedicate<sup class="footnote-marker">26</sup><i class="footnote">Animals as sacrifices or property to be given to the Temple.</i>, nor dedicate one’s worth<sup class="footnote-marker">27</sup><i class="footnote">As described in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 27:2-8" href="/Leviticus.27.2-8"><i>Lev.</i> 27:2–8</a> and Tractate <i>Arakhin</i>.</i>, nor dedicate as <i>herem</i><sup class="footnote-marker">28</sup><i class="footnote">Gifts to the priests, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 18:14" href="/Numbers.18.14"><i>Num</i>. 18:14</a>.</i>, nor collect heaves and tithes. All these were said regarding a holiday, so much more regarding the Sabbath. The only difference between holiday and Sabbath is preparation of food<sup class="footnote-marker">29</sup><i class="footnote">Repeated in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Megillah 1:3:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Megillah.1.3.1">Mishnah <i>Megillah</i>1:5</a>.</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> Sabbath rest which is voluntary, and obligation which is meritorious. What is Sabbath rest which is voluntary? One does not climb on a tree, nor rides on an animal, nor sail on water, [nor clap his hands in anger, nor clap his hands in joy, nor dance.]<sup class="footnote-marker">30</sup><i class="footnote">Corrector’s addition.</i> What is obligation which is meritorious? One does not judge, nor contract preliminary marriage, nor give <i>ḥaliṣah,</i> nor enter levirate marriage<sup class="footnote-marker">31</sup><i class="footnote">The paragraph makes clear that in the Mishnah רשות means “acts which may be obligatory and in that case are meritorious”, as discussed in detail by Liebermann (l. c. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Beitzah 5:2:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Beitzah.5.2.1">Note 25</a>).</i>.
It was stated: One does not climb on a tree whether moist<sup class="footnote-marker">32</sup><i class="footnote">Fruit bearing.</i> or dried-up<sup class="footnote-marker">33</sup><i class="footnote">Including trees without fruits. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Eruvin 100b" href="/Eruvin.100b">Babli <i>Eruvin</i>100b</a>.</i>. One understands moist; dried-up? So we are saying, it is forbidden to step on a twig on the Sabbath<sup class="footnote-marker">34</sup><i class="footnote">Even dry twigs; this is a minority opinion.</i>? Or does it follow Rebbi Meïr, as Rebbi Meïr said<sup class="footnote-marker">35</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Kilayim 7:2:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Kilayim.7.2.1">Mishnah <i>Kilaim</i>7:2</a>.</i>, “a dried-up vine is forbidden but does not sanctify”<sup class="footnote-marker">36</sup><i class="footnote">It is forbidden to grow vegetables in a vineyard. This applies also to dry vines, but in contrast to active vines the vegetables do not become forbidden for usufruct.</i>? It is everybody’s opinion; they forbade the dried-up tree because of a moist tree. Rav Ḥiyya bar Ashi came running before Rav. He asked him, how did you come? He told him, [by] the warped date palm<sup class="footnote-marker">37</sup><i class="footnote">Implying that he stepped over the trunk, which for Rav is forbidden.</i>. He said to him, who permitted you?
Rebbi &lt;aḥa&gt;<sup class="footnote-marker">38</sup><i class="footnote">The name is missing here. The entire paragraph is copied from <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Eruvin 10:8:3" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Eruvin.10.8.3"><i>Eruvin</i>10 (Note 116</a>) and the name is supplied from there.</i> in the name of Rav: It is forbidden to trample on a twig on the Sabbath. There is no difference between the roots of a tree or the leaves of cabbage. If they are three {hand-breadths} high<sup class="footnote-marker">39</sup><i class="footnote">Any growth three hand-breadths high is a tree for the laws of Sabbath and holiday.</i>. But if they are not three {hand-breadths} high they are like the ground.
Why did they say that one does not climb on a tree? Is it lest he forget and eat, or maybe lest he forget and shake it? Let us hear from the following: If he was riding on an animal one tells him, descend. This implies, lest he forget and shake it<sup class="footnote-marker">40</sup><i class="footnote">It will be shown in the sequel that riding on an animal is a biblical infraction. Since riding on an animal is quoted in the same sentence as climbing on a tree, the prohibition of climbing must be to avoid a biblical infraction. Shaking a tree to have its fruit fall down is the regular way of harvesting olives; therefore for all trees shaking is harvesting and a biblical transgression.</i>.
Why did they say, if he was riding on an animal one tells him, descend? The colleagues say, maybe the animal will (be girded, be nursing) [be damaged]<sup class="footnote-marker">41</sup><i class="footnote">The text is not clear, nor is its meaning. The scribe’s text is תיטק but the letter ט is damaged and was read as נו by the Venice printer and נז by the corrector. There is no Hebrew root נטק (or יטק) on record; the Arabic root נטק means “to gird”. In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Beitzah 36b" href="/Beitzah.36b">Babli 36b</a> the reason is purely rabbinic, lest one cut a twig from a tree to drive the animal. The reader’s own conjecture as to the meaning will be as good as any other proposed interpretation.</i>. Rebbi Yose said to them, think of it, if it was a large camel! Rebbi Aḥa bar Pappus stated before Rebbi Ze`ira: There is a difference, because he is commanded about the rest of his animals like his own: <i>That your ox and your donkey rest</i><sup class="footnote-marker">42</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 23:12" href="/Exodus.23.12"><i>Ex.</i> 23:12</a>.</i><i>; like you</i><sup class="footnote-marker">43</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 5:14" href="/Deuteronomy.5.14"><i>Deut</i>. 5:14</a>.</i>. There are Tannaim who state, one may lean on an animal. There are Tannaim who state, one may not lean on an animal. Rav Ḥisda said, he who said one may lean on, if he is healthy; he who said one may not lean on, if he is weak<sup class="footnote-marker">44</sup><i class="footnote">A healthy person leans on an animal as he would lean on a human; this must be permitted. A sickly person might use the animal to support his entire weight; this is counted as a biblical infraction.</i>. Rebbi Yose says, Rebbi Abba bar Mamal and the colleagues disagreed. One said, one may lean on, but the other said, one may not lean on. He who said one may lean on, if he leans a little bit; he who said one may not lean on, if he puts on his full weight. We do not know who said what. Since Rebbi Yose did not say anything in the name of Rebbi Abba bar Mamal, this implies<sup class="footnote-marker">45</sup><i class="footnote">Since R. Yose reported the disagreement between Amoraim without further discussion he indicated that this disagreement was unimportant since the apparent disagreement between Tannaim already was explained away by Rav Ḥisda; the latter’s explanation was accepted also in the Galilean Academy. In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Shabbat 155a" href="/Shabbat.155a">Babli (<i>Šabbat</i>155a</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Chagigah 16b" href="/Chagigah.16b"><i>Ḥagigah</i>16b</a>) any leaning on animals is forbidden.</i> that a healthy person may lean on, a weak one may not lean on.
“Nor sail on water<sup class="footnote-marker">46</sup><i class="footnote">The person sailing does not do anything; the wind provides the power which pushes the water away on both sides of the boat.</i>” Rebbi Jacob bar Zavdi in the name of Rebbi Abbahu: This implies that it is forbidden to set something under sail to send it away from him. And similarly, it is forbidden to set something under sail to bring it to him. Rebbi Abba Mari and Rebbi Mattaniah were sitting<sup class="footnote-marker">47</sup><i class="footnote">On a weekday; therefore they did not have to actually intervene.</i>. They saw a person splitting the water<sup class="footnote-marker">46</sup><i class="footnote">The person sailing does not do anything; the wind provides the power which pushes the water away on both sides of the boat.</i> to both sides and drinking. Rebbi Abba Mari said to Rebbi Mattaniah, this is what Rebbi Jacob bar Zavdi in the name of Rebbi Abbahu, a person is forbidden to set something under sail to send it away from him<sup class="footnote-marker">48</sup><i class="footnote">Since the sailboat splits the water and the man observed did split the water.</i>. He answered, it is what we have stated, “a person may break open an amphora to eat dried figs from it<sup class="footnote-marker">49</sup><i class="footnote">If the amphora is tightly closed with clay that sticks to its walls, one may open it by damaging the vessel even though the intent is for a positive action. Therefore one certainly may split the water in order to drink.</i>”
“Nor clap the hands in anger.” סִיפּוּק is in anger, as you are saying, <i>Balaq got angry about Bileam and he clapped his hands in anger</i><sup class="footnote-marker">50</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 24:10" href="/Numbers.24.10"><i>Num</i>. 24:10</a>.</i>.
“Nor clap the hands in joy.” טִיפּוּחַ is by his desire. Rebbi Jonah and the colleagues, one said this way it is permitted, the other way it is forbidden<sup class="footnote-marker">51</sup><i class="footnote">As explained in the sequel, it means clapping his palms is forbidden but the backs of the hands is permitted.</i>, but the other said, both ways it is forbidden. We did not know who said what, but since Rebbi Jonah said, in our days there were old men who were clapping the backs of their hands on the Sabbath; this implies that he said, this way it is permitted, the other way it is forbidden. Rebbi was marrying off his son Rebbi Simeon where they were clapping the backs of their hands on the Sabbath. Rebbi Meïr passed by and heard their sound<sup class="footnote-marker">52</sup><i class="footnote">But he could not see how they clapped. (Since according to all other sources R. Meïr died during R. Simeon ben Gamliel’s lifetime, this might have been R. Meïr’s ghost.) <a class="refLink" data-ref="Eruvin 13b" href="/Eruvin.13b">Babli <i>Eruvin</i>13b</a>.</i>. He said, our teachers, is the Sabbath permitted<sup class="footnote-marker">53</sup><i class="footnote">Are Sabbath prohibitions lifted?</i>? Rebbi heard his voice; he said, who is that who comes to trouble us in our home? [There are those who are saying that he said so: who is the one who comes to hide us in our home?]<sup class="footnote-marker">54</sup><i class="footnote">Corrector’s addition, not found in the quotes of this passage in Rif, Ravan, Rosh (on <i>Beṣah</i> Chapter 5.)</i> Rebbi Meïr heard his voice and fled. They<sup class="footnote-marker">55</sup><i class="footnote">The guests of the Sabbath post-wedding meal.</i> went out and ran after him running. The wind blew his turban<sup class="footnote-marker">56</sup><i class="footnote">According to H. L. Fleischer, Greek φακίολον.</i> from Rebbi Meïr’s neck. Rebbi peeked from the window and saw the neck of Rebbi Meïr from behind. He said, I attained learning only because I saw Rebbi Meïr’s neck from behind. Rebbi Joḥanan and Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish both said, we attained learning only because we saw Rebbi’s finger from [the back of his sleeve.<sup class="footnote-marker">57</sup><i class="footnote">The scribe’s text is no longer legible. The word printed in the <i>editio princeps,</i> גולגוקין, should be deleted from the dictionaries.</i>]
“Nor dance.” Rebbi Jeremiah, Rebbi Ze`ira in the name of Rav Ḥuna: Jumping: one lifts both legs simultaneously. Dancing: one lifts one and keeps the other down. Rebbi Eleazar said, all sound making is forbidden on the Sabbath<sup class="footnote-marker">58</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Eruvin 104a" href="/Eruvin.104a">Babli <i>Eruvin</i>104a</a>.</i>. Rebbi Samuel bar Rav Isaac was of the opinion to knock for a new synagogue<sup class="footnote-marker">59</sup><i class="footnote">He disagrees with R. Eliezer and holds that some noise making by knocking on doors is permitted. The text is quoted differently by Raviah (§795) and Mordochai (§696): רִבִּי שְׁמוּאֵל בַּר רַב יִצְחָק סַבִּי מַקּוּשָׁה דִכְנִשְׁתָּא “Rebbi Samuel bar Rav Isaac: My grandfather was knocking for the synagogue,” finding in this passage the justification for the German usage of the “Schulklopfer” who in the morning knocked at the doors of Jewish houses to call people to the synagogue. Therefore practice has to follow R. Samuel bar Rav Isaac against R.</i>. Rebbi Jeremiah explained Scripture with Rebbi Immi’s son. He went and wanted to wake him up in the morning of the Sabbath. He started knocking on the door. His father told him, who did permit this to you? Rebbi Abba bar Cahana asked before Rebbi Yasa, may one knock on a belly on the Sabbath? He said to him, who permitted that on a weekday<sup class="footnote-marker">60</sup><i class="footnote">Since it could injure the person.</i>? Rebbi Samuel ben Eudaimon said, the rabbis are accustomed on a weekday from the navel upwards, through his clothing from the navel downwards<sup class="footnote-marker">61</sup><i class="footnote">As current practice in medical massage. Differently <a class="refLink" data-ref="Niddah 13b" href="/Niddah.13b">Babli <i>Niddah</i>13b</a>.</i>.
“One does not judge.” <sup class="footnote-marker">62</sup><i class="footnote">The same text is found in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Ketubot 1:1:8" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Ketubot.1.1.8"><i>Ketubot</i>1:1 (Notes 43–46</a>) and <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Sanhedrin 4:6:4" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Sanhedrin.4.6.4"><i>Sanhedrin</i>4:7 (Notes 53–53a</a>).</i> Rebbi Ḥizqiah, Rebbi Aḥa, said in the name of Rebbi Abbahu: It is forbidden to judge money matters on Friday. Does not a Mishnah object: “Therefore one does not judge &lt;criminal matters&gt; on Sabbath eve or holiday eve”<sup class="footnote-marker">63</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Sanhedrin 4:6:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Sanhedrin.4.6.1">Mishnah <i>Sanhedrin</i>4:7</a>.</i>? Hence, one judges money matters! Also, Rebbi Ḥiyya stated thus: One judges money matters on Friday but not criminal matters. One is for practice, the other for words of the Torah<sup class="footnote-marker">64</sup><i class="footnote">In theory it would be possible to judge on a Friday but in practice it is not done. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Kamma 113a" href="/Bava_Kamma.113a">Babli <i>Bava Qamma</i>113a</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Sanhedrin 32a" href="/Sanhedrin.32a"><i>Sanhedrin</i>32a</a>.</i>.
<sup class="footnote-marker">65</sup><i class="footnote">The parallels are in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Ketubot 1:1:7" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Ketubot.1.1.7"><i>Ketubot</i>1:1 (Notes 34–42</a>,<b>כ</b>), <i>Ta`aniot</i>4:9 (69b l. 40,<b>ת</b>).</i> Rebbi Abba bar Cohen said before Rebbi Yose: Rebbi Aḥa in the name of Rebbi Jacob bar Idi: A man may not preliminarily marry on a Friday. That means, to make an engagement feast<sup class="footnote-marker">66</sup><i class="footnote">Since this would impinge on the Sabbath meal. It is in order to make the preliminary marriage on Friday and arrange the festive meal as Sabbath meal.</i>. This implies that the preliminary marriage itself is permitted. Samuel says, even on the Ninth of Ab<sup class="footnote-marker">67</sup><i class="footnote">The anniversary of the destruction of both Temples.</i> a preliminary marriage is permitted, lest another forestall him. The argument of Samuel seems inverted. There<sup class="footnote-marker">68</sup><i class="footnote">A similar argument is in the Babli, <i>Mo`ed qaṭan</i>18b.</i>, he says, <i>God puts singles in a house</i>; <i>To rise on scales; they all are of vapor!</i><sup class="footnote-marker">69</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Psalms 68:6" href="/Psalms.68.6"><i>Ps.</i> 68:6</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Psalms 62:10" href="/Psalms.62.10">62:10</a>. This means that marriages are pre-ordained in Heaven. (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Vayikra Rabbah 29:5" href="/Vayikra_Rabbah.29.5"><i>Lev. rabba</i>29(5</a>).)</i> And here, he says so<sup class="footnote-marker">70</sup><i class="footnote">If marriages are pre-ordained, why should anybody be afraid that another man could snatch the bride preselected for him? Another man might by his prayer cause the Heavenly decree to be changed, but this would lead to the early death of one of the partners of the marriage. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Sotah 2a" href="/Sotah.2a">Babli <i>Soṭa</i>2a</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Sanhedrin 22a" href="/Sanhedrin.22a"><i>Sanhedrin</i>22a</a>.</i>? That means, that he should not forestall him in prayer. Even so, it would not be permanent.
<sup class="footnote-marker">71</sup><i class="footnote">This paragraph also is in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Pesachim 8:3:2-5" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Pesachim.8.3.2-5"><i>Pesaḥim</i>8:3</a>.</i> Rebbi Jacob bar Aḥa in the name of Rebbi Immi: Rebbi Joḥanan and Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish disagreed. One said, one dedicates; the other said, one does not dedicate. The rabbis of Caesarea make it explicit: Rebbi Joḥanan said, one dedicates without problems on a holiday; Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish said, one does not dedicate without problems on a holiday<sup class="footnote-marker">72</sup><i class="footnote">There is no doubt that biblically one is permitted to dedicate animals as sacrifices on a holiday; the question is whether there is a rabbinic prohibition.</i>. There are Tannaim who state, he goes to sellers of lambs; there are Tannaim who state, he goes to sellers of <i>Pesaḥim</i><sup class="footnote-marker">73</sup><i class="footnote">If the 14th of Nisan is a Sabbath. In the first formulation, the lambs are profane and have to be dedicated on the Sabbath to be permitted into the Temple court; in the second version they already are dedicated (cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Shabbat 148b" href="/Shabbat.148b">Babli <i>Šabbat</i>148b</a>).</i>. He who says, he goes to sellers of lambs, follows him who says, one dedicates. He who says, he goes to sellers of <i>Pesaḥim</i>, follows him who says, one does not dedicate. This implies that it does not become profane and then dedicated again. If you would say that it becomes profane and then dedicated again, it should be forbidden [because of dedicating], and we have stated, “one does [not] dedicate.<sup class="footnote-marker">74</sup><i class="footnote">It seems that one has to read with the corrector “that one does not dedicate”. Since our Mishnah explicitly declares that the prohibition of dedication of sacrifices is rabbinical, it implies that inside the Temple precinct proper the prohibition does not apply. In this situation there is no disagreement between R. Joḥanan and R. Simeon ben Laqish and no inference may be drawn about the status of monies dedicated for <i>Pesaḥ</i>.</i>” Rebbi Ḥananiah and Rebbi Mana, one said, if he dedicates for the next day; but the other said, if he dedicated for the upkeep of the Temple<sup class="footnote-marker">75</sup><i class="footnote">Dedication of a sacrifice to be eaten on the holiday has to be considered part of the preparation of food and is permitted; what the Mishnah forbids are dedications either not for use on the holiday or not for food at all.</i>. Rebbi Shammai said, even for him who said that one dedicates, in the Temple Court because rabbinic Sabbath prohibitions were permitted in the Temple<sup class="footnote-marker">76</sup><i class="footnote">As explained in the last sentence of Mishnah <i>Eruvin</i>.</i>.
<sup class="footnote-marker">77</sup><i class="footnote">The paragraph also appears in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Megillah 1:6:2-5" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Megillah.1.6.2-5"><i>Megillah</i>1:8</a> (71a l. 36–45, <b>מ</b>).</i>“All these were said on a holiday, so much more on the Sabbath..” Are these it? Are there no others<sup class="footnote-marker">78</sup><i class="footnote">One proves that the list is incomplete.</i>? There is stoning on the Sabbath, there is no stoning on a holiday. There is extirpation on the Sabbath, there is no extirpation on a holiday. There is flogging on a holiday, there is no flogging on the Sabbath<sup class="footnote-marker">79</sup><i class="footnote">Desecrating the Sabbath is a capital crime, intentional desecration being punishable by stoning if there are witnesses, by divine extirpation otherwise. Desecration of a holiday is a simple transgression, intentional desecration before witnesses is punishable by flogging.</i>. If you would say, we are coming to state cases connected with food, did we not state: “one lowers produce through a skylight<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">If there is danger of rain one may remove the produce stored on the flat roof through the skylight.</i> on the holiday but not on the Sabbath”? In addition from the following, one grinds aromatic wood for a circumcision on a holiday but not on the Sabbath<sup class="footnote-marker">80</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Shabbat 134a" href="/Shabbat.134a">Babli <i>Šabbat</i>134a</a>.</i>. Rebbi Yose said, only if he circumcised<sup class="footnote-marker">81</sup><i class="footnote">Since grinding aromatic wood is not preparation of food it may be done if the necessity is clearly established, not in advance of an expected event.</i>. In addition from what was stated<sup class="footnote-marker">82</sup><i class="footnote">The paragraph appears in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Megillah 1:6:2-5" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Megillah.1.6.2-5"><i>Megillah</i>1:8</a> as an addition by the corrector.</i>, “The Sages agree with Rebbi Meïr about seals in the ground that one pushes aside, and removes, and unties, and cuts. On the Sabbath one pushes aside but one does not remove nor untie nor cut. For implements on the Sabbath it is permitted; it is not necessary to mention on a holiday.”
“The only difference between holiday and Sabbath is preparation of food.” <sup class="footnote-marker">82</sup><i class="footnote">The paragraph appears in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Megillah 1:6:2-5" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Megillah.1.6.2-5"><i>Megillah</i>1:8</a> as an addition by the corrector.</i> What is between them<sup class="footnote-marker">83</sup><i class="footnote">This refers to a text like <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Megillah (Lieberman) 1:7" href="/Tosefta_Megillah_(Lieberman).1.7">Tosephta <i>Megillah</i>1:7</a>: “The only difference between holiday and Sabbath is preparation of food; Rebbi Jehudah says, also actions preparatory to the preparation of food.” The question is, what are the actions which the rabbis forbid but R. Jehudah permits.</i>? Rav Ḥisda said, to sharpen the tip of the spit is between them<sup class="footnote-marker">84</sup><i class="footnote">The rabbis hold that preparing utensils for use on the holiday is not included in the permission to prepare food.
